The Mu'tazila and Shi'a First Proof Against Eternal Attributes
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazila-Shi'a first proof that affirming eternal attributes is like Christian disbelief, and critiques its presentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila-Shi'a argument that affirming eternal attributes is akin to Christian disbelief, the response distinguishing essence from attribute, and the methodological critique of how the argument is presented.

Core Idea
Affirming eternal attributes does not entail multiple eternal essences.

This passage presents the first of three proofs used by the Mu'tazila and Shi'a against the view that Allah has eternal attributes. They claim that affirming such attributes is the same disbelief as the Christians who affirmed multiple eternal hypostases. The author offers a brief response, and then the commentator critiques the presentation for not clarifying the Mu'tazila's position beforehand. Let's examine the argument and its methodological concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila and Shi'a argue that affirming eternal attributes is like Christian disbelief, and the brief answer that follows.
2
Identify the analogy with Christian disbelief
The proof claims that affirming multiple eternal entities (the attributes) is the same as the Christian belief in multiple eternal hypostases. Understand why this analogy might seem compelling.
The response clarifies that Christian disbelief is in multiple eternal essences, not attributes of a single essence.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator finds an awkwardness: the author did not properly explain the Mu'tazila's position before refuting it. This raises a methodological point about the order of argumentation.
Look for the phrase 'فيه حزازة' which signals the critique.
4
See the reconciliation offered
The commentator suggests that the Mu'tazila are grouped with the philosophers in denying attributes, so their general argument is mentioned here, with details reserved for later. This resolves the awkwardness.
This shows the importance of proper framing in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واحتجت المعتزلة والشيعة بوجوه ثلاثة الأول ما مر من أن إثبات القدماء كفروا به كفرت النصارى والجواب ما مر أيضاً من أن الكفر إثبات ذوات قديمة لا إثبات ذات واحدة ) ولهذا قال الشارح: (فيه حزازة لأنه ما بين مدعى المعتزلة في تحرير المبحث حالاً بل قال لهم تفصيل يأتي في كل مسألة فلا معنى لذكر أدلتهم قبل تحرير مدعاهم اللهم إلا أن يقال قوله فيما سبق والمعتزلة معطوف على الفلاسفة في قوله وذهبت الفلاسفة ويكون قوله لهم منه فيفهم حينئذ مشاركة المعتزلة للفلاسفة في نفي الصفات الموجودة الزائدة، وإن كان لهم تفصيل يأتي في كل مسألة هذا وإنما قدر الشارح والشيعة موافقاً لما في الأبكار إشارة إلى أن المصنف إنما تركه لاشتراكهما في الوجوده الثلاثة فكان ذكر أحدهما ذكر الآخر.
His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila and the Shi'a have argued with three points. The first: what has passed, that affirming eternal entities [attributes] is the disbelief with which the Christians disbelieved. And the answer: what has also passed, that disbelief is affirming multiple eternal essences, not a single essence.' And therefore the commentator said: 'There is an awkwardness in this because he has not clarified the claim of the Mu'tazila in this investigation properly, rather he said that they have a detailed position to come in each issue. So there is no point in mentioning their proofs before clarifying their claim. However, it could be said that his statement in what preceded, "and the Mu'tazila" is coordinated with "the philosophers" in his saying "and the philosophers held..." and his statement "they have" is from it, so then it is understood that the Mu'tazila share with the philosophers in denying additional existing attributes, even though they have detail in each issue. And thus he assumed the commentator and the Shi'a, in agreement with what is in al-Abkār, indicating that the author only omitted it because they share the three points, so mentioning one of them suffices for the other.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage deals with the first proof of the Mu'tazila and Shi'a against additional attributes. The proof is that affirming multiple eternal entities (the attributes) is the same disbelief as the Christians who affirmed multiple eternal hypostases. The answer is that disbelief involves affirming multiple eternal essences, not a single essence with attributes. The commentator then critiques the presentation of this argument, saying the author did not properly set up the position of the Mu'tazila before refuting it. He then offers a reconciliation: the Mu'tazila are grouped with the philosophers in denying attributes, so their general argument is mentioned here, reserving details for later. This illustrates the methodological concerns in theological debate.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشيعة
إثبات القدماء
كفر النصارى
ذوات قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument equates attributes with separate essences, so affirming attributes means believing in multiple gods.
Correction: The response clarifies that believing in multiple attributes of a single essence is not the same as believing in multiple essences. The Christian error was in affirming multiple hypostases as separate entities, not multiple attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of the presentation of the Mu'tazila-Shi'a argument?
A) The argument is invalid.
B) The author did not properly explain the Mu'tazila position.
C) The answer is insufficient.
D) The argument is circular.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila-Shi'a proof conflates attributes with separate essences, but the Sunni response and the commentator's critique both highlight the need for precise definitions and proper framing in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a criticism of your faith that uses an analogy (like comparing your beliefs to those of another religion), do you pause to examine the assumptions behind the analogy? This lesson shows that a dishonest or mistaken analogy can lead to false conclusions. Recall a time when you accepted or rejected an argument based on an analogy without checking its accuracy. How will you change your approach to such arguments from now on?
?
The commentator criticizes the author for not clarifying the opponent's position before refuting it. In your own life, do you sometimes dismiss or argue against a view without first ensuring you understand it correctly? Before your next debate or disagreement, will you commit to being able to state the other person's position in a way they would agree with? If not, what does that say about your pursuit of truth?
?
The response to the Mu'tazila hinges on a distinction between 'essences' and 'attributes'. Is there an area of your own understanding of God where you might be conflating two things that should remain distinct? Spend a moment reflecting on your concept of God's mercy and anger—are you tempted to see them as separate 'parts' of God? What would it mean to affirm that they are both attributes of one undivided essence, and how would that change your worship?

Refuting the Philosophers' 'One from One' Principle
Refutes the philosophers' claim that from the One only one can proceed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim made by the divine philosophers that from the One only one can proceed, and the theological response that this principle is denied.

Core Idea
The 'one from one' rule is rejected in Islamic theology.

This lesson introduces the first group of opponents to the doctrine of God's absolute power: the divine philosophers. They argued that God, being absolutely one, can directly create only one thing—the First Intellect—and that everything else comes into being through intermediaries. The passage presents this view and then dismisses its foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the philosophers' claim and the author's response.
The refutation is brief; it asserts the principle is denied.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
Find the core claim: from a true one, only one effect can directly issue. This leads to the First Intellect as the first creation.
3
Understand the response
The author simply denies the principle and states that their proofs have already been refuted. Why is this a sufficient response?
The refutation is not detailed here; it is referenced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعني عموم قدرته تعالى الممكنات كلها وهو أعظم الأصول فرق متعددة كما سيتلى عليك. الأولى: الفلاسفة الإلهيون فإنهم قالوا: إنه تعالى واحد حقيقي فلا يصدر عنه أثران والصادر عنه ابتداء هو العقل الأول والباقي صادرة عنه بالوسائط كما شرحناه من قبل. والجواب منعه قولهم: الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد. وما تمسكوا به في إثباته فقد زيفناه.
I mean the generality of His power, exalted is He, over all possible things, and it is the greatest of principles; there are multiple groups as will be recited to you. The first: the divine philosophers, for they said: He, exalted is He, is a true one, so two effects cannot issue from Him; what issues from Him initially is the first intellect, and the rest issue from Him through intermediaries, as we explained before. The answer is to deny their statement: 'From the one only one issues.' And what they clung to in proving it, we have already refuted.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage identifies the first group of opponents to the generality of God's power: the divine philosophers (al-falāsifa al-ilāhiyyūn). Their view is that God, being absolutely one, cannot directly produce more than one effect. The first effect is the First Intellect, and everything else comes into existence through intermediate causes. The response is a plain denial of the principle 'from the one only one issues', and a claim that their arguments for it have already been refuted elsewhere. The refutation is not detailed here; it is referenced as already accomplished.

Key Terms

واحد حقيقي

وسائط

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the divine philosophers regarding God's power?
A) God can create all things directly without any intermediaries.
B) God is one and cannot directly produce more than one effect; the first effect is the First Intellect.
C) God only creates the first intellect by coercion.
D) God's power is limited to the celestial spheres.

Key Takeaway
The divine philosophers' claim that only one can proceed directly from God is denied by the sanad of Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously limit God's power by assuming He cannot intervene directly in your daily struggles? What would change if you truly believed He can act without any intermediary?
?
The philosophers felt they had to protect God's oneness by limiting His direct action. Are you ever guilty of 'protecting' God's majesty by lowering your expectations of what He might do for you?
?
Reflect: Is there a difficult situation in your life where you have given up hope because you think God works only through 'normal' means (means that have failed)? Would you be willing to ask Him directly for an impossible solution?

The Dahriyya Denial of Self-Knowledge
Sanusi presents the Dahriyya argument and the initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dahriyya's argument that God cannot know Himself because knowledge requires two distinct things, and the initial refutation that knowledge is a real attribute with possible relations to the same essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a pure relation but a real attribute that can relate to the same essence.

The Dahriyya sect argued that if knowledge is a relation, then God cannot know Himself since a relation requires two distinct relata. This lesson presents their argument and the first response: knowledge is not a pure relation but an attribute that can relate to the known and the knower. We will follow the logical exchange step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: claim, answer, objection, reply.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Dahriyya premise
Find the sentence that states why the Dahriyya deny self-knowledge. Notice the key term 'relation' (nisa) and the requirement of two distinct things.
3
Analyze the first answer
The author denies that knowledge is a pure relation. Instead, it is a real attribute with a relation to the known. Consider why this answers the Dahriyya.
The relation of an attribute to its subject is possible even if subject and object are the same.
4
Follow the objection and reply
An objector says the attribute itself necessitates a relation between knower and known. The reply distinguishes two relations: one to the known and one to the knower. Both are possible.
This sets up the deeper discussion in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمخالف في هذا الأصل أيضاً فرق ست : الأولى من قال من الدهرية : إنه لا يعلم نفسه لأن العلم نسبة والنسبة لا تكون إلا بين شيئين متغايرين هما طرفاها بالضرورة ونسبة الشيء إلى نفسه محال إذ لا تغاير هناك . والجواب منع كون العلم نسبة محضة بل هو صفة حقيقة ذات نسبة إلى المعلوم ونسبة الصفة إلى الذات ممكنة . فإن قيل : تلك الصفة تقتضي نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم فلا يجوز أن يكونا متحدين. قلنا: هي تقتضي نسبة بينها وبين المعلوم ونسبة أخرى بينها وبين العالم وهما ممكنتان كما.
قوله : من قال من الدهرية المفهوم من سياق كلامه أن الدهرية يثبتون الواجب تعالى وأما ما سيذكره في النبوات من أن الدهرية على اختلاف أصنافهم ينفون القادر المختار فكأنه محمول على نفي القدرة والاختيار وإن كان بعيدا من المشهور .
قوله : لأن العلم نسبة إلخ قيل : هذا إنما يدل على عدم العلم بالعلم لا بالذات كما تراه الفلاسفة ومتأخرو المعتزلة والجواب : إن المراد أن العلم للمعنى المصدري أعني الكشف والتميز نسبة لا يلائمه تقرير الجواب فتأمل.
قوله : والجواب منع كون العلم إلخ هذا الجواب لا يناسب ما ذكره في آخر المقصد الأول من مقاصد العلم في موقف الأعراض وقد ذكرت هناك فلينظر فيه .
قوله : قلنا : هي تقتضي إلخ قيل : العلم كما هو المختار صفة توجب تمييزاً وانكشافاً ولا شك في أن التميز والانكشاف نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم لا بين العلم وأحدهما، فيعود الإشكال ويحتاج إلى الجواب التسليمي الذي ذكره، وبالجملة ظهور المعلوم للعالم نسبة بينهما بالذات والعلم مرآه وآلة لتلك النسبة ونسبة الشيء إلى صاحبه أولى وأقدم من نسبته إلى آلته .
قوله : ونسبة أخرى بينهما وبين العالم كون هذه النسبة نسبة أخرى لا يتوقف على تحقق التغاير بين العالم والمعلوم ولو بالاعتبار حتى يرد أن الكلام هاهنا مسوق على نفي اقتضاء ذلك التغاير لجواز أن يكون لشيء واحد بالنسبة إلى شيء آخر بعينهما نسبتان متغايرتان . فإن علم زيد بنفسه له نسبة إلى زيد بالقيام ونسبة أخرى إليه بالتعلق نعم بعد تحقق النسبتين حصل له.
And the opponent on this principle also are six sects: the first is what was said by the Dahriyya: that He does not know Himself because knowledge is a relation, and a relation is only between two distinct things which are its two sides necessarily, and the relation of a thing to itself is impossible, since there is no distinction there. The answer is the denial that knowledge is a pure relation; rather it is a real attribute having a relation to the known, and the relation of the attribute to the essence is possible. And if it is said: that attribute necessitates a relation between the knower and the known, so they cannot be one. We say: it necessitates a relation between it and the known and another relation between it and the knower, and both are possible, as... His statement: 'what was said by the Dahriyya' – it is understood from the context of his speech that the Dahriyya affirm the Necessary Exalted, and as for what he will mention in prophethoods that the Dahriyya in all their varieties deny the powerful chooser, it is as if it is interpreted as a denial of power and choice, although it is far from the well-known. His statement: 'because knowledge is a relation etc.' – it is said: this only indicates the non-existence of knowledge of knowledge, not of the essence, as the philosophers and later Mu'tazila see. The answer: the intention is that knowledge in the verbal noun sense, i.e., disclosure and distinction, is a relation; the formulation of the answer does not align, so reflect. His statement: 'the answer is the denial that knowledge is etc.' – this answer does not suit what he mentioned at the end of the first objective of the objectives of knowledge in the section on accidents, and I have mentioned there, so let it be examined. His statement: 'we say it necessitates etc.' – it is said: knowledge, as chosen, is an attribute that necessitates distinction and disclosure, and there is no doubt that distinction and disclosure are a relation between the knower and the known, not between knowledge and one of them; so the problem returns and needs the concessive answer that he mentioned. In sum, the manifestation of the known to the knower is a relation between them essentially, and knowledge is its mirror and instrument for that relation, and the relation of a thing to its owner is more fitting and prior than its relation to its instrument. His statement: 'and another relation between it and the world' – that this relation is another relation not dependent on the realization of distinction between the knower and the known, even by consideration, so that it might be objected that the speech here is launched to deny that distinction entails permissibility of one thing having two different relations with respect to the same other thing. For Zayd's knowledge of himself has a relation to Zayd by way of existence, and another relation by way of connection; indeed after the realization of the two relations, he has obtained.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a heretical view (the Dahriyya). It begins by listing six sects that oppose the principle of divine knowledge; the first is the Dahriyya who say God does not know Himself because knowledge is a relation requiring two distinct relata, and a relation of a thing to itself is impossible. The answer denies that knowledge is a pure relation; it is a real attribute that has a relation to the known, and a relation of an attribute to its subject is possible. Then an objection is raised: the attribute itself necessitates a relation between knower and known, so they cannot be identical. The reply distinguishes between the relation of the attribute to the known and the relation of the attribute to the knower; both relations are possible. The commentary then addresses several points: (1) The Dahriyya here seem to affirm the necessary being, contrary to the common view that they deny the Powerful Chooser—this may be a different sub-group. (2) A possible misinterpretation that the argument only addresses knowledge of knowledge, not self-knowledge, is noted but dismissed. (3) The given answer may conflict with earlier discussions in the text, so the commentator advises cross-referencing. (4) A deeper problem: if knowledge is an attribute that produces distinction/disclosure, that distinction is a relation between knower and known, not between knowledge and either of them, so the original objection recurs. The resolution is that the manifestation of the known to the knower is a direct relation between them, and knowledge is merely the instrument. The relation of a thing to its essence is more primordial than to an instrument. (5) The commentator clarifies that the two relations (to the known and to the knower) do not require real distinction between knower and known; even if we consider them identical, the two relations can obtain, as in the example of Zayd's knowledge of himself: there is a relation to Zayd as existing and a relation of connection.

Key Terms
الدهرية
النسبة
صفة حقيقية
العالم
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Dahriyya's main argument against God knowing Himself?
A) Knowledge requires sensation, and God lacks senses.
B) Knowledge is a relation requiring two distinct things, and self-relation is impossible.
C) God only knows universals, not Himself.
D) Knowledge implies change, and God cannot change.

Key Takeaway
The initial refutation of the Dahriyya denial establishes that knowledge is not a pure relation but an attribute capable of relating to the same essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own soul, do you avoid looking deeply because you fear you won't find a coherent 'self'? The Dahriyya thought self-knowledge is impossible because it requires two distinct things. What would it take for you to truly know yourself as you are before Allah, even if that knowledge feels like a relation to an unknown?
?
Do you reduce your relationship with Allah to mere rituals and outward acts—as if it were just a relation—rather than a real attribute of faith that defines who you are? How can you make your faith a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) that transforms you from within?
?
When you hold yourself accountable (muhasaba), are you aware that you are both the knower and the known? This lesson distinguishes two relations in self-knowledge. How can this awareness change your self-reflection to be more honest and less self-deceived?

Resolving the Deeper Objection: Knowledge as Instrument
Sanusi presents a deeper objection and its resolution using the concept of instrumental knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper objection that even as an attribute, knowledge produces a relation between knower and known, and the resolution that the relation of manifestation is direct while knowledge is merely instrumental.

Core Idea
The manifestation of the known to the knower is a direct relation; knowledge is only its instrument.

After the initial refutation, a more subtle problem emerges: if knowledge is an attribute that produces distinction, that distinction is a relation between the knower and the known. The resolution distinguishes between the direct relation of manifestation and the instrumental role of knowledge. This lesson explores that refinement and the example of self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the problem and the solution presented.
This passage contains a quotation and then commentary.
2
Identify the deeper objection
The objection states that even if knowledge is an attribute, it produces distinction and disclosure, which is a relation between the knower and the known. This brings back the original problem.
The problem is that the relation is between knower and known, not between knowledge and one of them.
3
Understand the resolution
The answer is that manifestation (zuhur) of the known to the knower is a direct relation between them essentially. Knowledge is merely a mirror and instrument for that relation.
The relation of a thing to itself is more fundamental than its relation to an instrument.
4
Examine the example of Zayd
The passage gives the example of Zayd's knowledge of himself, showing how two relations can exist without real distinction: one to Zayd as the subject and one to Zayd as the object.
This illustrates the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمخالف في هذا الأصل أيضاً فرق ست : الأولى من قال من الدهرية : إنه لا يعلم نفسه لأن العلم نسبة والنسبة لا تكون إلا بين شيئين متغايرين هما طرفاها بالضرورة ونسبة الشيء إلى نفسه محال إذ لا تغاير هناك . والجواب منع كون العلم نسبة محضة بل هو صفة حقيقة ذات نسبة إلى المعلوم ونسبة الصفة إلى الذات ممكنة . فإن قيل : تلك الصفة تقتضي نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم فلا يجوز أن يكونا متحدين. قلنا: هي تقتضي نسبة بينها وبين المعلوم ونسبة أخرى بينها وبين العالم وهما ممكنتان كما.
قوله : من قال من الدهرية المفهوم من سياق كلامه أن الدهرية يثبتون الواجب تعالى وأما ما سيذكره في النبوات من أن الدهرية على اختلاف أصنافهم ينفون القادر المختار فكأنه محمول على نفي القدرة والاختيار وإن كان بعيدا من المشهور .
قوله : لأن العلم نسبة إلخ قيل : هذا إنما يدل على عدم العلم بالعلم لا بالذات كما تراه الفلاسفة ومتأخرو المعتزلة والجواب : إن المراد أن العلم للمعنى المصدري أعني الكشف والتميز نسبة لا يلائمه تقرير الجواب فتأمل.

The resolution is that the manifestation of the known to the knower is a direct relation between them, and knowledge is merely the instrument. The relation of a thing to its essence is more primordial than to an instrument. (5) The commentator clarifies that the two relations (to the known and to the knower) do not require real distinction between knower and known; even if we consider them identical, the two relations can obtain, as in the example of Zayd's knowledge of himself: there is a relation to Zayd as existing and a relation of connection.

Key Terms
تميز
انكشاف
ظهور
مرآة
آلة
قيام
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if knowledge is an attribute that produces distinction, then the knower and known must be distinct.
Correction: The distinction produced by knowledge is a relation of manifestation (zuhur) that is direct and does not require real separation; knowledge is merely the instrument.

Checkpoint
In the example of Zayd's knowledge of himself, what are the two relations mentioned?
A) Relation of existence and relation of sensation
B) Relation of existence (qiyam) and relation of connection (ta'alluq)
C) Relation of cause and relation of effect
D) Relation of similarity and relation of difference

Key Takeaway
The deeper objection is resolved by understanding that manifestation is a direct relation between knower and known, and knowledge is merely its instrument, allowing self-knowledge without real distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own self-awareness, do you experience a separation between the 'knower' and the 'known'? Or are you directly present to yourself without any gap? How does this intimate reality challenge the way you assume distance in your relationship with Allah? Will you trust that your knowledge of Him can be immediate, not mediated by your own shortcomings?
?
If knowledge is merely an instrument, a mirror for the truth, then what are you truly seeking in your studies? Are you in love with the mirror itself or with what it reflects? Examine your heart: do you pursue knowledge for its own sake, or to bring you face-to-face with the Real? Make a concrete decision today to redirect one of your study habits towards contemplation instead of accumulation.
?
The passage teaches that a thing's relation to itself is more fundamental than its relation to any instrument. You know yourself more directly than any tool or concept can provide. Are you ignoring this direct self-presence, hiding behind roles, degrees, or opinions? What would it mean for you to stop using your knowledge as a shield and instead let it be a transparent window to your own soul and to your Lord? Commit to a silent moment today where you simply are, without any instrument of knowledge.

Incomplete Summary of Schools on Divine Will
A critical note on the incompleteness of a theological summary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Imam's summary of theological schools on divine will is not exhaustive, and why acknowledging this incompleteness matters for accurate scholarship.

Core Idea
Even great summaries can omit important positions — critical reading is essential.

In this brief concluding note, the author points out a deficiency in an earlier summary of positions on God's will. The Imam's recording omitted the views of notable theologians al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn. This passage reminds us that scholarly summaries should be read critically, as they may not capture every relevant school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the tone: it is a critical remark.
The passage is short but significant — it marks a methodological point.
2
Identify the specific omission
Find the names mentioned: al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn. These are Mu'tazili theologians whose positions were left out of the Imam's summary.
This tells you that the earlier summary was not comprehensive.
3
Reflect on why the author points this out
Ask yourself why the author includes this note. It serves as a warning to readers: be aware that the summary is incomplete and seek out the missing views elsewhere.
This is a common scholarly practice — flagging limitations.
4
Consider the impact on your understanding
Think about how this critique affects your view of the Imam's work. Does it undermine his authority? No, but it shows that scholarship is cumulative and critical.
Always read summaries with awareness of potential omissions.

The text ends abruptly due to the page break, but the point is clear: the earlier classification omitted some known schools, so it is incomplete.

The Ash'ari-Maturidi Dispute Over Takwin
Objection and answer clarifying the Ash'ari-Maturidi dispute on takwin's identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on whether takwin is identical to the made thing or an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Takwin's relation to the created thing divides Ash'aris and Maturidis.

This lesson addresses an objection that the earlier argument (sufficiency of qudra) contradicts the statement that takwin is identical to the made thing. The answer clarifies that this objection stems from conflating two different theological schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: objection followed by answer.
The passage contains a repeated phrase, likely a scribal error.
2
Identify the two schools
Look for the names Ash'aris and Maturidis. Understand each school's stance on takwin.
Ash'aris deny it as an attribute; Maturidis affirm it.
3
Understand the core point
The objection only applies if one assumes the Maturidi view. The answer clarifies that the statement 'takwin is identical to the made thing' is the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل من أنه لا يلائم قولهم : التكوين عين المكون، إذ لا معنى لكون كلمة كن عين المكون فجوابه أن ذلك زعم الأشاعرة النافين لكونه صفة زائدة، والقائل بكون التكوين هو كلمة كن عين المكون فجوابه أن ذلك زعم الأشاعرة النافين لكونه صفة زائدة والقائل بكون التكوين هو كلمة كن الماتريدية المثبتون له. قوله : ( وأنه غير القدرة وغير الإرادة لأن الإيجاد مسبوق بالإرادة . قوله : ( للممكن ذاتي قيل : عليه يجوز أن يراد بالصحة الإمكان الاستعدادي وهو قرب وقوع المفعول فيجوز تعليله. أجيب : بأن هذا الاستعداد راجع إلى صحة الفعل وسيأتي بجوابه. قوله : ( وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن التكوين هو المعنى الذي نجده في الفاعل وبه يمتاز عن غيره ويرتبط بالمفعول، وإن لم يوجد بعد وهذا لمعنى يعم الموجب أيضاً، بل هو موجود في الواجب بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة والإرادة فكيف لا يكون صفة أخرى. وأجيب : بأن الظاهر أن ارتباط الفاعل بالمفعول صلاحية تأثيره فيه والمعنى الذي يخص الفاعل مبدأ تلك الصلاحية وذلك المبدأ في الواجب بالنسبة إلى المحدثات نفس القدرة والإرادة وبالنسبة إلى صفاته تعالى نفس ذاته الممتازة بذاتها عن سائر الذوات
It was said: This does not accord with their statement that takwin is identical with the made thing (al-mukawwan), for there is no meaning to the word 'kun' being identical with the made thing. The answer is that this is the claim of the Ash'aris who deny that it is an additional attribute, and the one who says that takwin is the word 'kun' identical with the made thing – the answer is that this is the claim of the Ash'aris who deny that it is an additional attribute, and the one who says that takwin is the word 'kun' are the Maturidis who affirm it as an attribute. His saying: 'And that it is other than power and will because bringing into existence is preceded by will.' His saying: 'For the possible, it is intrinsic.' It was said: Against this, it is permissible that what is meant by soundness is preparedness (al-isti'dadi), which is the nearness of occurrence of the effect, so it can be caused. The answer: This preparedness reduces to the soundness of the act, and its answer will come later. His saying: 'And then there is no need for a principle etc.' An objection was raised against it: Takwin is the meaning that we find in the agent, by which he is distinguished from other agents, and it is connected to the effect even if it does not yet exist, and this meaning also applies to the necessitating agent (al-mujib); rather, it exists in the Necessary Being (al-wajib) with respect to power and will themselves, so how is it not another attribute? The answer: The apparent meaning is that the connection of the agent to the effect is the suitability (salahiyya) of his causing an effect in it, and the meaning specific to the agent is the principle of that suitability, and that principle in the Necessary Being with respect to originated things is power and will themselves, and with respect to His attributes, it is His essence itself, which is distinguished by its essence from all other essences.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a collection of objections and answers defending the earlier argument that qudra suffices without a separate attribute of takwin. It begins with an objection that the statement 'takwin is identical with the made thing' (al-mukawwan) is problematic because the word 'kun' cannot be the same as the created thing. The answer distinguishes between the Ash'ari view (denying takwin as an additional attribute) and the Maturidi view (affirming it as attribute). Another objection states that takwin is other than power and will because bringing into existence is preceded by will. Then an objection about prepared possibility (imkan isti'dadi) is raised and deferred. The main objection is then presented: takwin is a distinct meaning found in the agent, by which he is distinguished, and this meaning also applies to the necessitating agent, and even exists in the Necessary Being with respect to power and will, so it must be an additional attribute. The answer explains that the connection between agent and effect is the suitability (salahiyya) for causation, and the principle of that suitability in the Necessary Being is power and will themselves with respect to created things, and with respect to His attributes, it is His essence which is inherently distinguished. Thus, no separate attribute of takwin is needed.

Key Terms
تكوين
مكون
أشاعرة
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all theologians agree that takwin is a separate attribute.
Correction: Ash'aris deny takwin as an additional attribute, while Maturidis affirm it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that takwin is not an additional attribute?
A) Maturidis
B) Ash'aris
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement on takwin hinges on whether takwin is identical to the made thing or a distinct attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to dismiss a theological position without understanding its internal framework? This passage shows how an objection can miss its target when it conflates different systems. How might you apply this lesson to how you listen to those you disagree with?
?

?
When you encounter a disagreement among scholars, do you seek to understand the underlying assumptions—or do you stay on the surface and take sides? What would change if you first tried to see from the other perspective before forming an opinion?

The Suitability Argument Against Takwin as a Distinct Attribute
Objection and answer proving takwin is not an additional attribute but rooted in suitability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why takwin does not need to be a separate attribute: the connection between agent and effect is rooted in suitability (salahiyya), which is grounded in power, will, or essence.

Core Idea
Takwin is not a separate attribute; it reduces to power, will, or essence.

In this lesson, we examine a major objection: that takwin appears to be a distinct meaning found in the agent. The answer develops the concept of 'suitability' (salahiyya) to show that the agent's connection to the effect is already accounted for by power, will, or essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full passage in Arabic and translation. Note the progression from preliminary objections to the main one.
The main objection starts with 'قوله : ( وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ إلخ)'
2
Identify the main objection
The objector argues that takwin is a meaning found in the agent that distinguishes him and connects to the effect, and it also applies to necessitating agents and the Necessary Being.
3
Understand the answer via suitability
The answer explains that the connection is suitability (salahiyya), and the principle of that suitability in the Necessary Being is power and will for created things, and His essence for His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting the Karramiyya on the Modality of Vision
Al-Iji refutes the Karramiyya claim that vision requires facing direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari position on divine vision does not require facing direction, and how al-Iji refutes the Karramiyya claim of necessity.

Core Idea
Seeing God does not require facing direction because God transcends space.

Al-Iji now addresses a sectarian objection: the Karramiyya and Mujassima agree that God can be seen, but they insist that vision requires the object to be facing the viewer. This contradicts the Ash'ari belief that God is beyond direction. In this passage, al-Iji exposes the flaw in their reasoning by comparing it to a prior false claim about existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Notice the two positions: the Ash'ari view that vision occurs without facing, and the Karramiyya claim that vision requires facing.
The passage is an appendix (tadhnib) addressing a specific sectarian dispute.
2
Identify the key disagreement
The Karramiyya claim it is self-evident (necessary) that something not in front of the viewer cannot be seen. Al-Iji denies this necessity. Understand what is at stake: divine transcendence.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Iji compares the Karramiyya's claim to the false claim that every existent must be in a direction. Since that claim has been rejected by philosophers and Mu'tazila, the analogous claim about vision is also false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum by analogy.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflecting on this argument reinforces the Ash'ari commitment to divine transcendence (tanzih). God is not confined by space or direction, so vision of Him does not require spatial orientation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وماذا فيه من الدليل على نفي الرؤية . ( تذنيب ( الكرامية ) والمجسمة ( وافقونا في الرؤية وخالفونا في الكيفية فعندنا أن الرؤية تكون من غير مواجهة ) ولا مقابلة ولا ما في حكمها ( إذ يمتنع ذلك في الموجد المنزه عن الجهة والمكان وهم يدعون الضرورة في أن ما لا يكون في جهة قدام الرائي ولا مقابلاً له أو في حكم المقابل لا يرى موافقين في ذلك للمعتزلة ) ومخالفين لهم في أصل الرؤية ) والجواب أنا نمنع الضرورة وما ذلك ) أي ادعاء الضرورة ) منهم ) هاهنا ) إلا كدعوى الضرورة في أن كل موجود فإنه في جهة وحيز وما ليس في حيز وجهة فإنه ليس بموجود ولعل هذا الادعاء ( فرعه) أني فرع ذلك الادعاء وقد وافقنا الحكماء والمعتزلة على أن حصر الموجود فيما ذكر حكم وهمي مما ليس بمحسوس فيكون باطلا فكذا الضرورة التي ادعاها الكرامية والمجسمة في الرؤية .
And what evidence is there in it for negating vision? An appendix: The Karramiyya and the Mujassima agree with us on vision but disagree on the modality. For us, vision occurs without facing or confrontation or anything like it, because that is impossible for the Existent who is transcendent over direction and place. They claim necessity that what is not in the direction of the viewer's front, nor confronting him, nor in the status of confronting, is not seen, agreeing with the Mu'tazila on that, but disagreeing with them on the reality of vision. The answer: we deny the necessity, and this [claim of necessity] from them here is nothing but like the claim of necessity that every existent is in a direction and place, and what is not in place and direction is not existent. Perhaps this claim is a branch of that claim. We have agreed with the philosophers and the Mu'tazila that restricting existents to what is mentioned is an imaginary judgment not based on sense, so it is false. Likewise the necessity that the Karramiyya and Mujassima claim concerning vision.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is an appendix (tadhnib) addressing the Karramiyya and Mujassima (anthropomorphists) who agree with the Ash'aris that God can be seen but disagree on the modality: they claim that vision requires the seen object to be facing the viewer or in front. The author (al-Iji) states that for Ash'aris, vision occurs without any facing or confrontation, because God is transcendent over direction and place. The Karramiyya and Mujassima claim it is self-evident that something not in front of the viewer cannot be seen, and they agree with the Mu'tazila on this point (though the Mu'tazila deny vision entirely). The refutation is that this claimed necessity is false; it parallels their false claim that every existent must be in a direction. The philosophers and Mu'tazila have already refuted that, so similarly the necessity regarding vision is baseless.

Key Terms
كرامية
مجسمة
رؤية
كيفية
جهة
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing something always requires it to be physically in front of the viewer.
Correction: Al-Iji argues that this claimed necessity is baseless; it parallels the false claim that every existent must be in a direction. God, being transcendent, can be seen without spatial orientation.

Checkpoint
How do the Karramiyya and Mujassima differ from the Ash'aris on the vision of God?
A) They deny vision completely
B) They agree on vision but claim it requires facing direction
C) They say vision is metaphorical
D) They claim God can only be seen in dreams

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji refutes the Karramiyya by showing their claimed necessity about vision parallels a false claim about existence, thus it is baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing God, do you unconsciously picture it like seeing a physical object? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what concrete step can you take to guard your understanding of God's transcendence?
?
Al-Iji compares the Karramiyya's claim to a false necessity about existence. Can you identify a belief you hold about God that might be based on a false 'necessity' from your daily experience? What would it take to truly submit your intellect to divine transcendence?
?
This lesson shows that even well-intentioned attempts to affirm vision can lead to anthropomorphism. Have you ever defended a belief about God without realizing it compromised His transcendence? How will you ensure your theology remains pure from such subtle distortions?

Refuting the Necessity of Facing in Vision of God
Refutation of the claim that facing is necessary for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karramiyya's claim that facing is necessary for vision is rejected in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Vision of God does not require direction or confrontation.

This passage tackles a key sectarian dispute about how God is seen. The Karramiyya and Mujassima argue that vision necessarily involves facing the object, but the Ash'ari position holds that God, being beyond direction, can be seen without such conditions. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Position of agreement and disagreement
Read the opening to see where the Karramiyya agree with us (on the reality of vision) and where they differ (on modality).
The disagreement is not about whether God is seen, but how.
2
The Ash'ari stance on vision without facing
Notice that the Ash'ari affirm vision without facing, because God is transcendent above direction.
3
The Karramiyya claim of necessity
Identify their claim: that it is self-evident that something not in front cannot be seen.
This claim aligns them with the Mu'tazila on this point.
4
The refutation and analogy
See how the author denies this necessity and compares it to the false claim that every existent must be in a direction.
The analogy shows the parallel between two alleged necessities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب الكرامية والمجسمة وافقونا في الرؤية وخالفونا في الكيفية فعندنا أن الرؤية تكون من غير مواجهة ولا مقابلة ولا ما في حكمها إذ يمتنع ذلك في الموجد المنزه عن الجهة والمكان وهم يدعون الضرورة في أن ما لا يكون في جهة قدام الرائي ولا مقابلاً له أو في حكم المقابل لا يرى موافقين في ذلك للمعتزلة ومخالفين لهم في أصل الرؤية والجواب أنا نمنع الضرورة وما ذلك أي ادعاء الضرورة منهم هاهنا إلا كدعوى الضرورة في أن كل موجود فإنه في جهة وحيز وما ليس في حيز وجهة فإنه ليس بموجود ولعل هذا الادعاء فرع ذلك الادعاء وقد وافقنا الحكماء والمعتزلة على أن حصر الموجود فيما ذكر حكم وهمي مما ليس بمحسوس فيكون باطلا فكذا الضرورة التي ادعاها الكرامية والمجسمة في الرؤية
Appendix: The Karramiyya and the Mujassima agree with us on [the reality of] vision but differ on modality. For us, vision occurs without facing, nor confrontation, nor anything in their ruling, because that is impossible for the Existent who is transcendent above direction and place. They claim necessity in that what is not in the direction in front of the seer, nor confronting him, nor in the ruling of confrontation, is not seen, agreeing thereby with the Mu'tazila and differing from them on the principle of vision. The answer: we deny the necessity. That claim of necessity from them here is nothing but like the claim of necessity that every existent is in a direction and space, and what is not in space and direction is not existent. Perhaps this claim is a branch of that claim. And we, along with the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, have agreed that confining existence to what was mentioned is an imaginary judgment about what is not sensible, so it is false. Likewise is the necessity claimed by the Karramiyya and the Mujassima concerning vision.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (tadhniib) of the Karramiyya and Mujassima sects regarding the vision of God (ru'ya). The author states that these sects agree with the Ash'aris that God will be seen in the afterlife, but disagree on the modality (kayfiyya). The Ash'ari position is that vision can occur without the seer facing the object or being opposite it, since God is beyond direction and place. The Karramiyya and Mujassima, however, claim a self-evident necessity (darura) that something not in front of the seer or not confronting him cannot be seen. This claim aligns them with the Mu'tazila in this specific point, even though they differ on the principle of vision's possibility. The author responds by denying this supposed necessity. He compares it to another false necessity: the claim that every existent must be in a direction or place, and what is not in place or direction does not exist. This latter claim is rejected by majority of sound thinkers, including philosophers and Mu'tazila, because it is an imaginary judgment (hukm wahmi) about non-sensible matters. By analogy, the Karramiyya's claim about vision is equally baseless.

Key Terms
رؤية
كرامية
مجسمة
مواجهة
مقابلة
ضرورة
جهة
مكان
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view on vision involves direction or facing.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that vision occurs 'without facing nor confrontation' because God is beyond direction.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the modality of seeing God, according to this passage?
A) God is seen while facing Him directly.
B) God is seen without facing or confrontation.
C) God is seen only in a direction.
D) God cannot be seen at all.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's claimed necessity for facing in vision is rejected as an imaginary judgment, analogous to the false claim that all existents must be in space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any 'necessities' you have unconsciously assumed about Allah—such as that He must 'face' you to hear your dua or see your actions? How does this passage challenge you to purify your understanding of His transcendence? What specific step will you take today to remind yourself that Allah is beyond direction and place?
?
The passage calls the Karramiyya's claim an 'imaginary judgment' that stems from limiting Allah to your own experience. What areas of your life—your desires, fears, or routines—reveal that you are still imagining Allah in a human-like way? Write down one such area and commit to replacing that assumption with the teaching that He is beyond all limitations.

The Dispute over Knowing God's Essence and the Philosophers' Proof
The commentator lists differing opinions and presents the philosophers' proof for impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on whether knowledge of God's essence is possible and the philosophers' logical argument that it is impossible.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that knowledge of God's essence is impossible because it is neither self-evident nor definable.

This passage begins by noting that the possibility of knowing God's essence is a contested issue. Some deny it, some suspend judgment, and others imply its impossibility. The philosophers offer a logical proof that knowledge of an essence can only come through self-evidence or definition, and since God's essence is neither, it cannot be known. We will examine their argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the different positions
Read the opening sentences to see how various scholars and groups differ on the possibility of knowing God's essence. Not the philosophers, al-Ghazali, al-Juwayni, al-Baqillani, Dirar ibn Amr, and the Sufis.
2
Understand the philosophers' premise
The philosophers claim that knowledge of an essence is either self-evident or acquired through reasoning, and reasoning uses either description (rasm) or definition (hadd).
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Since God's essence is not self-evident and cannot be defined due to lack of composition, the philosophers conclude that knowledge of it is impossible.
4
Reflect on the structure of the argument
Notice how the argument is built on a dichotomy and careful elimination of possibilities. This sets the stage for the answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني: الجواز. وفي جواز العلم بحقيقة الله تعالى خلاف، منعه الفلاسفة وبعض أصحابنا كالغزالي وإمام الحرمين، ومنهم من توقف كالقاضي أبي بكر وضرار بن عمرو، وكلام الصوفية في الأكثر مشعر بالامتناع. وإنما منعه الفلاسفة لأن المعقول إما بالبديهة وحقيقته ليست بديهية، وإما بالنظر، والنظر إما في الرسم وهو لا يفيد الحقيقة، وإما في الحد، فإذن لا نعلم الحقيقة إلا بالبديهة أو بالحد، وحقيقته تعالى ليست بديهية ولا يمكن تحديدها لعدم التركيب فيها، فلا يمكن العلم بها. والجواب: منع حصر المدرك بالكنه في البديهية والحد والرسم، لجواز خلق الله تعالى علماً متعلقاً بما ليس ضرورياً بالقياس إلى عموم الناس في شخص بلا سابقة نظر، كما سبق من أن النظري قد ينقلب ضرورياً لبعض الأشخاص. وأيضاً فالرسم وإن لم يجب أن يفيد الحقيقة فلا يمتنع أن يفيدها. وأجيب بأن هذا أيضاً كلي إذ لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين وإن كان المفروض محالاً. قوله: (الجواب منع حصر المدرك إلخ) منع الحصر إنما يتم بجعل البديهي بمعنى بالنسبة إلى عموم الناس كما أشار إليه الشارح، فلك أن تقرر الجواب بمنع عدم البداهة البديهي أيضاً، فتأمل.
The second station: Possibility. And concerning the possibility of knowledge of the reality of God Almighty there is disagreement. The philosophers and some of our companions like al-Ghazali and Imam al-Haramayn forbade it, and among them is one who suspended judgment like al-Qadi Abu Bakr and Dirar ibn Amr. The speech of the Sufis mostly indicates impossibility. The philosophers forbade it because the intelligible is either self-evident and His reality is not self-evident, or [acquired] by reflection, and reflection is either by description (rasm) which does not convey the reality, or by definition (hadd); thus we only know reality by the self-evident or by definition, and His reality is not self-evident, nor can it be defined due to lack of composition in Him, so knowledge of it is impossible. The answer: The denial of limitation of the means of attaining [knowledge] of essence to the self-evident, definition, and description, because it is possible for God Almighty to create knowledge related to what is not necessary in relation to the generality of people in a person without prior reflection, just as it preceded that the theoretical may become self-evident for some persons. Also, description even if it is not necessarily informative of reality, it is not impossible that it should inform it. And it was answered: This is also universal, since it is not impossible to suppose its truth for many, even if the supposed is impossible. His saying: (The answer is the denial of limitation etc.) The denial of limitation is only complete by making the self-evident in the sense of relative to the generality of people as the commentator indicated; so you can formulate the answer by denying the non-self-evidentness of the self-evident also. Reflect.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility (jawaz) of knowing God's essence. It reports disagreement: philosophers and some theologians (like al-Ghazali and al-Juwayni) deny it, others like al-Baqillani and Dirar ibn Amr suspend judgment, and Sufi statements usually imply impossibility. The philosophers' proof is presented: knowledge of an essence is either self-evident (badihi) or acquired through reasoning. Reasoning uses either description (rasm) which does not convey the true nature, or definition (hadd). Since God's essence is not self-evident and cannot be defined due to lack of composition, knowledge is impossible. The answer given is to deny that the means of knowing an essence are limited to these three. God can create direct knowledge in a person without prior reasoning, and theoretical knowledge can become self-evident for some. Also, description may inform the true nature even if it is not necessary. A further objection is raised that this answer is still universal (kulli) and does not specify God. The commentator then suggests a way to refine the answer by redefining 'self-evident' as relative to the generality of people.

Key Terms
الجواز
البديهة
النظر
الرسم
الحد
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of an essence can only be attained through self-evidence or definition.
Correction: The answer will show that other means are possible, such as direct divine creation of knowledge and description possibly conveying reality.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' main reason for denying knowledge of God's essence, as stated in this passage?
A) God's essence is too exalted for human intellect
B) Knowledge of essences is only by self-evidence or definition, and God's essence is neither
C) The soul cannot perceive the divine
D) All knowledge of God is forbidden

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that knowledge of God's essence is impossible because it is neither self-evident nor definable, setting the stage for a response.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realize that even the greatest minds concede they cannot know Allah's essence, does that humble your intellectual pride? In what specific areas of your faith do you subtly assume you have Allah figured out, and how can you replace that assumption with awe?
?
If description (rasm) does not convey reality, then your prayers and supplications are limited to describing Allah. How does that affect the way you approach Him? Do you truly surrender to His unknowable essence, or do you cling to the comfort of your descriptions?
?
This passage reveals a disagreement among scholars—some suspended judgment on this matter. Are there aspects of your belief that you treat as fully known when they are actually beyond your grasp? Commit today to admitting ignorance where it is due and trusting Allah's revelation without demanding rational certainty.

The Answer to the Philosophers: Alternate Means of Knowledge
The commentator presents the answer to the philosophers' argument and discusses its implications and a refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the philosophers' argument by denying the limitation of means of knowing and introducing the possibility of direct divine knowledge.

Core Idea
The answer denies that knowledge of an essence is limited to self-evidence and definition.

Having presented the philosophers' argument, the commentator now gives an answer. The key move is to deny that the only ways to know an essence are self-evidence, definition, or description. God can create knowledge directly, and even description may reveal true nature. This lesson unpacks the answer and the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
The answer begins by denying the limitation of the means of knowing essences to just three: self-evident, definition, and description.
2
First point: direct creation of knowledge
It is possible for God to create knowledge in a person without prior reasoning, and theoretical knowledge can become self-evident for some individuals.
This undercuts the premise that all knowledge arises from reflection.
3
Second point: description may suffice
Even if description does not necessarily convey the true nature, it is not impossible that it could do so, especially in the case of God?
4
Consider the objection and refinement
An objection is raised that the answer is still universal and does not specify God. The commentator then notes that the denial of limitation is complete when 'self-evident' is understood relative to the generality of people, and one can even deny that God's essence is not self-evident in that relative sense.
This refinement strengthens the answer.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Notice how the answer does not disprove the philosophers' premises directly but shows that the list of possible means is incomplete.

The Scope of 'Most of them': Exceptions Among the Mu'tazila
Commentator clarifies the phrase 'most of them' and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator specifies 'most of them' and which scholars it excludes.

Core Idea
Not all Mu'tazila hold the same view; al-Najjar and Abu al-Husayn are exceptions.

When reading theological texts, phrases like 'most of them' are important qualifications. In this passage, the commentator explains that the author's use of 'most of them' regarding the Mu'tazila is meant to exclude certain figures, such as al-Najjar and Abu al-Husayn. This clarification prevents misunderstanding about the unity of the Mu'tazilite school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the quoted phrase 'most of them' and the commentator's explanation.
Pay attention to the word 'احتراز' (precaution/exclusion).
2
Identify the purpose of 'most of them'
The commentator states that 'most of them' is a precaution. What is it guarding against? It excludes al-Najjar and Abu al-Husayn from the general statement.
This is a common technique in scholarly writing to be precise.
3
Note the excluded scholars
Al-Najjar and Abu al-Husayn are named as exceptions. Understand that they are Mu'tazilite figures but hold distinct views.
Abu al-Husayn's exclusion is noted as a point of discussion (محل بحث).
4
Understand the concept of 'احتراز'
The term 'احتراز' means a precautionary exclusion. It is used to avoid misattributing a view to all members of a group.
This shows the author's careful wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي أكثرهم احتراز عن النجار كما سيظهر بل عن أبي الحسين أيضاً كما يقتضيه سياق كلام المصنف وإن كان محل بحث . قوله : (بلا إيجاب بل (باختبار لا خفاء أنه لا يظهر من المتن فرق بين مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء لأن المعتزلة قائلون بأن الله تعالى خالق القوى والقدر فأشار الشارح إلى الفرق بينهما
His statement: 'i.e., most of them' is a precaution against al-Najjar, as will appear, and also against Abu al-Husayn, as the context of the author's words requires, though it is a point of discussion. His statement: 'without necessitation but with choice' — it is clear that from the text no difference appears between the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers, because the Mu'tazila say that God is the Creator of powers and ability. So the commentator indicates the difference between them.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This note clarifies that the Mu'tazila are not all of the same view; 'most of them' excludes some like al-Najjar. Also, a distinction between Mu'tazila and philosophers is that Mu'tazila affirm that God creates the powers, but the philosophers may have a different conception of causality.

Distinguishing Mu'tazila from Philosophers on Divine Causality
Commentator explains the difference between Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding God's creation of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key difference between Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding the creation of human powers.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila affirm God as Creator of powers; philosophers do not.

A superficial reading of the text might suggest that the Mu'tazila and philosophers agree on human acts. However, the commentator highlights a crucial distinction: the Mu'tazila explicitly state that God is the Creator of human powers and abilities. This sets them apart from the philosophers, who may hold a different view on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on the phrase 'بلا إيجاب بل باختبار' and the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the apparent similarity
The commentator notes that from the text alone, no difference appears between Mu'tazila and philosophers. Both seem to affirm choice without necessitation.
This could lead to confusion.
3
Spot the key distinction
The commentator points out that the Mu'tazila explicitly say God is the Creator of powers and ability (خالق القوى والقدر). This is where they differ from philosophers.
Philosophers may not attribute the creation of these powers to God in the same way.
4
Understand the purpose of the commentary
The commentator's clarification prevents the reader from mistakenly equating the two schools on this point.
This is a common need in theological discussions.

قوله : (أي أكثرهم احتراز عن النجار كما سيظهر بل عن أبي الحسين أيضاً كما يقتضيه سياق كلام المصنف وإن كان محل بحث . قوله : (بلا إيجاب بل (باختبار لا خفاء أنه لا يظهر من المتن فرق بين مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء لأن المعتزلة قائلون بأن الله تعالى خالق القوى والقدر فأشار الشارح إلى الفرق بينهما

Commentary
This note clarifies that the Mu'tazila are not all of the same view; 'most of them' excludes some like al-Najjar. Also, a distinction between Mu'tazila and philosophers is that Mu'tazila affirm that God creates the powers, but the philosophers may have a different conception of causality.

?

?

Abu al-Husayn's Denial of Mu'tazilism Refuted
The author shows Abu al-Husayn's argument implicitly accepts Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Husayn's denial of Mu'tazilism is considered logically inconsistent with his own view on decisive will.

Core Idea
Necessitating will implies Mu'tazilism, making its denial insincere.

In this passage, the author of al-Mawaqif exposes a tension in Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's thought. While Abu al-Husayn explicitly distances himself from Mu'tazilism, the author argues that his theory of decisive will logically commits him to the very position he rejects. Let's examine how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noticing the rhetorical structure. Then review the translation to ensure you grasp the literal meaning.
2
Identify Abu al-Husayn's position
The passage refers to someone who admits that an act necessarily occurs when the decisive will arises. Who is this, and what is his view?
Abu al-Husayn al-Basri held that the act follows necessarily from the decisive will.
3
Understand the author's analogy
The author draws comparisons between commanding/punishing for an act done by the servant and for an act necessitated by something in him. Why does he say there is no rational difference?
He argues that in both cases the servant is capable of action or omission, so the moral responsibility is the same.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that admitting the necessity of the act closes the door to denying Mu'tazilism. What does this reveal about Abu al-Husayn's own stance?
His position logically leads to Mu'tazilite agency, so his denial is a mere obfuscation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الأول : في أفعاله تعالى وأفعال العباد غير لا فرق في العقل بين أن يأمر الله ) عبده ( بما يفعله ) هو بنفسه (و) بين أن يأمره (بما يجب ) عند فعله ويمتنع عند عدمه فإن المأمور على كلا التقديرين متمكن من الفعل أو الترك، وأيضاً لا فرق بين أن يعذب الله العبد على ما أوجده فيه وبين أن يعذبه على فعل يجب حصوله عندما وجده فيه، لأنه لا فرق في العقول بين فاعل القبيح والظلم وبين فاعل ما يوجب القبيح والظلم فمن اعترف بوجوب حصول الفعل عند حصوله الإرادة الجازمة انسد عليه باب القول بالاعتزال، فظهر أن أبا الحسين أنكر الاعتزال في هذه المسألة وأن تلك المبالغة منه تمويه وتلبيس انتهى كلامه .
The sixth observation - first objective: concerning His acts, may He be exalted, and the acts of servants. Not: there is no difference in reason between God commanding His servant to do what he does himself and between commanding him to what is necessary when he does it and impossible when he does not do it, for the commanded one in both estimates is capable of doing or leaving. Also, there is no difference between God punishing the servant for what He [God] originated in him and between punishing him for an act whose occurrence is necessary when it is found in him, because there is no difference in intellects between the doer of the ugly and injustice and between the doer of that which necessitates the ugly and injustice. So whoever admits the necessity of the occurrence of the act when the decisive will occurs, the door of saying Mu'tazilism is closed upon him. So it appears that Abu al-Husayn denied Mu'tazilism in this issue and that this exaggeration from him is camouflage and obfuscation. End of his speech.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's attempt to distance himself from Mu'tazilism. The author argues that Abu al-Husayn's position, which holds that an act necessarily follows when the decisive will (al-irada al-jazima) occurs, logically entails Mu'tazilism. The reasoning is that there is no rational distinction between commanding or punishing a servant for an act he does himself and for an act that is necessitated by something in him. Both cases leave the servant capable of action or omission. Since Abu al-Husayn admits that the act is necessitated by the will, he cannot consistently deny Mu'tazilism, which asserts human agency. Thus, his denial is insincere ('tamwih wa talbis').

Key Terms
إرادة جازمة
اعتزال
تمويه وتلبيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abu al-Husayn's position on decisive will is neutral regarding Mu'tazilism.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that his view logically entails Mu'tazilite agency, making his denial inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider Abu al-Husayn's denial of Mu'tazilism insincere?
A) Because he explicitly stated he is a Mu'tazilite.
B) Because his admission that acts follow necessarily from will entails Mu'tazilite agency.
C) Because he denied the existence of divine acts.
D) Because he rejected the concept of punishment.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Husayn's admission that acts necessarily follow from decisive will logically commits him to Mu'tazilism, making his denial inconsistent and insincere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that rest on a hidden premise which, if examined, would force you to abandon them? Will you search for such premises today?
?
When you defend a position, do you ever camouflage inconsistencies to avoid embarrassment, even to yourself? What would it take to face them with honesty?
?
If your own reasoning commits you to a conclusion you dislike, will you follow the truth wherever it leads, or will you cling to comfort and obfuscate?

The Orthodox Interpretation of Quranic Sealing Terms
Orthodox view on Quranic sealing terms as real creation of misguidance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the orthodox (Ahl al-Haqq) position that Quranic terms like 'sealing' and 'stamping' refer to the creation of misguidance in the hearts.

Core Idea
Quranic seals are real preventives: God creates misguidance that bars guidance.

This lesson focuses on the first part of the passage where the author presents the orthodox position on Quranic terms like tab`, khatm, akinnah, aqfal. The Ahl al-Haqq take these literally as descriptions of God creating misguidance in the hearts, acting as barriers to faith. This contrasts with the Mu`tazilite allegorical interpretations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses and their terms
Start by reading the Arabic passage that lists the Quranic terms: tab`, khatm, akinnah, and aqfal. Notice the verses cited.
These are the key terms that will be interpreted.
2
Understand the Ahl al-Haqq's linguistic argument
The passage explains that these words in Arabic denote real preventives. Misguidance (dalal) is a preventive of guidance, so the terms are applied literally to the creation of misguidance.
3
Grasp the principle of continuity (al-istitrad)
The author states that the original principle is continuity until a preventive occurs; the burden of proof is on one who claims otherwise. This supports the literal interpretation.
This principle is used to justify the orthodox view.
4
Prepare to contrast with Mu`tazilite views
Keep the orthodox view in mind as you proceed to the next lesson on Mu`tazilite interpretations. Note how each avoids attributing the creation of misguidance directly to God.
This will highlight the theological differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن [ المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن ] ۱۸۷ ( في البحث عن أمور صرح بها القرآن وانعقد عليها الإجماع وهم يؤولونها ) الأول : الطبع : قال الله تعالى بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم ﴾ [النساء: ١٥٥] ( والختم ) ختم الله على قلوبهم ﴾ [البقرة : ٧] ، ( والأكنة ) وجعلنا على قلوبهم ﴾ : أكنة أن يفقهوه ﴾ [الأنعام : ٢٥]، ( ونحوها كالأفعال في قوله تعالى : أم على قلوب أقفالها ﴾ [محمد : ٢٤ ] ، فذهب أهل الحق إلى أنها عبارة عن خلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة، وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة وخلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة، وخلق الضلال في القلوب مانع من الهدى، فصح تسميته بهذه الأسماء لأن الأصل هو الاطراد إلى أن يمنع مانع والأصل عدمه، فمن ادعاه يحتاج إلى البيان والمعتزلة ( أولوها بوجوه : الأول : ( وهو لأوائل المعتزلة ( ختم الله على قلوبهم إلى آخر الآيات أي سماها مختوماً عليها ومطبوعاً عليها ومجعولاً عليها أكنة وأقفال ووصفها بذلك كما قال وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً ﴾ [الزخرف : ۱۹]، أي سموهم بذلك ووصفوهم بالأنوثة إذ لا قدرة لهم على الجعل الحقيقي . ( الثاني : ( وهو للجائي وابنه ومن تابعهما ( وسميها ) أي الله على قلوب الكافر ( بسمات ) وعلامات ) تعرفها الملائكة فيتميز بها الكافر عن المؤمن)، وذلك لأن الختم والطبع في اللغة هو الوسم ولا يمتنع أن يخلق الله في قلوب الفجار سمة تتميز بها عن قلوب الأبرار ونتبين تلك السمة للملائكة فيذمون من اتسم بها، وذلك في مصلحة دينية لأنه إذا علم العبد أنه إذا كفر وسم بسمة يتحقق بها ذمه ولعنه من الملائكة كان ذلك سبباً لانزجاره عنه. ( الثالث : ) وهو للكعبي (منع الله منهم اللطف المقرب إلى الطاعة ) المبعد عن المعصية ( لعلمه أنه ينفعهم ) ولا يؤثر فيهم ، ( فلما لم يوفقوا لذلك ) اللطف ( فكأنهم ختم على قلوبهم) لأن قطع اللطف مانع من دخول الإيمان كما أن الختم والطبع والأكنة والأقفال موانع من الدخول . ( الرابع ) وهو لبعض أصحاب عبد الواحد من المعتزلة (منعهم الله الإخلاص الموجب لقبول العمل فكانوا لذلك ) كمن يمنع دخول الإيمان قلبه بالختم عليه وسقوله : ( لعلمه أنه لا ينفعهم إلخ فيه دلالة على أن المعتزلة وإن أوجبوا اللطف على الله تعالى لكن لا يوجبونه إذا علم أنه لا ينفعهم.
The Sixth Observatory – The Third Goal: Matters Explicitly Stated in the Quran. [ The Third Goal: Matters Explicitly Stated in the Quran ] 187 (In investigation of matters explicitly stated in the Quran and upon which consensus is concluded, and they interpret them allegorically.) First: al-tab` (sealing): Allah Most High said: 'But Allah has sealed them for their disbelief' [al-Nisa', 155]; and al-khatm (stamping): 'Allah has stamped upon their hearts' [al-Baqarah, 7]; and al-akinnah (covers): 'And We have placed upon their hearts covers so that they do not understand it' [al-An'am, 25]; and similar things like al-aqfal (locks) in His statement Most High: 'Or are there locks upon their hearts?' [Muhammad, 24]. Ahl al-Haqq go to that they are expressions for the creation of misguidance in the hearts, because these things in language are real preventives, and they are named thus for being preventive, and the creation of misguidance in the hearts is preventive of guidance. So their naming by these names is correct, because the original principle is continuity until a preventive prevents, and the original is its absence; so whoever claims it needs clarification. And the Mu`tazila interpreted them in ways: First: (and it is for the early Mu`tazila): 'Allah has stamped upon their hearts' etc., i.e., He named them stamped upon, sealed upon, and made upon them covers and locks, and described them as such, just as He said: 'And they make the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females' [al-Zukhruf, 19], i.e., they named them so and described them as female, since they have no power for real making. Second: (and it is for al-Jubba'i and his son and those who followed them): And He named them – i.e., Allah, upon the hearts of the disbelievers – with marks and signs that the angels know, whereby the disbeliever is distinguished from the believer. This is because stamping and sealing in language is branding, and it is not impossible that Allah creates in the hearts of the wicked a mark that distinguishes them from the hearts of the righteous, and We make that mark evident to the angels, so they blame whoever is marked with it. And that is in a religious interest, because if the servant knows that when he disbelieves he is marked with a mark that realizes his blame and curse from the angels, that becomes a cause for his refraining from it. Third: (and it is for al-Ka`bi): Allah's prevention of them from the divine grace (al-lutf) that brings near to obedience and distances from disobedience, because He knows that it does not benefit them and does not affect them. So when they were not granted that grace, it is as if their hearts were stamped, because the cutting off of grace is a preventive from the entry of faith, just as stamping, sealing, covers, and locks are preventives from entry. Fourth: (and it is for some of the companions of 'Abd al-Wahid from the Mu`tazila): Allah prevented them from sincerity (al-ikhlas) which necessitates the acceptance of deeds, so they were therefore like one whose heart is prevented from the entry of faith by stamping. And his saying: 'because He knows that it does not benefit them' etc. contains an indication that the Mu`tazila, although they obligate grace (al-lutf) upon Allah Most High, do not obligate it when He knows that it does not benefit them.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage begins the third goal of the sixth observatory, which treats Quranic terms that Mu`tazila interpret allegorically. It first lists key terms: tab` (sealing), khatm (stamping), akinnah (covers), and aqfal (locks). The author states that Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) take these words literally as referring to the creation of misguidance (dalal) in the hearts. The reasoning is that these terms linguistically denote real preventive barriers, and misguidance prevents guidance, so the metaphorical extension is valid; the default is continuity until a preventive arises. Then the passage presents four Mu`tazilite interpretations: (1) The early Mu`tazila consider these as metaphorical descriptions (tasmiah) – God merely describes the hearts as stamped, just as He described the angels as female without real gender change. (2) Al-Jubba'i and his son interpret them as actual marks (simat) created by God in the hearts for angels to recognize disbelievers; this serves as a deterrent. (3) Al-Ka`bi interprets them as withholding divine grace (lutf) that would lead to obedience, because God knows it would not benefit them; the absence of grace is like a seal. (4) Some followers of 'Abd al-Wahid interpret them as withholding sincerity (ikhlas) which makes deeds acceptable. A concluding remark from the commentator notes that the Mu`tazila only obligate grace if beneficial.
