الختم (al-khatm)
الأكنة (al-akinnah)
الأقفال (al-aqfal)
الضلال (al-dalal)
مانع (mani`)
أصل الاطراد (asl al-istitrad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Haqq interpret sealing terms metaphorically, like the Mu`tazila.
Correction: No, Ahl al-Haqq take them literally as real creation of misguidance, while Mu`tazila offer metaphorical interpretations.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what does 'tab` Allahu 'ala qulubihim' mean?
A) God physically stamped a seal on their hearts
B) God created misguidance in their hearts
C) God described them as sealed
D) God withheld grace from them

Key Takeaway
The orthodox view holds that Quranic sealing terms denote the real creation of misguidance, which prevents guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your own heart could be sealed? What would that mean for your relationship with the Quran and with God? Today, ask yourself: is there any area where you feel a 'lock' preventing you from submitting fully? What must you do to unlock it while the key is still in your hand?
?
If God truly creates misguidance in hearts as a real preventive, how does that shape your view of His absolute sovereignty? Does it unsettle you or bring you peace? Let this question press on you until you acknowledge that only He can give or withhold guidance — and beg Him tonight to never seal your heart.
?
The default state is continuity of guidance until a preventive prevents. Are you actively removing preventives in your life — like pride, procrastination, or sin — that might become the very seal the Quran describes? Do not delay; examine your habits today and remove one barrier before you sleep.

Mu`tazilite Allegorical Interpretations of Quranic Sealing
Four Mu`tazilite interpretations of Quranic sealing terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four Mu`tazilite interpretations of Quranic sealing terms and their rationales.

Core Idea
Mu`tazila reinterpret seals as metaphors, marks, withheld grace, or sincerity.

This lesson presents the Mu`tazilite allegorical interpretations of the same Quranic terms. Four distinct views are outlined: the early Mu`tazila's metaphorical description, al-Jubba'i's mark theory, al-Ka`bi's withheld grace, and some companions of 'Abd al-Wahid's withheld sincerity. Each interpretation avoids the implication that God directly creates misguidance in hearts.

Guided Path
1
Read each of the four interpretations
Read the Arabic passage and translation that enumerates the four Mu`tazilite views. Note the names associated with each.
The first is for early Mu`tazila, second for al-Jubba'i, third for al-Ka`bi, fourth for followers of 'Abd al-Wahid.
2
Compare the linguistic basis
Observe the first interpretation uses tasmiah (naming) as a metaphor; the second uses simat (marks) as literal signs; the third interprets as withholding grace; the fourth as withholding sincerity.
Each seeks to explain the terms without accepting that God creates misguidance.
3
Note the theological motivation
The Mu`tazila are driven by the principle that God does not create evil or misguidance. These interpretations maintain divine justice by attributing the effect to the disbelievers' own actions.
This is a key difference from the Ahl al-Haqq view.
4
Reflect on the concluding remark
The passage ends with a note that Mu`tazila only obligate grace (lutf) if beneficial, showing the conditionality of their theology.
This ties into the broader Mu`tazilite doctrine of al-lutf.

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن [ المقصد الثالث : أمور صرح بها القرآن ] ۱۸۷ ( في البحث عن أمور صرح بها القرآن وانعقد عليها الإجماع وهم يؤولونها ) الأول : الطبع : قال الله تعالى بل طبع الله عليها بكفرهم ﴾ [النساء: ١٥٥] ( والختم ) ختم الله على قلوبهم ﴾ [البقرة : ٧] ، ( والأكنة ) وجعلنا على قلوبهم ﴾ : أكنة أن يفقهوه ﴾ [الأنعام : ٢٥]، ( ونحوها كالأفعال في قوله تعالى : أم على قلوب أقفالها ﴾ [محمد : ٢٤ ] ، فذهب أهل الحق إلى أنها عبارة عن خلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة، وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة وخلق الضلال في القلوب وذلك لأن هذه الأمور في اللغة موانع في الحقيقة وإنما سميت بذلك لكونها مانعة، وخلق الضلال في القلوب مانع من الهدى، فصح تسميته بهذه الأسماء لأن الأصل هو الاطراد إلى أن يمنع مانع والأصل عدمه، فمن ادعاه يحتاج إلى البيان والمعتزلة ( أولوها بوجوه : الأول : ( وهو لأوائل المعتزلة ( ختم الله على قلوبهم إلى آخر الآيات أي سماها مختوماً عليها ومطبوعاً عليها ومجعولاً عليها أكنة وأقفال ووصفها بذلك كما قال وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً ﴾ [الزخرف : ۱۹]، أي سموهم بذلك ووصفوهم بالأنوثة إذ لا قدرة لهم على الجعل الحقيقي . ( الثاني : ( وهو للجائي وابنه ومن تابعهما ( وسميها ) أي الله على قلوب الكافر ( بسمات ) وعلامات ) تعرفها الملائكة فيتميز بها الكافر عن المؤمن)، وذلك لأن الختم والطبع في اللغة هو الوسم ولا يمتنع أن يخلق الله في قلوب الفجار سمة تتميز بها عن قلوب الأبرار ونتبين تلك السمة للملائكة فيذمون من اتسم بها، وذلك في مصلحة دينية لأنه إذا علم العبد أنه إذا كفر وسم بسمة يتحقق بها ذمه ولعنه من الملائكة كان ذلك سبباً لانزجاره عنه. ( الثالث : ) وهو للكعبي (منع الله منهم اللطف المقرب إلى الطاعة ) المبعد عن المعصية ( لعلمه أنه ينفعهم ) ولا يؤثر فيهم ، ( فلما لم يوفقوا لذلك ) اللطف ( فكأنهم ختم على قلوبهم) لأن قطع اللطف مانع من دخول الإيمان كما أن الختم والطبع والأكنة والأقفال موانع من الدخول . ( الرابع ) وهو لبعض أصحاب عبد الواحد من المعتزلة (منعهم الله الإخلاص الموجب لقبول العمل فكانوا لذلك ) كمن يمنع دخول الإيمان قلبه بالختم عليه وسقوله : ( لعلمه أنه لا ينفعهم إلخ فيه دلالة على أن المعتزلة وإن أوجبوا اللطف على الله تعالى لكن لا يوجبونه إذا علم أنه لا ينفعهم.
The Sixth Observatory – The Third Goal: Matters Explicitly Stated in the Quran. [ The Third Goal: Matters Explicitly Stated in the Quran ] 187 (In investigation of matters explicitly stated in the Quran and upon which consensus is concluded, and they interpret them allegorically.) First: al-tab` (sealing): Allah Most High said: 'But Allah has sealed them for their disbelief' [al-Nisa', 155]; and al-khatm (stamping): 'Allah has stamped upon their hearts' [al-Baqarah, 7]; and al-akinnah (covers): 'And We have placed upon their hearts covers so that they do not understand it' [al-An'am, 25]; and similar things like al-aqfal (locks) in His statement Most High: 'Or are there locks upon their hearts?' [Muhammad, 24]. Ahl al-Haqq go to that they are expressions for the creation of misguidance in the hearts, because these things in language are real preventives, and they are named thus for being preventive, and the creation of misguidance in the hearts is preventive of guidance. So their naming by these names is correct, because the original principle is continuity until a preventive prevents, and the original is its absence; so whoever claims it needs clarification. And the Mu`tazila interpreted them in ways: First: (and it is for the early Mu`tazila): 'Allah has stamped upon their hearts' etc., i.e., He named them stamped upon, sealed upon, and made upon them covers and locks, and described them as such, just as He said: 'And they make the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females' [al-Zukhruf, 19], i.e., they named them so and described them as female, since they have no power for real making. Second: (and it is for al-Jubba'i and his son and those who followed them): And He named them – i.e., Allah, upon the hearts of the disbelievers – with marks and signs that the angels know, whereby the disbeliever is distinguished from the believer. This is because stamping and sealing in language is branding, and it is not impossible that Allah creates in the hearts of the wicked a mark that distinguishes them from the hearts of the righteous, and We make that mark evident to the angels, so they blame whoever is marked with it. And that is in a religious interest, because if the servant knows that when he disbelieves he is marked with a mark that realizes his blame and curse from the angels, that becomes a cause for his refraining from it. Third: (and it is for al-Ka`bi): Allah's prevention of them from the divine grace (al-lutf) that brings near to obedience and distances from disobedience, because He knows that it does not benefit them and does not affect them. So when they were not granted that grace, it is as if their hearts were stamped, because the cutting off of grace is a preventive from the entry of faith, just as stamping, sealing, covers, and locks are preventives from entry. Fourth: (and it is for some of the companions of 'Abd al-Wahid from the Mu`tazila): Allah prevented them from sincerity (al-ikhlas) which necessitates the acceptance of deeds, so they were therefore like one whose heart is prevented from the entry of faith by stamping. And his saying: 'because He knows that it does not benefit them' etc. contains an indication that the Mu`tazila, although they obligate grace (al-lutf) upon Allah Most High, do not obligate it when He knows that it does not benefit them.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage begins the third goal of the sixth observatory, which treats Quranic terms that Mu`tazila interpret allegorically. It first lists key terms: tab` (sealing), khatm (stamping), akinnah (covers), and aqfal (locks). The author states that Ahl al-Haqq (the orthodox) take these words literally as referring to the creation of misguidance (dalal) in the hearts. The reasoning is that these terms linguistically denote real preventive barriers, and misguidance prevents guidance, so the metaphorical extension is valid; the default is continuity until a preventive arises. Then the passage presents four Mu`tazilite interpretations: (1) The early Mu`tazila consider these as metaphorical descriptions (tasmiah) – God merely describes the hearts as stamped, just as He described the angels as female without real gender change. (2) Al-Jubba'i and his son interpret them as actual marks (simat) created by God in the hearts for angels to recognize disbelievers; this serves as a deterrent. (3) Al-Ka`bi interprets them as withholding divine grace (lutf) that would lead to obedience, because God knows it would not benefit them; the absence of grace is like a seal. (4) Some followers of 'Abd al-Wahid interpret them as withholding sincerity (ikhlas) which makes deeds acceptable. A concluding remark from the commentator notes that the Mu`tazila only obligate grace if beneficial.

Key Terms
الطبع (al-tab`)
الختم (al-khatm)
الأكنة (al-akinnah)
الأقفال (al-aqfal)
التسمية (al-tasmiah)
السمة (al-simah)
اللطف (al-lutf)
الإخلاص (al-ikhlas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu`tazila reject the Quranic terms entirely.
Correction: They accept the terms but interpret them allegorically to avoid attributing evil to God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i's interpretation, what does 'khatm' refer to?
A) A metaphorical description
B) A physical mark on the heart
C) Withholding of grace
D) Withholding of sincerity

Key Takeaway
The Mu`tazila offer four different allegorical interpretations to avoid attributing the creation of misguidance to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about these four interpretations, do you find yourself gravitating toward one of them? What does that choice reveal about your own understanding of God's justice and mercy? Are you willing to let that understanding be challenged by the plain text of the Quran?
?
Al-Ka'bi said God withholds grace from some because He knows it won't benefit them. Have you ever assumed that your own faith or progress is solely due to your own effort, forgetting that divine grace could be withdrawn? In what area of your life are you most prone to pride, thinking you deserve God's guidance?
?
The early Mu'tazila described God's actions as mere naming, not causing. Where in your life have you used similar logic to excuse yourself from responsibility—calling something a 'test' when it was really a consequence of your choices? How would your relationship with God change if you believed He is directly involved in every detail of your heart's state?

The Mu'tazila's First Intellectual Proof
The Mu'tazila present their first intellectual proof against Allah willing disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first objection against the Ash'ari view that Allah wills all things, which argues that commanding contrary to one's will is foolish.

Core Idea
If Allah wills disbelief but commands faith, that command would be foolish.

The Mu'tazila are known for their rationalist approach to theology. One of their key objections to the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah wills all things, including disbelief, is that it would make God's command of faith inconsistent with His will. This lesson examines that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'safah' (foolishness).
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument into premise (Allah wills disbelief and commands faith) and conclusion (commanding contrary to will is foolish).
This is a conditional argument: if X then Y.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is a challenge for the Ash'ari position. Think about how they might respond.
The response is not included here, but the objection is the starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أي المعتزلة على أنه تعالى لا يزيد الكفر والمعاصي بوجوه عقلية : الأول : لو كان تعالى مريداً لكفر الكافر وقد أمره بالإيمان فالأمر بخلاف مايريده يعد عند العقلاء سفيهاً
They — the Mu'tazila — argued that He, exalted, does not will disbelief and acts of disobedience with intellectual proofs: The first: If He, exalted, were willing for the disbelief of the disbeliever and has commanded him with faith, then commanding contrary to what one wills is considered foolish by the rational people.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the first intellectual proof of the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah wills all things, including disbelief and sins. The argument is that if Allah wills the disbelief of a disbeliever, then His commanding him to believe would be commanding the opposite of what He wills, which is considered foolish (safah) by rational people. This is a classic Mu'tazilite objection based on the principle that a wise agent does not command what he does not want to happen. The response to this objection is not provided in the current text; it is likely addressed elsewhere in the work.

Key Terms
سفه (safah)
أمر (amr)
معتزلة (Muʿtazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila objection is unanswerable.
Correction: Ash'ari theologians typically respond by distinguishing between God's will and His command, arguing that they are not contradictory because the command serves a different purpose (e.g., establishing accountability).

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the consequence if Allah wills disbelief and commands faith?
A) It would be just.
B) It would be foolish.
C) It would be impossible.
D) It would be merciful.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first rational proof argues that a command contradicting God's will would be an act of foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever tell yourself 'Allah willed it' to lessen your guilt? The Mu'tazila argue that commanding contrary to will is foolish. If you truly believed Allah did not will your sin, how would that change your repentance?
?
Consider a moment you commanded someone to do something while secretly wanting them to fail. The Mu'tazila call that foolish. Do you see any hypocrisy in your own commands that Allah must be free from?
?
If Allah does not will disbelief (as this proof claims), then your disbelief is solely your own doing. How does that accountability shape your attitude towards faith today?

The Philosophers' Classification of Good and Evil
Presentation of the philosophers' classification and definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of existents into pure good and predominantly good, and their definition of evil as non-existence.

Core Idea
Evil, for the philosophers, is not a real entity but an absence of perfection.

The philosophers of the Islamic tradition sought to reconcile the existence of evil with divine wisdom by arguing that evil is not a positive reality. In this passage, the author presents their systematic classification of all existents into two categories: pure good and predominantly good. This sets the stage for understanding their broader metaphysical framework and the subsequent critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the passage and note the two divisions: pure good (with no evil) and predominantly good (where good outweighs evil). The philosophers include the intellects and spheres in the first, and the sublunar world in the second.
Pure evil and that in which evil predominates are said to have no existence.
2
Understand the definition of evil
Evil in itself is defined as non-existence (ʿadam) of a perfection. Notice that when something existent (like cold) is called evil, it is only because it leads to that non-existence.
3
See the application to intellects and spheres
Intellects are pure goods because they possess all perfections actually. For the spheres, the author notes a problem: they have some perfections but lack others, making their classification questionable.
Al-Ṭūsī omitted the spheres from his commentary on al-Ishārāt, suggesting doubt.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this classification implies about God's creation. Does it adequately account for the reality of evil in the world? This will be critiqued in the next lesson.
The philosophers assume this definition without providing a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة : ( للمقصد الرابع ( في نقل رأي في القضاء والقدر قالوا الموجود إما خير محض لا شر فيه أصلاً كالعقول والأفلاك، وإما الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم الواقع تحت كرة القمر فإن المرض مثلاً وإن كان كثيراً فالصحة أكثر منه، وكذلك الألم كثير واللذة أكثر منه فالموجود عندهم منحصر في هذين القسمين، وأما ما يكون شراً محضاً أو كان الشر فيه غالباً أو مساوياً فليس شيء منها موجوداً. الشر بالذات عندهم عدم شيء من حيث هو غير مؤثر أعني فقدان كل كمال الشيء وإذا أطلق على أمر موجود مانع عن الكمال كالبرد المفسد للثمار يمنعها عن وصولها إلى كمالها كان ذلك باعتبار كونه مؤدياً إلى ذلك العدم، وهذا مقرر عندهم وإن لم يقم عليه برهان كما أشار إليه الشارح في حواشي التجريد وبهذا يظهر كون العقول خيرات محضة، إذ جميع كمالاتها حاصلة لها بالفعل عندهم وأما كون الأفلاك كذلك ففيه نظر لأن لها كمالات مؤثرة عندها مفقودة لها كما علم من قولهم في سبب حركاتها، ولذا لم يذكرها الطوسي في شرح الإشارات اللهم إلا أن يراد بالشر هاهنا معنى آخر، ثم إن ظاهر تقريرهم في عالم الموجودات لا في الجزئيات، ولذا أجاب الشيخ في الإشارات عن الاعتراض بأن الغالب على الإنسان بحسب القوة العقلية الجهل وبحسب القوة الشهوانية والغضبية طاعة الشهوة والغضب وهي شرور لأنها أسباب الشقاوة والعقاب فيكون الشر غالبا في نوع الإنسان بأن الجهل المركب نادر بالقياس إلى البسيط وقد انضم البسيط إلى الطرف الأفضل بمعنى الأكمل في العلم فلا جرم يكون الغلبة لأهل النجاة وكذا الكلام في التوسط بين حسن الخلق وقبحه، واعترض عليه بأن الجهل البسيط أيضاً شر لأنه فقدان الإنسان كماله العلمي فلما كان هو العام الفاشي يكون الشر أكثر وأجيب بأن الكلام في الموجود الذي هو الشر والجهل البسيط ليس بموجود والإنسان ليس بشرير بالإضافة إليه لأنه ليس سببا والحق أن ما ذكره في هذا المحل من الإشارات فسادات إذ لا يلائم أصول أهل السنة.
Conclusion: (of the fourth objective) concerning transmitting the opinion about al-qada' and al-qadar. They said: The existent is either pure good having no evil at all, like the intellects and the spheres; or good predominates over it, as in this world under the sphere of the moon — for example, sickness, though abundant, health is more abundant; likewise, pain is abundant, pleasure is more abundant. So existent according to them is confined to these two divisions. As for that which is pure evil, or in which evil predominates or is equal, no part of it exists. Evil in itself according to them is the non-existence of something insofar as it is non-effective, i.e., the loss of every perfection of a thing. When it is said of an existent thing that prevents perfection, like the cold that spoils fruits and prevents them from reaching their perfection, that is because it leads to that non-existence. This is established according to them even though no proof has been given for it, as the commentator indicated in his glosses on al-Tajrid. By this it becomes clear that the intellects are pure goods, since all their perfections are actually possessed by them according to them. As for the spheres being likewise, there is consideration, because they have [certain] perfections that are effective [elsewhere] and are lacking in them, as is known from their statement about the cause of their movements. Therefore, al-Tusi did not mention them in his commentary on al-Isharat, unless evil is meant here in another sense. Then their apparent discussion concerns the world of existents, not particulars. Hence, al-Shaykh in al-Isharat answered the objection that the predominant in man regarding the rational faculty is ignorance, and regarding the appetitive and irascible faculties is obedience to desire and anger, and these are evils because they are causes of misery and punishment, so evil predominates in the human species — [he answered] by saying that compound ignorance is rare compared to simple ignorance, and simple ignorance has joined the better side, i.e., the more perfect in knowledge, so without doubt the majority are the people of salvation. Similarly, the middle ground between good character and its opposite. It was objected against him that simple ignorance is also evil because it is man's loss of his cognitive perfection, and since it is widespread and common, evil is greater. The answer was that the discussion concerns the existent which is evil, and simple ignorance is not existent, and man is not evil in relation to it because it is not a cause. The truth is that what he mentioned in this place from al-Isharat is invalid, for it does not harmonize with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critique of the philosophers' classification of existents regarding good and evil. The author begins by stating the philosophers' view: existents are divided into two categories — (1) pure good with no evil at all, such as the intellects and the spheres, and (2) that in which good predominates, like the sublunar world. They assert that pure evil, or that in which evil predominates or is equal, does not exist. The commentator then explains the philosophers' definition of evil in itself: it is non-existence (absence of a perfection). When an existent thing is called evil because it hinders perfection, it is only in a secondary sense as a cause of that non-existence. This definition, though unproven, is assumed by them. Based on this, the intellects are pure goods as they possess all perfections. The status of the spheres is questionable because they lack some perfections. The commentator notes that al-Tusi omitted them from his commentary on al-Isharat. Next, the philosopher's response to an objection about the predominance of evil in humans is discussed: ignorance (jahl) in human beings is addressed by distinguishing simple and compound ignorance, and the answer claims that simple ignorance is not existent. However, the commentator rejects this as incompatible with Sunni principles, showing that the philosophers' treatment is unsatisfactory.

Key Terms
خير محض
شر محض
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' definition of evil as non-existence makes evil completely unreal and irrelevant.
Correction: According to the philosophers, evil does exist as a privation (absence of perfection), and things that cause that privation are considered evil derivatively. Evil is not a positive entity but it is still real as a lack.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers as presented in this passage, what is evil in itself?
A) A positive entity created by God
B) The non-existence of a perfection
C) A necessary attribute of matter
D) An illusion with no basis in reality

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define evil as non-existence and divide existents into pure good and predominantly good, with pure evil having no existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If evil is merely an absence of perfection in the philosophers' view, what does that imply about your own moral failings? Are they simply absences, or do they carry real guilt? How does this perspective affect your sense of accountability before Allah?
?
The philosophers claim that in this world good predominates over evil (health outweighs sickness, pleasure outweighs pain). Do you truly believe that good outweighs evil in your own life? Where do you see evidence of Allah's goodness prevailing? Are you living with that conviction, or do you let the presence of evil overshadow your gratitude?
?
The passage notes that the philosophers' definition of evil lacks proof. What is your ultimate authority on good and evil? When you face suffering, do you tend to view it as a mere absence of perfection, or do you recognize it as something created by Allah with wisdom? How might your response to trials change if you adopted the correct Islamic view that Allah creates both good and evil for a purpose?

Critique of the Philosophers' View on Evil
Critique of the philosophers' response to the problem of evil in humans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that evil predominates in humans, the philosophers' response distinguishing simple and compound ignorance, and the author's critique that this response is inconsistent with Sunni theology.

Core Idea
The philosophers' claim that simple ignorance is not existent fails to address the reality of evil in human beings.

Having presented the philosophers' classification, the passage now confronts a pressing objection: if evil is predominant in human beings due to ignorance and base desires, then their system seems flawed. The philosopher al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) offers a response based on the distinction between simple and compound ignorance. However, the author ultimately rejects this response as incompatible with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection states that in humans, ignorance (jahl) predominates in the rational faculty, and obedience to desire and anger in the other faculties. These are evils that cause misery and punishment, so evil predominates in the human species.
This challenges the philosophers' claim that evil never predominates.
2
See the philosopher's response
The philosopher responds that compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) is rare, while simple ignorance (jahl basīṭ) is widespread but has joined the better side (the more knowledgeable). Therefore, the majority are people of salvation.
3
Examine the counter-objection and answer
The counter-objection says simple ignorance is also evil because it is a loss of cognitive perfection, and since it is common, evil is greater. The answer insists that simple ignorance is not an existent thing, so it does not count as a positive evil.
The philosophers argue that the discussion concerns existent evil.
4
Learn the author's critique
The author declares that the philosopher's entire response is invalid (fasād) because it does not harmonize with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, implying that evil is real and has existential effects.
This shows the incompatibility between the philosophers' view and Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة : ( للمقصد الرابع ( في نقل رأي في القضاء والقدر قالوا الموجود إما خير محض لا شر فيه أصلاً كالعقول والأفلاك، وإما الخير غالب عليه كما في هذا العالم الواقع تحت كرة القمر فإن المرض مثلاً وإن كان كثيراً فالصحة أكثر منه، وكذلك الألم كثير واللذة أكثر منه فالموجود عندهم منحصر في هذين القسمين، وأما ما يكون شراً محضاً أو كان الشر فيه غالباً أو مساوياً فليس شيء منها موجوداً. الشر بالذات عندهم عدم شيء من حيث هو غير مؤثر أعني فقدان كل كمال الشيء وإذا أطلق على أمر موجود مانع عن الكمال كالبرد المفسد للثمار يمنعها عن وصولها إلى كمالها كان ذلك باعتبار كونه مؤدياً إلى ذلك العدم، وهذا مقرر عندهم وإن لم يقم عليه برهان كما أشار إليه الشارح في حواشي التجريد وبهذا يظهر كون العقول خيرات محضة، إذ جميع كمالاتها حاصلة لها بالفعل عندهم وأما كون الأفلاك كذلك ففيه نظر لأن لها كمالات مؤثرة عندها مفقودة لها كما علم من قولهم في سبب حركاتها، ولذا لم يذكرها الطوسي في شرح الإشارات اللهم إلا أن يراد بالشر هاهنا معنى آخر، ثم إن ظاهر تقريرهم في عالم الموجودات لا في الجزئيات، ولذا أجاب الشيخ في الإشارات عن الاعتراض بأن الغالب على الإنسان بحسب القوة العقلية الجهل وبحسب القوة الشهوانية والغضبية طاعة الشهوة والغضب وهي شرور لأنها أسباب الشقاوة والعقاب فيكون الشر غالبا في نوع الإنسان بأن الجهل المركب نادر بالقياس إلى البسيط وقد انضم البسيط إلى الطرف الأفضل بمعنى الأكمل في العلم فلا جرم يكون الغلبة لأهل النجاة وكذا الكلام في التوسط بين حسن الخلق وقبحه، واعترض عليه بأن الجهل البسيط أيضاً شر لأنه فقدان الإنسان كماله العلمي فلما كان هو العام الفاشي يكون الشر أكثر وأجيب بأن الكلام في الموجود الذي هو الشر والجهل البسيط ليس بموجود والإنسان ليس بشرير بالإضافة إليه لأنه ليس سببا والحق أن ما ذكره في هذا المحل من الإشارات فسادات إذ لا يلائم أصول أهل السنة.
Conclusion: (of the fourth objective) concerning transmitting the opinion about al-qada' and al-qadar. They said: The existent is either pure good having no evil at all, like the intellects and the spheres; or good predominates over it, as in this world under the sphere of the moon — for example, sickness, though abundant, health is more abundant; likewise, pain is abundant, pleasure is more abundant. So existent according to them is confined to these two divisions. As for that which is pure evil, or in which evil predominates or is equal, no part of it exists. Evil in itself according to them is the non-existence of something insofar as it is non-effective, i.e., the loss of every perfection of a thing. When it is said of an existent thing that prevents perfection, like the cold that spoils fruits and prevents them from reaching their perfection, that is because it leads to that non-existence. This is established according to them even though no proof has been given for it, as the commentator indicated in his glosses on al-Tajrid. By this it becomes clear that the intellects are pure goods, since all their perfections are actually possessed by them according to them. As for the spheres being likewise, there is consideration, because they have [certain] perfections that are effective [elsewhere] and are lacking in them, as is known from their statement about the cause of their movements. Therefore, al-Tusi did not mention them in his commentary on al-Isharat, unless evil is meant here in another sense. Then their apparent discussion concerns the world of existents, not particulars. Hence, al-Shaykh in al-Isharat answered the objection that the predominant in man regarding the rational faculty is ignorance, and regarding the appetitive and irascible faculties is obedience to desire and anger, and these are evils because they are causes of misery and punishment, so evil predominates in the human species — [he answered] by saying that compound ignorance is rare compared to simple ignorance, and simple ignorance has joined the better side, i.e., the more perfect in knowledge, so without doubt the majority are the people of salvation. Similarly, the middle ground between good character and its opposite. It was objected against him that simple ignorance is also evil because it is man's loss of his cognitive perfection, and since it is widespread and common, evil is greater. The answer was that the discussion concerns the existent which is evil, and simple ignorance is not existent, and man is not evil in relation to it because it is not a cause. The truth is that what he mentioned in this place from al-Isharat is invalid, for it does not harmonize with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critique of the philosophers' classification of existents regarding good and evil. The author begins by stating the philosophers' view: existents are divided into two categories — (1) pure good with no evil at all, such as the intellects and the spheres, and (2) that in which good predominates, like the sublunar world. They assert that pure evil, or that in which evil predominates or is equal, does not exist. The commentator then explains the philosophers' definition of evil in itself: it is non-existence (absence of a perfection). When an existent thing is called evil because it hinders perfection, it is only in a secondary sense as a cause of that non-existence. This definition, though unproven, is assumed by them. Based on this, the intellects are pure goods as they possess all perfections. The status of the spheres is questionable because they lack some perfections. The commentator notes that al-Tusi omitted them from his commentary on al-Isharat. Next, the philosopher's response to an objection about the predominance of evil in humans is discussed: ignorance (jahl) in human beings is addressed by distinguishing simple and compound ignorance, and the answer claims that simple ignorance is not existent. However, the commentator rejects this as incompatible with Sunni principles, showing that the philosophers' treatment is unsatisfactory.

Key Terms
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
شر بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' distinction between simple and compound ignorance fully resolves the problem of evil in humans.
Correction: The author argues that this response is invalid because it does not align with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna, which affirm that evil has real existential effects.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' response in this passage, why is simple ignorance not considered an evil?
A) Because it is rare
B) Because it is not existent
C) Because it leads to knowledge
D) Because it is a positive quality

Key Takeaway
The Sunni author rejects the philosophers' attempt to minimize evil by distinguishing types of ignorance, insisting that their response does not align with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a state of ignorance about a religious matter, do you consider that ignorance a real evil that you are responsible to rectify, or do you dismiss it as a mere absence of knowledge? How does your response reflect the principles of Ahl al-Sunna or the philosophers' view?
?
Your desires and anger often lead you to sin—do you treat these as trivial flaws or as genuine evils that cause misery? What change will you make today to truly combat the evil within you?
?
The philosophers claimed that simple ignorance is not an existent evil and thus the majority are saved. Does this excuse comfort you when you neglect learning? Or does the Sunni position—that ignorance is a real deficiency—drive you to seek knowledge? What is one specific step you will take this week to overcome a particular ignorance in your faith?

Mu'tazila Denial of Qada and Qadr for Human Actions
The author explains why the Mu'tazila deny qada and qadr for voluntary human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila deny qada and qadr for voluntary human actions.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny divine decrees for human acts because they attribute creation to humans.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazila position on qada and qadr. The passage explains the two aspects of their denial: rejecting qadr by attributing actions to humans, and rejecting qada by denying direct divine will for human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the two parts: 'أما إنكارهم القدر' and 'أما إنكارهم القضاء'.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the structure.
2
Understand the denial of qadr
The author explains that the Mu'tazila deny qadr because they ascribe creation of acts to themselves. Consider why this logically leads to denial of divine determination.
Qadr here refers to divine predestination of acts.
3
Understand the denial of qada
The denial of qada has two possible reasons: either they deny direct divine will for acts (saying God wills humans to act by their own power) or they deny God's will for evil and permissible acts. Compare these.
Qada refers to divine decree or will.
4
Reflect on the overall position
Put together how these denials form the Mu'tazila view on human free will versus divine decree.
Contrast with the Ash'ari position on acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والمعتزلة ينكرون القضاء والقدر إلخ) أما إنكارهم القدر فظاهر لإسنادهم خلق أفعال العباد إليهم ، وأما إنكارهم القضاء فيها فإما لأنهم لم يقولوا بإرادة الله تعالى لأفعالهم بالذات بل قالوا: إنه تعالى أراد أن يفعلوها بقدرهم ورغباتهم وإما لأنهم أنكروا الإرادة في فعل السيئة والمباح .
His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila deny al-qaḍā' and al-qadr, etc.' As for their denial of al-qadr, it is apparent because they attribute the creation of the actions of servants to themselves. As for their denial of al-qaḍā' therein, it is either because they did not assert God's will for their actions in themselves, but rather said: He, exalted, willed that they do them by their own power and desires, or because they denied [God's] will in the case of evil and permissible acts.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila position on qada and qadr. The author first notes that their denial of qadr is obvious: they attribute the creation of human actions to humans themselves, so they reject that God determines or creates those acts. As for their denial of qada (divine decree), it is twofold. First, they do not hold that God wills human acts directly (in themselves); rather, He wills that humans do them through their own power and inclinations. Second, they deny that God wills evil and permissible acts at all (i.e., He does not will sin or neutral acts). Thus, they reject both qada and qadr for voluntary acts.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
القضاء
القدر
خلق أفعال العباد
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila reject qada and qadr entirely, including for natural events.
Correction: They only reject them for voluntary human acts while affirming divine knowledge and predestination of other things.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila deny al-qadr according to the passage?
A) Because they believe God does not know human acts.
B) Because they attribute the creation of human acts to humans themselves.
C) Because they deny that God has any will.
D) Because they think qadr only applies to natural events.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila deny qadr by attributing human acts to human agency, and deny qada by rejecting direct divine will for voluntary actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself taking credit for your good deeds while unconsciously sidelining God's will and creation? How does the Mu'tazila error expose a similar pride in your own heart?
?
When you sin, do you ever think that God had no part in that act—that He didn't will it? How does affirming that God decrees everything, even what you don't understand, change the way you repent and rely on Him?

Mu'tazilite Internal Disagreements on the Basis of Good and Evil
Enumerates four Mu'tazilite views on ḥusn and qubḥ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main Mu'tazilite positions on whether ḥusn and qubḥ of acts are due to essence, a real attribute, or relational considerations.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites disagreed deeply on the very nature of moral value.

The Mu'tazilite school is often associated with rationalist ethics, but within the school there was significant disagreement on the foundational question: what makes an act good or evil? This passage from al-Ījī’s Mawāqif presents four distinct positions held by different Mu'tazilite scholars, ranging from essence-based to purely relational accounts. Understanding these internal debates is essential for grasping the full spectrum of kalām ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic text once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the sequence of positions as they appear.
Notice the structure: each position is introduced by a verb like 'ذهب' (went to) or 'فقالوا' (so they said).
2
Identify the four positions
List each position and the scholar or group associated with it: (1) earliest Mu'tazila: essence, (2) some later early Mu'tazila: real attribute for both, (3) Abu l-Husayn: real attribute only for evil, (4) al-Jubba'i: relational considerations.
The text uses 'الأوائل' (the earliest), 'بعض من يعدم من المتقدمين' (some later early), 'أبو الحسين' (Abu l-Husayn), and 'الجبائي' (al-Jubba'i).
3
Understand the arguments and examples
Note how each group supports its view. The text provides an example: slapping an orphan can be good (discipline) or evil (oppression), illustrating al-Jubba'i's relational view.
The commentator adds a clarification about 'after the capacity for being described by it'—this excludes acts of the incapable or compelled.
4
Reflect on the significance of the disagreement
Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology? The basis of moral value affects how we know what God commands and how we interpret revelation.
This disagreement shows that even rationalist theologians did not agree on a single moral epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم اختلفوا فذهب الأوائل منهم إلى أن حسن الأفعال وقبحها لذواتها لا لصفات فيها تقتضيهما وذهب بعض من يعدم من المتقدمين ( إلى إثبات صفة ) حقيقة ( توجب ذلك مطلقاً ) أي في الحسن والقبح جميعاً فقالوا: ليس حسن الفعل أو قبحه لذاته كما ذهب إليه من تقدمنا من أصحابنا بل لما فيه من صفة موجبة لأحدهما (و) ذهب ( أبو الحسين من متأخريهم إلى إثبات صفة في القبيح) مقتضية لقبحه ( دون الحسن إذ لا حاجة به إلى صفة محسنة له بل يكفيه انتفاء الصفة المقبحة (و) ذهب ( الجبائي إلى نفيه ) أي نفي الوصف الحقيقي ( فيهما مطلقاً ) فقال : ليس حسن الأفعال وقبحها لصفات حقيقية فيها بل لوجوه اعتبارية وأوصاف إضافية تختلف بحسب الاعتبار كما في لطمة اليتيم تأديبا وظلماً . قوله : ( انتفاء الصفة المقبحة ) أي بعد القابلية للاتصاف بها فخرج فعل العاجز والملجأ
Then they differed. The earliest among them held that the goodness and evilness of acts are due to their essences, not due to attributes in them that necessitate those [judgments]. And some of those who came after of the earlier ones went to affirming a real attribute that necessitates that absolutely, i.e., in both good and evil. So they said: The good or evil of an act is not due to its essence, as those before us among our companions held, but rather due to what is in it of an attribute necessitating one of them. And Abū l-Ḥusayn of their late ones went to affirming an attribute in the evil that necessitates its evilness, but not in the good, because the good has no need for a good-making attribute; rather, the absence of the evil-making attribute suffices for it. And al-Jubbāʾī went to its negation, i.e., the negation of the real attribute in both absolutely. So he said: The goodness and evilness of acts are not due to real attributes in them, but rather due to relational considerations and additional descriptions that vary according to consideration, like slapping the orphan as discipline and as oppression. His saying: (The absence of the evil-making attribute) i.e., after the capacity for being described by it, so the act of the incapable and the compelled is excluded.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage outlines the internal disagreements among Muʿtazilī scholars regarding the basis of ḥusn and qubḥ. Three main positions are presented: (1) The earliest Muʿtazila held that good and evil are due to the essences of acts themselves (dhātiyya), not due to any additional attribute. (2) Some later early Muʿtazila (probably referring to a group after the early ones) affirmed a real attribute that necessitates goodness or evil absolutely, meaning both good and evil are caused by a real quality in the act. (3) Abū l-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, a later Muʿtazilī, affirmed an attribute only in evil acts, but not in good acts; for good, the absence of an evil-making attribute suffices. (4) Al-Jubbāʾī denied any real attributes altogether, asserting that good and evil are due to relational considerations (wujūh iʿtibāriyya) and additional descriptions that vary according to perspective—for example, slapping an orphan can be good (as discipline) or evil (as oppression) depending on intention. The commentator adds that when al-Jubbāʾī speaks of the absence of an evil-making attribute as sufficient for good, he means after the act is capable of being described by it, thereby excluding the acts of the incapable (incapacitated person) and the compelled (who has no choice), which are not subject to moral judgment.

Key Terms
ذات
صفة حقيقية
وجه اعتباري
وصف إضافي
أبو الحسين
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agreed that moral qualities are intrinsic to acts.
Correction: In fact, they had significant internal disagreements, with some denying real attributes and others affirming them only for evil.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubbāʾī's position in this passage, what is the basis for an act being good or evil?
A) A real attribute inherent in the act.
B) The essence of the act itself.
C) Relational considerations and additional descriptions that vary according to perspective.
D) The command of the Lawgiver.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed among themselves on whether good and evil are intrinsic to acts, caused by a real attribute, or merely relational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge an action as good or evil, do you base it on the act itself, your intention, or something else? The Mu'tazilites debated this. How would your answer change if you knew that even the earliest among them disagreed? Are you comfortable basing your moral judgments on a foundation that even scholars disputed?
?
Consider an act you recently performed that you consider good. What makes it good? Is it because you intended it, or because it has some quality in itself? The passage shows that Mu'tazilites gave different answers. Does your personal conviction stand on solid ground, or is it shaped by convenience?
?
Al-Jubbāʾī used the example of slapping an orphan – same act, different moral values depending on context. Think of a time you were quick to condemn someone. Could there be a perspective you missed that would change the moral assessment? Will you now examine your own judgments more carefully?

Four Mu'tazila Views on the Basis of Good and Evil
Al-Jurjani enumerates four Mu'tazila opinions on the basis of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct Mu'tazila positions on whether goodness and badness are essential to actions or derive from attributes.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila disagreed fundamentally on the metaphysical basis of moral qualities.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary presents the internal diversity within the Mu'tazila school on a core theological issue: what makes an action good or evil? While the Mu'tazila are known for affirming rational good and evil, they differed sharply on whether this quality resides in the action's essence, in a real attribute, or is purely relative. Understanding these positions illuminates the broader kalam debate on moral ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the four distinct opinions introduced by 'ذهب'.
The passage is a classification; each 'ذهب' signals a new position.
2
Identify the four positions
List the four groups: early Mu'tazila, some later early scholars, Abu al-Husayn, and al-Jubba'i. Understand each view in your own words.
3
Compare the views
Note the spectrum from essentialism (early) to pure relativism (Jubba'i). Ask why each position developed and how they differ.
4
Reflect on significance
This shows that 'Mu'tazila' is not monolithic; their internal debates are crucial for understanding later Ash'ari critiques.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم اختلفوا فذهب الأوائل منهم إلى أن حسن الأفعال وقبحها لذواتها لا لصفات فيها تقتضيهما وذهب بعض من بعدهم من المتقدمين إلى إثبات صفة حقيقة توجب ذلك مطلقاً أي في الحسن والقبح جميعاً فقالوا: ليس حسن الفعل أو قبحه لذاته كما ذهب إليه من تقدمنا من أصحابنا بل لما فيه من صفة موجبة لأحدهما وذهب أبو الحسين من متأخريهم إلى إثبات صفة في القبيح مقتضية لقبحه دون الحسن إذ لا حاجة به إلى صفة محسنة له بل يكفيه انتفاء الصفة المقبحة وذهب الجبائي إلى نفيه أي نفي الوصف الحقيقي فيهما مطلقاً فقال : ليس حسن الأفعال وقبحها لصفات حقيقية فيها بل لوجوه اعتبارية وأوصاف إضافية تختلف بحسب الاعتبار كما في لطمة اليتيم تأديبا وظلما
Then they disagreed. The early ones among them held that the goodness and badness of actions are for their essences, not for attributes in them that necessitate them. And some of those after them from the earlier scholars held the affirmation of a real attribute that necessitates that absolutely — that is, in both goodness and badness. They said: The goodness or badness of an action is not for its essence, as our predecessors from our companions held, but because of an attribute in it that necessitates one of them. And Abu al-Husayn from their later scholars held the affirmation of an attribute in the bad that necessitates its badness, not in the good, since the good has no need of an attribute that makes it good; rather, the absence of the attribute of badness suffices for it. And al-Jubba'i held the negation of that — that is, the negation of a real attribute in both absolutely. He said: The goodness and badness of actions are not due to real attributes in them, but due to relative aspects and additional descriptions that vary according to consideration, as in the slap of the orphan, whether for discipline or injustice.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies the four main Mu'tazila positions on the ontological basis of moral qualities. (1) The earliest Mu'tazila held that goodness and badness belong to the very essence (dhat) of actions, not to any additional attribute. (2) Some later early scholars (like then) affirmed a real, existent attribute that causes goodness or badness in all cases. (3) Abu al-Husayn al-Basri from the later scholars affirmed a real attribute only for badness, not for goodness, because for goodness the mere absence of the badness attribute suffices. (4) Al-Jubba'i denied any real attribute entirely, arguing that goodness and badness are purely relative (i'tibari) and vary according to perspective, like slapping an orphan which can be either discipline (good) or injustice (bad). This shows significant internal diversity among the Mu'tazila on this fundamental issue.

Key Terms
الذات
الصفة الحقيقية
الوجه الاعتباري
الأوائل
المتقدمون
أبو الحسين
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila hold that good and evil are rational and inherent in actions in the same way.
Correction: While all Mu'tazila affirm rational knowledge of good and evil, they fundamentally disagree on whether the moral quality is essential, caused by a real attribute, or purely relative.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, why does he posit a real attribute for evil but not for good?
A) Because good is easier to define
B) Because good does not need an attribute; the absence of the evil attribute suffices
C) Because he follows the early Mu'tazila
D) Because he denies real attributes entirely

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila school contained significant internal disagreement on the metaphysical basis of moral qualities, ranging from essentialism to relativism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call an action 'good' or 'evil,' do you base that judgment on the action's inherent nature, its consequences, or something else? Which of the four Mu'tazila views most closely matches your own reasoning, and what does that reveal about how you relate to Allah's commands?
?
If you were to adopt al-Jubba'i's view that goodness and badness are relative, how would that affect your confidence in moral absolutes? Would it change how you justify your beliefs to others?
?
The early Mu'tazila held that goodness is in the essence of the action itself. Think of one action you believe is always evil. Is it possible for that action to ever become good under any circumstance? If not, are you an essentialist? If yes, does that mean you lean toward the relativist view? Be honest.

The Mu'tazili Claim of al-Aslah and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the Mu'tazili claim and a simple counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili principle of al-Aslah and the simple refutation based on the poor unbeliever.

Core Idea
If God must do what is most beneficial, why does He create a poor unbeliever who suffers in both worlds?

The Mu'tazila held that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for His servants. But a simple objection shows that this principle cannot be consistently applied. In this lesson, we examine the argument and its first refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the concise style.
The passage is a single argument with a counterexample.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claim
The claim is that al-aslah (the most beneficial) is obligatory for the servant in this world.
3
Understand the objection
The objection presents a concrete case: a poor unbeliever who suffers in both worlds. For him, non-existence would be better, yet he exists.
This shows God did not do what was most beneficial.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that al-aslah is not obligatory upon God.
This refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ( من الأمور الواجبة عندهم ( الأصلح للعبد في الدنيا فيقال : ) لهم ( الأصلح للكافر الفقير المعذب في الدنيا والآخرة أن لا يخلق ) مع أنه مخلوق فلم يراع في حقه ما كان أصلح له فلا يكون الأصلح واجباً عليه تعالى (حكاية) شريفة ( تنحى بالقلع على هذه القاعدة ) لقائلة بوجوب الأصلح على الله سبحانه وتعالى ( قال الأشعري لأستاذه أبي علي الجبائي : ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة عاش أحدهم الطاعة وأحدهم في المعصية ومات أحدهم صغيرا ؟ فقال : يثاب الأول بالجنة ويعاقب الثاني بالنار والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب . قال ( الأشعري : ( فإن قال الثالث : يا رب لو عمرتني فأصلح فأدخل الجنة) كما دخلها أخي المؤمن. (قال) الجبائي : ) يقول الرب : كنت أعلم أنك لو عمرت لفسقت وأفسدت فدخلت النار . قال : فيقول الثاني : يا رب لم لم تمتني صغيراً لئلا أذنب فلا أدخل النار كما أمت أخي؟ فبهت ( الجبائي ( فترك الأشعري مذهبه إِلى المذهب الحق) الذي كان عليه السلف الصالح ( وكان ) هذا ( أول ما خالف فيه ) الأشعري (المعتزلة) ثم اشتغل بهدم قواعدهم وتشييد مباني الحق بعون الله وحسن توفيقه . قوله: (تنحى بالقلع أي يقبل يقال : انحى عليه بالسوط أي أقبل به عليه والمعنى أنها تقلعه بإقبال وتوجه تام فهو أبلغ من القلع. قوله : ( والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب ) وإن كان يدخل الجنة عند عامة المعتزلة القائلين بأن أطفال المشركين خدم أهل الجنة فهم في الجنة ولا ثواب لهم إذ الثواب عندهم منفعة دائمة خالصة مقرونة بالتعظيم والإكرام، والقيد الأخير منتف في حقهم، ثم دخولهم الجنة بلا ثواب لا ينافي كونها دار ثواب لأن معناه أن الثواب لا يكون إلا فيها لا أن كل من دخلها يثاب . قوله : (يا رب لو عمرتني إلخ لو هذه للتمني وهي عند بعض النحاة قسم برأسها لا يحتاج إلى الجواب ولكن قد يؤتى لها بجواب منصوب كما فيما نحن فيه . وقيل : هي لو الشرطية التي أشربت معنى التمني وقيل : هي لو المصدرية أنيبت عن فعل التمني، والمراد بالدخول في قوله : فادخل الجنة، الدخول المقرون بالثواب، إذ الدخول بدونه حاصل لأطفال المشركين عندهم
The fourth: Among the obligatory matters according to them is [the principle of] what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for the servant in this world. So it is said to them: The most beneficial for the unbeliever, the poor, who is tormented in this world and the Hereafter, is that he not be created, whereas he is created; so what was most beneficial for him was not observed. Thus, al-aslah is not obligatory upon Him, exalted. There is a noble anecdote that attacks this principle with uprooting—i.e., it uproots it—of those who say that al-aslah is obligatory upon God, glorified and exalted. Al-Ash'ari said to his teacher Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i: What do you say about three brothers: one lived in obedience, one in disobedience, and one died as a small child? He said: The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari said: Then if the third says: O Lord, if only You had let me live so that I could have done good and entered Paradise, as my believing brother entered it? Al-Jubba'i said: The Lord says: I knew that if you had lived, you would have sinned and caused corruption, and thus entered Hellfire. He said: Then the second says: O Lord, why did You not let me die as a child so that I would not have sinned and thus not enter Hellfire, as You let my brother die? So al-Jubba'i was confounded, and al-Ash'ari abandoned his school for the true school, which was the way of the righteous predecessors (al-salaf al-salih). And this was the first matter in which al-Ash'ari opposed the Mu'tazila; then he occupied himself with demolishing their principles and building up the foundations of truth with God's help and good success. The commentator says: (Attacks with uprooting) means he advances upon it; it is said: 'he advanced upon him with the whip,' meaning he came at him with it, and the meaning is that it uproots it with complete coming and direction, so it is more intensive than mere uprooting. The commentator says: (And the third is neither rewarded nor punished) although he enters Paradise according to most Mu'tazila, who say that the children of polytheists are servants of the people of Paradise, so they are in Paradise but have no reward, for reward according to them is a permanent, pure benefit accompanied by veneration and honor, and the last restriction is absent in their case; then their entering Paradise without reward does not contradict it being the abode of reward, because its meaning is that reward occurs only in it, not that everyone who enters it is rewarded. The commentator says: (O Lord, if only You had let me live, etc.) 'law' here is for wish (tamanni), and according to some grammarians it is a category by itself that does not require a response, but sometimes a response in the accusative is given, as in this case. And it is said it is conditional 'law' imbued with the meaning of wish, and it is said it is 'law' of source (masdariyya) substituted for the verb of wish. What is meant by 'entering' in his saying 'so that I enter Paradise' is the entrance accompanied by reward, since entrance without it is attained for children of polytheists according to them.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazili argument concerning the obligation of al-aslah (the most beneficial) for the servant in this world, followed by the famous anecdote of al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i that refutes it. The argument is that God must do what is most beneficial for each servant, but the objection states that for a poor unbeliever who suffers in both worlds, the most beneficial would be non-existence, yet he exists; thus, God has not observed what is most beneficial, so the principle is false. Then a noble anecdote (hikaya sharifa) is given that 'attacks with uprooting' this principle. The story: Al-Ash'ari asks his teacher al-Jubba'i about three brothers: one obedient, one disobedient, one died young. Al-Jubba'i says the obedient is rewarded, the disobedient punished, the child neither. Al-Ash'ari then poses a hypothetical: if the child complains that had he lived he would have been good and entered Paradise, and if the disobedient complains that had he died young he would have avoided sin, then God's response to the first (that He knew he would sin) and to the second (that He knew he would sin) leads to a contradiction: the child is denied life because of foreknowledge of sin, but the sinner is given life despite foreknowledge of sin. Al-Jubba'i is confounded. This event caused al-Ash'ari to abandon the Mu'tazili school and adopt the true school of the salaf. The commentary (hashiya) then explains the phrase 'تنحى بالقلع' (attacks with uprooting) as an intensified metaphor. It also clarifies that according to most Mu'tazila, the third brother (the child) enters Paradise but without reward, because reward requires veneration, and children of polytheists are servants of the people of Paradise. Finally, it analyzes the grammatical particle 'law' in the child's speech, noting it expresses wish (tamanni) and explaining its syntactic properties.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الواجب
الكافر الفقير
المعذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazili position is that God must do the most beneficial for everyone equally.
Correction: The Mu'tazila held that God must do what is most beneficial in general, but they allowed exceptions. The objection shows that even with exceptions, the principle fails for this case.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most beneficial for the poor unbeliever?
A) To be created wealthy
B) To be guided to faith
C) Not to be created at all
D) To be given a second chance

Key Takeaway
The existence of a poor unbeliever who suffers in both worlds shows that God does not always do what is most beneficial for each individual, refuting the Mu'tazili claim of its obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not have to do what is most beneficial for each person, as the poor unbeliever's case shows, then what does that mean for you when you face hardships that seem useless? Will you still trust that God's wisdom is higher than your calculation of benefit?
?
The passage says that for the poor unbeliever, non-existence would have been more beneficial than existence. Yet God created him anyway. How does this truth change your perspective on your own existence? Are you grateful simply to exist, even if you face suffering that seems without worldly benefit?
?
The Mu'tazili claim assumed that God's justice requires maximizing benefit for each individual. This lesson shows that God's ways are not bound by our notions of 'most beneficial'. In what area of your life are you unconsciously demanding that God owe you a certain outcome that you consider 'best' for you? Will you surrender that demand today?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
6 min
Al-Ash'ari's Famous Refutation: The Three Brothers
Al-Jurjani narrates the famous anecdote that led al-Ash'ari to leave the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous anecdote of the three brothers used by al-Ash'ari to refute al-Jubba'i's position on al-aslah.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's question traps al-Jubba'i into a contradiction about God's justice.

The story of three brothers is a pivotal moment in Islamic intellectual history. Al-Ash'ari, then a student of the Mu'tazili master al-Jubba'i, poses a hypothetical that exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazili doctrine of al-aslah. This led al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism and adopt the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the dialogue format.
2
Identify the three brothers and their fates
One obedient (rewarded), one disobedient (punished), one died young (neither rewarded nor punished).
This is al-Jubba'i's initial ruling.
3
Understand the hypothetical wishes
The child wishes he had lived to be good; the sinner wishes he had died young to avoid sin.
These wishes create a contradiction.
4
See how al-Jubba'i is confounded
God's foreknowledge of the child's sin justifies his early death, but then the sinner can argue he should have also died young. Al-Jubba'i has no answer.
This exposes the inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the outcome
Al-Ash'ari leaves Mu'tazilism and begins to refute their principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قال) الجبائي : ) يقول الرب : كنت أعلم أنك لو عمرت لفسقت وأفسدت فدخلت النار . قال : فيقول الثاني : يا رب لم لم تمتني صغيراً لئلا أذنب فلا أدخل النار كما أمت أخي؟ فبهت ( الجبائي ( فترك الأشعري مذهبه إِلى المذهب الحق) الذي كان عليه السلف الصالح ( وكان ) هذا ( أول ما خالف فيه ) الأشعري (المعتزلة) ثم اشتغل بهدم قواعدهم وتشييد مباني الحق بعون الله وحسن توفيقه . قوله: (تنحى بالقلع أي يقبل يقال : انحى عليه بالسوط أي أقبل به عليه والمعنى أنها تقلعه بإقبال وتوجه تام فهو أبلغ من القلع. قوله : ( والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب ) وإن كان يدخل الجنة عند عامة المعتزلة القائلين بأن أطفال المشركين خدم أهل الجنة فهم في الجنة ولا ثواب لهم إذ الثواب عندهم منفعة دائمة خالصة مقرونة بالتعظيم والإكرام، والقيد الأخير منتف في حقهم، ثم دخولهم الجنة بلا ثواب لا ينافي كونها دار ثواب لأن معناه أن الثواب لا يكون إلا فيها لا أن كل من دخلها يثاب . قوله : (يا رب لو عمرتني إلخ لو هذه للتمني وهي عند بعض النحاة قسم برأسها لا يحتاج إلى الجواب ولكن قد يؤتى لها بجواب منصوب كما فيما نحن فيه . وقيل : هي لو الشرطية التي أشربت معنى التمني وقيل : هي لو المصدرية أنيبت عن فعل التمني، والمراد بالدخول في قوله : فادخل الجنة، الدخول المقرون بالثواب، إذ الدخول بدونه حاصل لأطفال المشركين عندهم

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazili argument concerning the obligation of al-aslah (the most beneficial) for the servant in this world, followed by the famous anecdote of al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i that refutes it. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari used this story to prove free will.
Correction: The story refutes the Mu'tazili notion that God must do what is most beneficial; it does not directly prove free will.

Checkpoint
What was al-Jubba'i's answer when al-Ash'ari asked about the three brothers?
A) The child enters Paradise with reward
B) The child is in a state between reward and punishment
C) The child is neither rewarded nor punished
D) The child is punished lightly

Key Takeaway
The three brothers anecdote refutes the Mu'tazili principle of al-aslah by showing that God's foreknowledge would require equal treatment for the child and the sinner, which is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was unfair to you because He didn't give you the same opportunities as someone else? How does the three brothers anecdote challenge your assumption that you deserve better from God?
?

?
Al-Ash'ari was willing to abandon his entire theological school when he saw the truth. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you have never questioned, even though deep down you sense it might be inconsistent with the truth? What would it take for you to leave it behind for the sake of Allah?

Understanding the Commentary on the Three Brothers Story
Al-Jurjani's commentary on the anecdote, explaining key terms and the Mu'tazili view on children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and theological clarifications made by al-Jurjani regarding the three brothers story.

Core Idea
The commentator explains why the third brother enters Paradise without reward and analyzes the grammar of wish.

After narrating the famous anecdote, al-Jurjani provides a detailed commentary that clarifies important points: the intensity of the refutation, the status of the child according to the Mu'tazila, and the grammatical function of 'law' (if only). This lesson unpacks those explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on 'تنحى بالقلع'
Understand that it means attacking with full force, implying a thorough uprooting.
This emphasizes the strength of the refutation.
2
Understand the status of the child
According to most Mu'tazila, the third brother enters Paradise but without reward because reward requires veneration and honor.
This explains why he is neither rewarded nor punished.
3
Analyze the grammar of 'law'
The particle 'law' (لو) in the child's speech expresses wish (tamanni). Different grammatical analyses are given.
This clarifies the meaning of the child's statement.
4
Connect to theological implications
The child's wish is for entry with reward, which he currently lacks. This nuance is important for the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ( من الأمور الواجبة عندهم ( الأصلح للعبد في الدنيا فيقال : ) لهم ( الأصلح للكافر الفقير المعذب في الدنيا والآخرة أن لا يخلق ) مع أنه مخلوق فلم يراع في حقه ما كان أصلح له فلا يكون الأصلح واجباً عليه تعالى (حكاية) شريفة ( تنحى بالقلع على هذه القاعدة ) لقائلة بوجوب الأصلح على الله سبحانه وتعالى ( قال الأشعري لأستاذه أبي علي الجبائي : ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة عاش أحدهم الطاعة وأحدهم في المعصية ومات أحدهم صغيرا ؟ فقال : يثاب الأول بالجنة ويعاقب الثاني بالنار والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب . قال ( الأشعري : ( فإن قال الثالث : يا رب لو عمرتني فأصلح فأدخل الجنة) كما دخلها أخي المؤمن. (قال) الجبائي : ) يقول الرب : كنت أعلم أنك لو عمرت لفسقت وأفسدت فدخلت النار . قال : فيقول الثاني : يا رب لم لم تمتني صغيراً لئلا أذنب فلا أدخل النار كما أمت أخي؟ فبهت ( الجبائي ( فترك الأشعري مذهبه إِلى المذهب الحق) الذي كان عليه السلف الصالح ( وكان ) هذا ( أول ما خالف فيه ) الأشعري (المعتزلة) ثم اشتغل بهدم قواعدهم وتشييد مباني الحق بعون الله وحسن توفيقه . قوله: (تنحى بالقلع أي يقبل يقال : انحى عليه بالسوط أي أقبل به عليه والمعنى أنها تقلعه بإقبال وتوجه تام فهو أبلغ من القلع. قوله : ( والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب ) وإن كان يدخل الجنة عند عامة المعتزلة القائلين بأن أطفال المشركين خدم أهل الجنة فهم في الجنة ولا ثواب لهم إذ الثواب عندهم منفعة دائمة خالصة مقرونة بالتعظيم والإكرام، والقيد الأخير منتف في حقهم، ثم دخولهم الجنة بلا ثواب لا ينافي كونها دار ثواب لأن معناه أن الثواب لا يكون إلا فيها لا أن كل من دخلها يثاب . قوله : (يا رب لو عمرتني إلخ لو هذه للتمني وهي عند بعض النحاة قسم برأسها لا يحتاج إلى الجواب ولكن قد يؤتى لها بجواب منصوب كما فيما نحن فيه . وقيل : هي لو الشرطية التي أشربت معنى التمني وقيل : هي لو المصدرية أنيبت عن فعل التمني، والمراد بالدخول في قوله : فادخل الجنة، الدخول المقرون بالثواب، إذ الدخول بدونه حاصل لأطفال المشركين عندهم
The fourth: Among the obligatory matters according to them is [the principle of] what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for the servant in this world. So it is said to them: The most beneficial for the unbeliever, the poor, who is tormented in this world and the Hereafter, is that he not be created, whereas he is created; so what was most beneficial for him was not observed. Thus, al-aslah is not obligatory upon Him, exalted. There is a noble anecdote that attacks this principle with uprooting—i.e., it uproots it—of those who say that al-aslah is obligatory upon God, glorified and exalted. Al-Ash'ari said to his teacher Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i: What do you say about three brothers: one lived in obedience, one in disobedience, and one died as a small child? He said: The first is rewarded with Paradise, the second is punished with Hellfire, and the third is neither rewarded nor punished. Al-Ash'ari said: Then if the third says: O Lord, if only You had let me live so that I could have done good and entered Paradise, as my believing brother entered it? Al-Jubba'i said: The Lord says: I knew that if you had lived, you would have sinned and caused corruption, and thus entered Hellfire. He said: Then the second says: O Lord, why did You not let me die as a child so that I would not have sinned and thus not enter Hellfire, as You let my brother die? So al-Jubba'i was confounded, and al-Ash'ari abandoned his school for the true school, which was the way of the righteous predecessors (al-salaf al-salih). And this was the first matter in which al-Ash'ari opposed the Mu'tazila; then he occupied himself with demolishing their principles and building up the foundations of truth with God's help and good success. The commentator says: (Attacks with uprooting) means he advances upon it; it is said: 'he advanced upon him with the whip,' meaning he came at him with it, and the meaning is that it uproots it with complete coming and direction, so it is more intensive than mere uprooting. The commentator says: (And the third is neither rewarded nor punished) although he enters Paradise according to most Mu'tazila, who say that the children of polytheists are servants of the people of Paradise, so they are in Paradise but have no reward, for reward according to them is a permanent, pure benefit accompanied by veneration and honor, and the last restriction is absent in their case; then their entering Paradise without reward does not contradict it being the abode of reward, because its meaning is that reward occurs only in it, not that everyone who enters it is rewarded. The commentator says: (O Lord, if only You had let me live, etc.) 'law' here is for wish (tamanni), and according to some grammarians it is a category by itself that does not require a response, but sometimes a response in the accusative is given, as in this case. And it is said it is conditional 'law' imbued with the meaning of wish, and it is said it is 'law' of source (masdariyya) substituted for the verb of wish. What is meant by 'entering' in his saying 'so that I enter Paradise' is the entrance accompanied by reward, since entrance without it is attained for children of polytheists according to them.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazili argument concerning the obligation of al-aslah (the most beneficial) for the servant in this world, followed by the famous anecdote of al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i that refutes it. The argument is that God must do what is most beneficial for each servant, but the objection states that for a poor unbeliever who suffers in both worlds, the most beneficial would be non-existence, yet he exists; thus, God has not observed what is most beneficial, so the principle is false. Then a noble anecdote (hikaya sharifa) is given that 'attacks with uprooting' this principle. The story: Al-Ash'ari asks his teacher al-Jubba'i about three brothers: one obedient, one disobedient, one died young. Al-Jubba'i says the obedient is rewarded, the disobedient punished, the child neither. Al-Ash'ari then poses a hypothetical: if the child complains that had he lived he would have been good and entered Paradise, and if the disobedient complains that had he died young he would have avoided sin, then God's response to the first (that He knew he would sin) and to the second (that He knew he would sin) leads to a contradiction: the child is denied life because of foreknowledge of sin, but the sinner is given life despite foreknowledge of sin. Al-Jubba'i is confounded. This event caused al-Ash'ari to abandon the Mu'tazili school and adopt the true school of the salaf. The commentary (hashiya) then explains the phrase 'تنحى بالقلع' (attacks with uprooting) as an intensified metaphor. It also clarifies that according to most Mu'tazila, the third brother (the child) enters Paradise but without reward, because reward requires veneration, and children of polytheists are servants of the people of Paradise. Finally, it analyzes the grammatical particle 'law' in the child's speech, noting it expresses wish (tamanni) and explaining its syntactic properties.

Key Terms
القلع
الثواب
التعظيم
الإكرام
التمني
لو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the child is punished or in limbo.
Correction: According to the Mu'tazila cited, the child enters Paradise but without reward.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why do children of polytheists enter Paradise without reward?
A) They are not accountable
B) They lack the veneration and honor required for reward
C) God is not obligated to reward them
D) They are not Muslims

Key Takeaway
The commentary clarifies that the child's entry into Paradise without reward is consistent with Mu'tazili theology, and the linguistic analysis of 'law' shows that the child's wish is for rewarded entry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The third brother entered Paradise without reward because he lacked the capacity for veneration and honor. When you perform acts of worship, do you consciously cultivate a sense of awe and honor towards Allah, or do you go through motions mechanically? What one change can you make today to infuse your next prayer with genuine veneration?
?
The child in the story wished for a longer life to obey Allah. If you were to die today, what specific act of obedience would you most regret not having done? Why have you delayed it, and what will you do about it today?
?
Al-Jurjani meticulously analyzed the grammar of a single word ('law') to understand the child’s desire. How carefully do you examine the words of the Quran and Hadith? Is there a verse or prophetic saying you have been taking for granted? How might a deeper study of its meaning change your understanding?
