Mu'tazila and Ash'ari on Miracles of a Liar
Presents Mu'tazila and Ash'ari views on liar's miracle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing positions of the Mu'tazila and a group of Ash'aris on whether a miracle can be performed through a liar, and the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Two paths, one conclusion: miracles can't come from liars—but for different reasons.

This passage presents two theological positions on the possibility of a miracle occurring at the hand of a liar. The Mu'tazila argue that it is within God's power but impossible due to divine wisdom, while the Shaykh and his companions consider it inherently impossible because a miracle by definition gives definitive proof of truthfulness. Let's examine each view carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct sections: one for the Mu'tazila and one for the Shaykh's group.
The passage begins with 'وقالت المعتزلة' then shifts to 'قال الشيخ'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
Find what the Mu'tazila say about the possibility of a miracle through a liar. Note the two parts: within God's power, but impossible due to wisdom.
They say 'مقدور لله تعالى' but 'ممتنع وقوعه في حكمته'.
3
Identify the Ash'ari position
Find what the Shaykh and companions say. They say it is 'غير مقدور في نفسه' (not possible in itself). Understand the reason: the miracle has a definitive indication of truthfulness.
The key term is 'دلالة قطعية'.
4
Compare the two arguments
Contrast the reasoning: Mu'tazila focus on divine wisdom and avoiding deception; Ash'ari focus on the inherent nature of a miracle's indication.
Ask yourself: does either view deny God's power?
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that the Ash'ari view restricts God's omnipotence. The passage clarifies it is about the nature of the miracle, not a limit on power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة خلق المعجز على يد الكاذب مقدور لله تعالى لعموم قدرته لكنه ممتنع وقوعه في حكمته لأن فيه إيهام صدقه وهو إضلال قبيح من الله فيمتنع صدوره عنه كسائر القبائح قال الشيخ وبعض أصحابنا إنه أي خلق المعجزة على يد الكاذب غير مقدور في نفسه لأن لها أي للمعجزة دلالة على الصدق قطعاً أي دلالة قطعية يمتنع التخلف فيها فلا بد لها من وجه دلالة إذ به يتميز الدليل الصحيح عن غيره وإن لم نعلمه أي ذلك الوجه بعينه فإن دل
The Mu'tazila said: Creating a miracle at the hand of a liar is within God's power due to His general power, but its occurrence is impossible according to His wisdom, because it would imply his truthfulness, which is an ugly deception from God, impossible to emanate from Him like other ugly acts. The Shaykh and some of our companions said: It (i.e., creating a miracle at the hand of a liar) is not possible in itself, because the miracle has a definitive indication of truthfulness, i.e., a definitive indication that cannot be lacking; so it must have a mode of indication, for by it a sound proof is distinguished from others, even if we do not know that mode precisely. If it indicates...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage contrasts the Mu'tazila and a group of Ash'aris (the Shaykh and some followers) on whether it is possible for a miracle to come from a liar. The Mu'tazila say it is within God's power but impossible due to divine wisdom because it would be misleading, which is evil. The Shaykh's group denies its possibility entirely: a miracle, by definition, has a necessary connection to truthfulness, so it cannot be performed by a liar even by God's power, as that would contradict its nature.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
مقدور
ممتنع في الحكمة
غير مقدور
دلالة قطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view that a liar's miracle is impossible contradicts God's omnipotence.
Correction: The Ash'ari do not deny God's power; they argue that the impossibility is due to the inherent nature of miracles as definitive proofs of truthfulness. It is not that God cannot create such an event, but that an event with a definitive indication of truthfulness cannot occur through a liar by definition.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between the Mu'tazila and the Shaykh's view on a liar's miracle?
A) One says possible but impossible due to wisdom, the other says impossible in itself
B) Both agree it's impossible
C) One says possible, the other says impossible
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and a group of Ash'aris both conclude miracles cannot come from liars, but the Mu'tazila base it on divine wisdom and the Ash'ari on the inherent nature of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila warn that a miracle from a liar would be an 'ugly deception' from God. When have you been deceived by something that appeared 'miraculous' or too good to be true? How does this lesson help you discern true signs from Allah from false claims?
?
The Ash'ari insist that a miracle has a definitive indication of truth that cannot be lacking. Are you treating the clear signs of Allah in your life—like the Qur'an and the example of the Prophet—as definitive, or do you allow doubts to creep in? What would change if you accepted them with certainty?
?
Both schools agree that truth and falsehood cannot be mixed when it comes to divine signs. Examine your own speech and actions: where do you blend small lies or exaggerations with truth? How can you emulate the absolute truthfulness of Allah's signs in your own life starting today?

Al-Murtada's Theory of I'jaz as Prevention
Al-Murtada presents his Shiite theory of I'jaz as prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shiite theory of I'jaz proposed by al-Murtada, which explains the Quranic inimitability as a divine prevention rather than an inherent quality.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is not in its words but in Allah's act of disabling the opposition.

Al-Murtada, a prominent Shiite scholar, offers a distinct explanation for the Quran's inimitability. Unlike the majority view that the Quran is inherently miraculous, he argues that Allah actively prevented the Arabs from being able to produce a similar text. This passage presents his theory concisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Murtada uses the term 'salb al-‘ulūm' (stripping of sciences).
2
Identify the key claim and its reasoning
Identify al-Murtada's main claim: that the i'jaz is due to prevention (sarf). Then trace his reasoning: the Arabs originally had the sciences needed to produce a text like the Quran, but Allah removed them, leaving them powerless.
3
Contrast with the Sunni theory of inherent i'jaz
Ask yourself: How does this theory differ from the more common view that the Quran itself is inimitable in content? Al-Murtada shifts the miracle from the text to the divine act.
This connects to the common misunderstanding about the nature of i'jaz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المرتضى : ( من الشيعة : (بل) صرفهم بأن ( سلبهم العلوم التي يحتاج إليها في المعارضة) يعني أن المعارضة والإتيان بمثل القرآن يحتاج إلى علوم يقتدر بها عليها، وكانت تلك العلوم حاصلة لهم لكنه تعالى سلبها عنهم فلم يبق لهم قدرة عليها .
And al-Murtada (from the Shi'a) said: 'Rather, He turned them away by stripping them of the sciences they needed for opposition.' Meaning that opposition and producing something like the Quran requires sciences by which one would be able to do so, and those sciences were possessed by them, but Allah stripped them from them, so they no longer had the ability for it.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Murtada, a Shiite scholar, on the nature of the Quranic inimitability (i'jaz). He argues that the inimitability lies not in the Quran's content but in a divine act of prevention: Allah stripped the Arabs of the knowledge necessary to produce a similar text, thus they became powerless. The text explains that producing a work like the Quran requires specific sciences, which the Arabs originally had, but Allah removed them, leaving them unable to oppose. This is the theory of 'sarf' (turning away or prevention). The passage is a straightforward statement of this theory.

Key Terms
المرتضى
الصرف
سلب العلوم
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-Murtada's theory is the same as the Sunni view that the Quran's inimitability is intrinsic.
Correction: Al-Murtada holds that the miracle is the act of Allah stripping the Arabs of the sciences needed for opposition, not an inherent property of the Quran.

Checkpoint
According to al-Murtada, what is the basis (theory) of the Quran's inimitability?
A) The Quran's unmatched eloquence
B) The Quran's predictions of the unseen
C) Allah's prevention of the Arabs from opposing it by removing their knowledge
D) The Quran's perfect legal rulings

Key Takeaway
Al-Murtada's theory of I'jaz is that Allah stripped the Arabs of the sciences needed to oppose the Quran, making opposition impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah can strip people of knowledge and ability, how secure is your own reliance on your intellect and skills? Does this humility penetrate your daily practice?
?
This theory says the Arabs had the ability to oppose but Allah removed it. Do you live as if your knowledge is a permanent possession or as a fragile gift that could be taken at any moment?
?
How does this perspective change your gratitude for being able to read and understand the Quran? What would your life be like if that ability were removed?

First Jewish Objection: Abrogation Implies Ignorance or Badā'
Presents the first Jewish objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish objection that abrogation implies ignorance or badā' in God, and the Ash'ari refutation based on the non-obligation of benefit and the variability of benefits over time.

Core Idea
Abrogation does not imply ignorance or badā' because benefits are not obligatory and can change.

This passage presents the first Jewish argument against the prophethood of Muhammad: that abrogation of previous laws implies either ignorance or badā' (change of mind) in God, both impossible. The author refutes this by clarifying the nature of divine rulings and benefits, showing that abrogation does not entail any imperfection in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the objection: abrogation indicates either ignorance (if God didn't know the benefit) or badā' (if He knew but changed His mind). Note the logic that every ruling must have a benefit.
The objection relies on the principle that God's acts must have a benefit, which Ash'aris do not accept as necessary.
2
Examine the first part of the response
The author answers that observing benefit is not obligatory in God's rulings, so the abrogated ruling need not contain a benefit. This removes the basis for accusing God of ignorance or badā'.
This is a distinct Ash'ari position; other schools may disagree.
3
Examine the second part of the response
Even if benefits are required, they can change over time, like medicine appropriate for one time but not another. Thus abrogation may simply reflect changing benefits, not ignorance or badā'.
The author also notes that the subjects of rulings differ (different peoples), further undermining the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا أي اليهود المنكرون بوجهين: الأول: أن نبوته تقتضي نسخ دين من قبله إذ قد خالفهم في كثير من الأحكام الشرعية العملية باتفاق منكم لكن النسخ أمر محال لأنه يدل إما على الجهل أو البداء وكلاهما محال على الله تعالى. بيانه أنه لا بد أن يكون الحكم الصادر عنه تعالى مشتملا على مصلحة لئلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، وحينئذ لو كان فيه مصلحة لا يعلمها فذلك الجهل، وإن كان يعلمها فرأى رعايتها أولا ثم أهملها بلا سبب ثانيا فالبداء. والجواب: أنه لا يجب رعاية المصلحة في الأحكام عندنا فلا يلزم اشتمال الحكم المنسوخ على مصلحة، وإن وجب أن تراعى المصالح في الأحكام فربما حدثت مصلحة لم تكن حاصلة قبل، فإن المصالح تختلف بحسب الأوقات كشرب الدواء الخاص في وقت دون وقت، فربما كانت المصلحة في وقت ثبوت الحكم لاشتماله فيه على ما يجب رعايته، وفي وقت آخر ارتفاعه لاشتماله فيه على مصلحة أخرى حادثة بعد زوال الأولى أو مرجوحيتها مقيسة إلى الثانية، فلا يلزم ما ذكرتم من الجهل أو البداء. وكيف لا يجوز ما ذكرناه والمحكوم عليه هنا ليس بمتحد إذ تلك لأقوام آخرين.
They argued, meaning the denying Jews, with two points: The first: that his prophethood necessitates abrogation of the religion before him, since he opposed them in many practical legal rulings by your agreement. But abrogation is impossible because it indicates either ignorance or badā', both of which are impossible for God Almighty. Its explanation: There must be in the ruling from God Almighty a benefit to avoid preference without a preponderator. Then if there was in it a benefit that He does not know, that is ignorance; and if He knows it and considered its observance first then neglected it without a second cause, that is badā'. The answer: Observance of benefit is not obligatory in rulings according to us, so it is not necessary that the abrogated ruling contain a benefit. And if it is obligatory to observe benefits in rulings, perhaps a benefit may arise that was not present before, for benefits differ according to times, like drinking a specific medicine at a specific time rather than another. So perhaps the benefit was at the time of establishing the ruling because it contained what must be observed, and at another time its removal because it contains another benefit that occurred after the first ceased or its inferiority compared to the second. So what you mentioned of ignorance or badā' does not follow. And how could what we have mentioned not be permissible, and the subject of the ruling here is not identical, for those were for other peoples.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage contains the first objection of the rejecting Jews and the author's response. The objection is that Muhammad's prophethood requires the abrogation of previous religions, which is impossible because abrogation implies either ignorance (if God did not know the benefit of the original ruling) or badā' (if He knew but changed His mind), both impossible for God. The proof is that every ruling must have a benefit; if the benefit was unknown to God, that is ignorance; if known but later abandoned without cause, that is badā'. The answer has two parts: First, in Ash'ari doctrine, benefit is not obligatory in divine rulings, so the abrogated ruling need not have a benefit. Second, even if benefit is required, benefits can change over time—like medicine appropriate for one time but not another. Thus abrogation does not necessarily involve ignorance or badā'. Additionally, the subject of the ruling (the people addressed) is not the same, so no contradiction arises.

Key Terms
نسخ
الجهل
البداء
المصلحة
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny that divine acts have any purpose or benefit.
Correction: Ash'aris deny that benefit is obligatory for God, but they affirm that benefits may exist and can change over time.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does abrogation not necessarily imply ignorance or badā'?
A) Because God does not have knowledge of future benefits.
B) Because observing benefit is not obligatory in rulings, and benefits can change over time.
C) Because the Jews misunderstood the nature of abrogation.
D) Because abrogation is only for non-practical rulings.

Key Takeaway
The first Jewish objection is refuted by showing that abrogation does not entail ignorance or badā' because benefits are not mandatory in divine rulings and can change over time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a change in your life that you didn't expect, do you secretly suspect God has 'changed His mind' or made a mistake? This lesson teaches that God's wisdom adapts to shifting circumstances. Will you trust that His timing is perfect even when you don't understand the benefit?
?
The Jewish objection assumed God must always act with obvious benefit. Have you ever obeyed God's command only because you saw a personal benefit, and neglected a command when it seemed costly? Would you obey even if you saw no benefit at all, simply because He commanded?
?
If God's rulings can change because benefits change, then your current situation is not static. Are you willing to submit to His authority in every season, knowing that He, not you, defines what is beneficial for your soul?

Refuting the 'Isawiyya: Muhammad's Universal Prophethood
The author refutes the 'Isawiyya's restriction of Muhammad's prophethood to Arabs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the 'Isawiyya's argument that Prophet Muhammad was sent only to Arabs, using both textual reinterpretation and the impossibility of denying abrogation.

Core Idea
The universality of Muhammad's mission is proven by tawātur, not limited by one verse.

The 'Isawiyya, a Jewish sect, accepted Muhammad's prophethood but restricted it to Arabs, citing Quran 14:4. This lesson unpacks the author's two-pronged response: first, reinterpreting 'his people' to mean the initial audience, not a permanent restriction; second, pointing out that the Torah itself contains abrogation, undermining their claim that abrogation is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'Isawiyya's argument
Read the passage and note how they use Quran 14:4 to claim Muhammad was only for Arabs.
2
Examine the first rebuttal
The author reinterprets 'his people' and invokes tawātur to prove Muhammad's universal mission.
3
Examine the second rebuttal
The author gives examples from the Torah to show that abrogation is not impossible, contradicting the 'Isawiyya's stance.
4
Reflect on the combined evidence
Consider how both scriptural reinterpretation and historical/cross-scriptural evidence work together to refute the sect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (إلا العيسوية إلخ) احتجوا بقوله تعالى: {وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا بلسان قومه} [إبراهيم: 4]، وجوابه أن المراد بالقوم يحتمل أن يكون قوما نشأ النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم فيهم لأمته، على أن تسليم رسالته ولو خاصة تستلزم تسليم صدقه، وقد ثبت بالتواتر أنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم ادعى أنه مبعوث إلى كافة الأمم لا إلى العرب خاصة. قوله: (لكن) النسخ أمر إلخ فحينئذ يرد عليهم أنه جاء في التوراة أن الله تعالى قال لآدم عليه السلام وحواء: قد أحللت لكما كل ما دب على وجه الأرض، وقد حرم على نوح بعض الحيوانات، وحرم الجمع بين الأختين في شريعة موسى عليه السلام مع إباحته في شريعة آدم عليه السلام فكيف يدعون استحالة النسخ مطلقا.
His saying: (except the 'Isawiyya etc.) They argued with His saying: {And We sent no messenger except with the language of his people} [Ibrahim: 4]. The answer is that the meaning of 'people' could be the people among whom the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, grew up, for his nation, and that conceding his message even specific entails conceding his truthfulness. And it has been established by tawātur that he claimed to be sent to all nations, not to Arabs alone. His saying: (but) abrogation is an impossible matter etc. Then it is rebutted against them that it came in the Torah that God said to Adam and Eve: 'I have made lawful for you every creature that crawls on the face of the earth,' and He forbade to Noah some animals, and forbade marrying two sisters in the law of Moses while permitting it in the law of Adam. So how can they claim the impossibility of abrogation absolutely?
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses the 'Isawiyya, a Jewish sect that accepted Muhammad's prophethood but limited it to Arabs. They cite Quran 14:4 that every prophet was sent in the language of his people, implying Muhammad was only for Arabs. The response has two points: First, 'his people' could mean the people he was born among, but he was sent to his entire nation, and the verse does not restrict universality. Second, even if his message were specific, admitting his prophethood requires admitting his truthfulness, and he claimed by tawātur to be sent to all. Then a further rebuttal is given: the Torah itself shows abrogation (e.g., dietary laws changed from Adam to Noah to Moses), so the Jews cannot claim abrogation is absolutely impossible.

Key Terms
العيسوية
بلسان قومه
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'Isawiyya believe that Quran 14:4 restricts Muhammad's prophethood to Arabs because he must speak their language.
Correction: The verse only addresses the need for a prophet to communicate in his people's language; it does not indicate a permanent restriction. Moreover, tawātur confirms Muhammad's universal mission.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the 'Isawiyya's use of Quran 14:4?
A) By arguing that the verse has been abrogated.
B) By reinterpreting 'people' as the group the prophet was sent to, not his ethnic group.
C) By citing that Muhammad's claim of universality is proven by tawātur, and the verse is not restrictive.
D) By denying the authenticity of the verse.

Key Takeaway
The 'Isawiyya's restriction of Muhammad's prophethood to Arabs is refuted by reinterpreting Quran 14:4 and by demonstrating that abrogation is not impossible, as shown in the Torah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever limited the reach of God's guidance to a specific group, culture, or era—perhaps by assuming that your understanding of Islam is sufficient and others must conform exactly to it? What in this lesson calls you to expand your view?
?
The 'Isawiyya rejected the overwhelming evidence (tawātur) because of a single verse interpreted narrowly. Where in your own life have you clung to a limited reading of a text or a prior belief, even when cumulative evidence pointed to a broader truth? What would it cost you to surrender that narrowness?
?
The author uses examples from the Torah to show that divine laws change. Are you willing to accept that God's commands can supersede previous ones, even when it challenges your comfort or prior assumptions? What specific area of your obedience to God might need to change if you truly submit to His latest command?

The Second Jewish Objection on Abrogation and Its Refutation
Second Jewish objection: Moses denied abrogation; three possibilities analyzed and refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Jewish objection against abrogation, its three-part logical analysis, and the major responses offered by Muslim theologians.

Core Idea
Moses' denial of abrogation is analyzed into three possibilities, all refuted using logic and transmission.

Jewish opponents argued that Moses himself forbade abrogation, so any prophet claiming to abrogate his law cannot be true. This lesson examines that objection, the three possibilities they raised, and how Muslim scholars refuted them by questioning the transmission and reinterpreting the scope of Moses' statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its proof
Start by reading the objection: Moses denied abrogation, so his statement must be true, thus no prophet can come after him. Note the use of tawātur as evidence.
The objection relies on a supposed widely transmitted command to hold the Sabbath forever.
2
Analyze the three possibilities
The author presents three logical possibilities regarding Moses' statement: he explicitly stated perpetuity, explicitly stated non-perpetuity, or remained silent. Understand why the last two are considered false.
This is a classic kalam method of exhaustive division.
3
Examine the refutation
Study the refutation: the claim of tawātur is denied, and it is argued that the statement was fabricated. If it were true, it would have been used against Muhammad and widely transmitted.
Also note the alternative interpretation: perpetuity until a future prophetic abrogator.
4
Understand the alternative interpretation
The author offers that Moses may have stated perpetuity until the appearance of an abrogator, but the statement was not widely transmitted because it contained a proof against the Jews.
This resolves the objection without accepting absolute perpetuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: من الوجهين أن موسى عليه السلام نفى نسخ دينه ولا بد من الاعتراف بصدقه لكونه نبيا بالاتفاق، وحينئذ لا نبوة من يدعي نسخه وهو المطلوب. بيانه: أنه تواتر عنه قوله: تمسكوا بالسبت ما دامت السماوات والأرض، وإذا ثبت دوام السبت وامتناع نسخه ثبت امتناعه في سائر أحكامه. بل نقول: المراد بدوامه دوام اليهودية كما يتبادر إليه الفهم. وأيضا: فإنه إما أن يكون موسى قد صرح بدوام دينه أو بعدم دوامه أو سكت عنهما، والأخيران باطلان. أما الثاني وهو تصريحه بعدم دوامه فلأنه لو قال ذلك لتواتر عنه قطعا لكونه من الأمور العظيمة التي تتوفر الدواعي على نقلها وإشاعتها سيما من الأعداء ومن يدعي نسخ دينه، وذلك حجة له فيه، فلا بد أن تتوفر دواعيه على نقله لكنه لم يتواتر إجماعا. وأما الثالث وهو سكوته عنهما فلأنه يقتضي ثبوت دينه مرة واحدة وعدم تكرره، لأن مقتضى الإطلاق يتحقق بالمرة الواحدة، وأنه معلوم الانتفاء لتقرره إلى أوان النسخ إما بشريعة عيسى أو بشريعة محمد باتفاق بيننا وبينكم. والجواب: منع تواتر ذلك من موسى عليه السلام، ولو كان كذلك لاحتج به على محمد، ولو احتج به عليه لنقل ذلك متواترا لتوفر الدواعي على نقله ولا تواتر أصلا. كيف وقد اشتهر أنه اختلقه ابن الراوندي لليهود. وأما الترديد فنختار منه أنه صرح بدوامه إلى ظهور الناسخ على لسان نبي يأتي من بعده، وإنما لم ينقل تواترا إما لقلة الدواعي إلى نقله لما فيه من الحجة عليهم.
The second of the two points is that Moses, peace be upon him, denied the abrogation of his religion, and it is necessary to acknowledge his truthfulness because he is a prophet by agreement; then there is no prophethood for one who claims its abrogation, and that is what is sought. Its explanation: It has been transmitted by tawātur from him his saying: 'Hold fast to the Sabbath as long as the heavens and the earth endure.' And if the perpetuity of the Sabbath and the impossibility of its abrogation are established, then the impossibility is established in all his rulings. Rather, we say: by its perpetuity is meant the perpetuity of Judaism, as the understanding would immediately incline. Also: either Moses explicitly stated the perpetuity of his religion, or stated its non-perpetuity, or remained silent about both; the last two are false. As for the second—his explicit statement of its non-perpetuity—because if he said that, it would have been transmitted by tawātur certainly, because it is among the great matters that motives are ample to transmit and publicize, especially from enemies and those who claim the abrogation of his religion; and that would be a proof for him in it, so motives would certainly be ample to transmit it—but it did not reach tawātur by consensus. As for the third—his silence about both—because it would imply the establishment of his religion once and not its repetition, because the implication of absolute statement is realized by a single occurrence, and that is known to be false because it remained until the time of abrogation, either by the law of Jesus or by the law of Muhammad, by agreement between us and you. The answer: Denial that there is tawātur of that from Moses, peace be upon him; if it were so, he would have argued with it against Muhammad, and if he argued with it against him, then that argument would have been transmitted by tawātur because motives are ample for its transmission, but there is no tawātur at all. Moreover, it is well-known that Ibn al-Rawandi fabricated it for the Jews. As for the disjunction (the three possibilities), we choose from it that he explicitly stated its perpetuity until the appearance of an abrogator through the tongue of a prophet who comes after him; and it was not transmitted by tawātur only because of scarcity of motives to transmit it, since it contained a proof against them.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage contains the second Jewish objection and its refutation. The objection is that Moses denied abrogation of his law, so anyone claiming to abrogate it cannot be a prophet. The proof is a supposed tawātur report that Moses commanded to hold the Sabbath forever. The author first reinterprets 'perpetuity' as the perpetuity of Judaism as a whole, not necessarily every ruling. Then he analyzes three possibilities: (1) Moses explicitly stated his law would last forever, (2) explicitly stated it would end, or (3) remained silent. (2) is false because such a statement would have been widely transmitted, but it is not. (3) is false because it would imply the law was established only once, but it continued until abrogation by Jesus or Muhammad. Thus the only remaining possibility is (1) that Moses stated perpetuity—but the response denies the tawātur of such a statement. If it were true, Muhammad's opponents would have used it and it would be widely transmitted, but it is not. In fact, Ibn al-Rawandi is said to have fabricated it. The author then offers an alternative: Moses may have stated perpetuity until a future prophetic abrogator, but this was not widely transmitted because it worked against Jewish interests.

Key Terms
تواتر
السبت
دوام
ابن الراوندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' mention of 'perpetuity' means his law is valid forever without any possibility of abrogation.
Correction: The passage shows that 'perpetuity' may refer to the religion as a whole or be conditioned until a future prophetic abrogator, and the transmission of the statement is disputed.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that Moses explicitly stated the non-perpetuity of his law?
A) Because such a statement would contradict other scriptures.
B) Because it would have been transmitted by tawātur due to its importance, but it is not.
C) Because Moses never spoke about the future of his law.
D) Because the Jews would have hidden it.

Key Takeaway
The second Jewish objection is refuted by denying the tawātur of Moses' statement and by reinterpreting 'perpetuity' as conditioned on the arrival of a future prophetic abrogator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim that challenges your deeply held beliefs, do you automatically reject it, or do you objectively examine its evidence? How does the tripartite analysis in this lesson challenge you to be more thorough in your own reasoning?
?
The Jews clung to the Sabbath as eternal, yet this passage shows that even a prophet's statement can be misinterpreted or fabricated. What are you holding onto in your faith that you have not critically examined? Are you willing to let go if clear evidence demands it?
?
The author denies the tawātur of the statement and attributes it to a fabricator. How often do you accept reports (in religion or daily life) simply because they are repeated, without verifying their authenticity? What step will you take this week to verify one claim you have been assuming is true?

The Shi'a Claim of Taqiyya and the First Refutation
Shi'a claim and first refutation about hiding the da'wa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument for prophets showing disbelief under threat and the first refutation that it leads to abandoning the prophetic mission.

Core Idea
Taqiyya for prophets would destroy the very purpose of prophethood.

The Shi'a claim that prophets may outwardly show disbelief (kufr) to save their lives seems plausible at first, but it has serious consequences. This passage presents the Shi'a argument and the author's first powerful refutation: if prophets hide their faith, the entire da'wa collapses. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a claim
Find the sentence where the Shi'a permit prophets to show disbelief as taqiyya. Notice their justification: fearing destruction and avoiding self-harm.
The Arabic phrase 'وجوزا الشيعة إظهاره تقية' captures the permission.
2
Understand the refutation
The author says this is 'absolutely false' (باطل قطعاً). Identify the reason: it leads to hiding the da'wa completely.
The refutation targets the essence of prophethood—conveying the message.
3
Connect to context of da'wa
The author adds that the most appropriate time for taqiyya (if it were allowed) would be when the da'wa is weak. This makes the problem worse.
Under duress, prophets would be most needed to speak out, not hide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوزا الشيعة إظهاره ) أي إظهار الكفر ( تقية ) عند خوف الهلاك لأن إظهار الإسلام حينئذ إلقاء للنفس في التهلكة ) وذلك ) باطل قطعاً لأنه ( يفضي إلى إخفاء الدعوة ) بالكلية وترك تبليغ الرسالة ( إذ أولى الأوقات بالتقية وقت الدعوة للضعف ) بسبب قلة الموافق أو عدمه ) وكثرة المخالفين) وأيضاً ما ذكروه منقوض بدعوة إبراهيم وموسى عليهما السلام في زمن نمرود وفرعون مع شدة خوف الهلاك
قوله : ( وأيضاً ما ذكروه منقوض إلخ) النقض بالنسبة إلى ما دل عليه دليلهم من وجوب إظهار الكفر عند خوف الهلاك لأن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة حرام يجب اجتنابه لا بالنسبة إلى ما يفهم من ظاهر قوله وجوز الشيعة لأن الجواز لا ينافي التخلف لكن يرد على النقض بدعوة عليه السلام أن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة إنما يلزم إذا لم يكن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه بعدم إهلاك نومه وعدم قدرتهم عليه وموسى عليه السلام كان عالماً به كما دل عليه صریح قوله تعالى : ولا تخافا إنني معكما ﴾ [طه: ٤٦] ، أي بالحفظ والنصر بعد قول موسى وهارون عليهما السلام وربنا إننا نخاف أن يفرط علينا أو أن يطغى ﴾ [ طه : ٤٥ ]، نعم يتم [طه: النقض ببعض الأنبياء فإن العصمة غير لازمة فكيف إعلامها ألا يرى أن الكفار قتلوا فريقا من الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين ولم يسمع من أحدهم إظهار الكفر.
And the Shi'a permitted showing it, i.e., showing disbelief, as taqiyya (dissimulation) out of fear of destruction, because showing Islam then would be casting oneself into destruction. And that is absolutely false because it leads to hiding the da'wa (call) completely and abandoning conveying the message. Indeed, the most appropriate times for taqiyya are the time of the da'wa when it is weak due to few or no supporters and many opponents. Also, what they mentioned is refuted by the da'wa of Ibrahim and Musa, peace be upon them, in the time of Nimrod and Pharaoh despite severe fear of destruction. His statement (and also what they mentioned is refuted, etc.): The refutation is with respect to what their evidence indicated of the obligation to show disbelief when fearing destruction, because casting oneself into destruction is forbidden and must be avoided, not with respect to what is understood from the apparent meaning of his statement 'the Shi'a permitted' because permission does not preclude non-occurrence. However, an objection is raised against the refutation by the da'wa of those prophets: that casting oneself into destruction only applies if the Prophet was not such that God would not destroy his sleep and they would not have power over him, and Moses was aware of this as clearly indicated by His statement: 'Fear not, indeed I am with you' [Ta-Ha 46], i.e., with protection and victory, after the statement of Moses and Aaron: 'Our Lord, indeed we fear that he will hasten against us or that he will transgress' [Ta-Ha 45]. Yes, the refutation is complete for some prophets, for infallibility is not necessary, so how could it be known? Do you not see that the disbelievers killed a group of prophets, peace be upon them all, and not one of them was heard to show disbelief?
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a position that prophets are permitted to outwardly show disbelief (kufr) under threat of death (taqiyya). The author begins by stating the Shi'a claim: that a prophet may display kufr as taqiyya when fearing destruction, because publicly affirming Islam would lead to self-destruction, which is forbidden. The author then refutes this as absolutely false for two reasons. First, it would lead to completely hiding the da'wa (the call to Islam) and abandoning the mission of conveying the message, especially since the time when the da'wa is weak (due to few supporters) is precisely when taqiyya would be most needed—thus the practice would undermine the very purpose of prophethood. Second, the author points out that this argument is refuted by the examples of Prophet Ibrahim and Prophet Musa, who delivered their da'wa to Nimrod and Pharaoh respectively despite intense fear of destruction. A marginal gloss then clarifies the logic of the refutation: the Shi'a evidence only shows that showing disbelief is obligatory when there is unavoidable destruction, but the prophets in those examples were aware that God would protect them, as shown in the Quranic verses where God tells Musa not to fear. However, the gloss notes that the refutation fully applies to prophets who were actually killed by disbelievers—none of them resorted to showing disbelief. Thus, the Shi'a position is invalid.

Key Terms
تقية
إخفاء الدعوة
إلقاء النفس في التهلكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqiyya is always permissible for prophets to save their lives.
Correction: This passage shows that such permission would negate the very purpose of prophethood—conveying the message—especially when the da'wa is weak.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author gives for rejecting the Shi'a claim?
A) It contradicts the Quran
B) It leads to hiding the da'wa completely
C) It was not practiced by earlier prophets
D) It is forbidden by consensus

Key Takeaway
Taqiyya for prophets is rejected because it would undermine the essential duty of conveying the divine message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you hidden your faith to avoid discomfort or conflict? What does the author's refutation of taqiyya for prophets reveal about the excuses you make? Will you commit to being openly truthful about your beliefs in one specific situation this week, regardless of the cost?
?
The Shi'a argument for taqiyya sounds practical: protect yourself. But the author says it would subvert the entire purpose of prophethood. Where in your own life do you choose the 'practical' safe route over the path of fully conveying what is true? What would it mean for you to truly abandon that safety for the sake of Allah's message?
?
If you were among the first Muslims facing persecution, would you have seen taqiyya as wisdom? The author says the time of weakness is precisely when the da'wa must be proclaimed, not hidden. How does this challenge your natural desire for self-preservation? Are you willing to be 'foolish' for the sake of truth, even if it means personal loss?

Second Refutation: Examples of Ibrahim and Musa
Second refutation using Ibrahim/Musa and response to objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation using the examples of Ibrahim and Musa, and how the author defends his refutation against an objection.

Core Idea
The prophets Ibrahim and Musa faced great fear but never resorted to taqiyya.

Having given a logical refutation, the author now provides historical counterexamples. The stories of Ibrahim facing Nimrod and Musa facing Pharaoh show that prophets can deliver their message even under extreme threat. A marginal gloss then addresses a potential objection: weren't those prophets protected by God? Let's see how this strengthens the overall argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counterexample
Locate the sentence mentioning Ibrahim and Musa. Note the context: they called to God despite severe fear of destruction from Nimrod and Pharaoh.
The phrase 'بدعوة إبراهيم وموسى عليهما السلام' is key.
2
Understand the objection
Read the gloss: it mentions that some might argue that Ibrahim and Musa were protected by God, so their example doesn't apply when the prophet is not protected.
The objector uses Quranic proof that God was with Musa.
3
See the final rejoinder
The author concludes that for prophets who were actually killed by disbelievers, the refutation stands completely—none showed disbelief.
This covers cases where divine protection was not manifest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوزا الشيعة إظهاره ) أي إظهار الكفر ( تقية ) عند خوف الهلاك لأن إظهار الإسلام حينئذ إلقاء للنفس في التهلكة ) وذلك ) باطل قطعاً لأنه ( يفضي إلى إخفاء الدعوة ) بالكلية وترك تبليغ الرسالة ( إذ أولى الأوقات بالتقية وقت الدعوة للضعف ) بسبب قلة الموافق أو عدمه ) وكثرة المخالفين) وأيضاً ما ذكروه منقوض بدعوة إبراهيم وموسى عليهما السلام في زمن نمرود وفرعون مع شدة خوف الهلاك
قوله : ( وأيضاً ما ذكروه منقوض إلخ) النقض بالنسبة إلى ما دل عليه دليلهم من وجوب إظهار الكفر عند خوف الهلاك لأن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة حرام يجب اجتنابه لا بالنسبة إلى ما يفهم من ظاهر قوله وجوز الشيعة لأن الجواز لا ينافي التخلف لكن يرد على النقض بدعوة عليه السلام أن إلقاء النفس في التهلكة إنما يلزم إذا لم يكن النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه بعدم إهلاك نومه وعدم قدرتهم عليه وموسى عليه السلام كان عالماً به كما دل عليه صریح قوله تعالى : ولا تخافا إنني معكما ﴾ [طه: ٤٦] ، أي بالحفظ والنصر بعد قول موسى وهارون عليهما السلام وربنا إننا نخاف أن يفرط علينا أو أن يطغى ﴾ [ طه : ٤٥ ]، نعم يتم [طه: النقض ببعض الأنبياء فإن العصمة غير لازمة فكيف إعلامها ألا يرى أن الكفار قتلوا فريقا من الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم أجمعين ولم يسمع من أحدهم إظهار الكفر.
And the Shi'a permitted showing it, i.e., showing disbelief, as taqiyya (dissimulation) out of fear of destruction, because showing Islam then would be casting oneself into destruction. And that is absolutely false because it leads to hiding the da'wa (call) completely and abandoning conveying the message. Indeed, the most appropriate times for taqiyya are the time of the da'wa when it is weak due to few or no supporters and many opponents. Also, what they mentioned is refuted by the da'wa of Ibrahim and Musa, peace be upon them, in the time of Nimrod and Pharaoh despite severe fear of destruction. His statement (and also what they mentioned is refuted, etc.): The refutation is with respect to what their evidence indicated of the obligation to show disbelief when fearing destruction, because casting oneself into destruction is forbidden and must be avoided, not with respect to what is understood from the apparent meaning of his statement 'the Shi'a permitted' because permission does not preclude non-occurrence. However, an objection is raised against the refutation by the da'wa of those prophets: that casting oneself into destruction only applies if the Prophet was not such that God would not destroy his sleep and they would not have power over him, and Moses was aware of this as clearly indicated by His statement: 'Fear not, indeed I am with you' [Ta-Ha 46], i.e., with protection and victory, after the statement of Moses and Aaron: 'Our Lord, indeed we fear that he will hasten against us or that he will transgress' [Ta-Ha 45]. Yes, the refutation is complete for some prophets, for infallibility is not necessary, so how could it be known? Do you not see that the disbelievers killed a group of prophets, peace be upon them all, and not one of them was heard to show disbelief?
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a position that prophets are permitted to outwardly show disbelief (kufr) under threat of death (taqiyya). The author begins by stating the Shi'a claim: that a prophet may display kufr as taqiyya when fearing destruction, because publicly affirming Islam would lead to self-destruction, which is forbidden. The author then refutes this as absolutely false for two reasons. First, it would lead to completely hiding the da'wa (the call to Islam) and abandoning the mission of conveying the message, especially since the time when the da'wa is weak (due to few supporters) is precisely when taqiyya would be most needed—thus the practice would undermine the very purpose of prophethood. Second, the author points out that this argument is refuted by the examples of Prophet Ibrahim and Prophet Musa, who delivered their da'wa to Nimrod and Pharaoh respectively despite intense fear of destruction. A marginal gloss then clarifies the logic of the refutation: the Shi'a evidence only shows that showing disbelief is obligatory when there is unavoidable destruction, but the prophets in those examples were aware that God would protect them, as shown in the Quranic verses where God tells Musa not to fear. However, the gloss notes that the refutation fully applies to prophets who were actually killed by disbelievers—none of them resorted to showing disbelief. Thus, the Shi'a position is invalid.

Key Terms
تقية
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets always have divine protection from harm, so their example does not apply.
Correction: The passage notes that some prophets were killed, yet none showed disbelief, proving taqiyya is not valid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author mention prophets who were killed by disbelievers?
A) To show that prophets are not infallible
B) To prove that even when not protected, they did not show disbelief
C) To argue that taqiyya is only for weaker prophets
D) To demonstrate that prophets can be killed

Key Takeaway
The historical examples of Ibrahim and Musa, and especially prophets who were killed, prove that prophets never resort to taqiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your life have you toned down your faith or remained silent about Islam to avoid discomfort, rejection, or harm? How does the example of prophets who faced death without concealment expose the weakness of that choice? What is one specific situation this week where you can practice being more open about your belief, even if it costs you something?
?
The marginal note in the lesson distinguishes between being permitted to do something and it being obligatory. Where in your own life do you hide behind 'permissibility' to avoid a harder, more truthful stance? What would change if you acted as if the higher path were obligatory rather than just allowed?
?
The author points out that prophets who were killed still did not show disbelief. If you were faced with a choice between denying your faith or losing something precious—your reputation, a relationship, or even your life—do you think you would hold firm? What is the one fear that currently holds you back from full obedience, and what step can you take today to begin overcoming it?

The Disagreement on Prophets vs Angels
States the positions on the superiority of prophets versus angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on whether prophets or angels are superior and the scope of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The debate is only about heavenly angels, not earthly ones.

This passage introduces a classic theological debate: who is superior, prophets or angels? The author first clarifies that the disagreement concerns only the heavenly angels, as all agree prophets are superior to earthly angels. He then presents the two opposing camps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: first a clarification of scope, then the two positions.
Pay attention to the groups mentioned.
2
Identify the two positions
List which groups hold that prophets are superior and which hold that angels are superior. Use the key terms to identify them.
Some Ash'aris are on both sides.
3
Reflect on the scope
Notice that the debate is not about all angels, only the heavenly ones. This is important for understanding the argument that follows.
The lower angels are not in dispute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة
في تفضيل الأنبياء على الملائكة: لا نزاع في أنها أفضل من الملائكة السفلية (الأرضية) إنما النزاع في الملائكة العلوية (السماوية) فقال: أكثر أصحابنا الأنبياء أفضل وعليه الشيعة وأكثر أهل الملل. وقالت المعتزلة: وأبو عبد الله الحليمي والقاضي أبو بكر منا الملائكة أفضل، وعليه الفلاسفة.
The first observatory - the eighth topic: The preference of prophets over angels. Regarding the preference of prophets over angels: there is no dispute that they are better than the lower (earthly) angels; rather the dispute is about the higher (heavenly) angels. He said: Most of our companions (Ash'aris) hold that prophets are better, and upon this are the Shia and most people of the religions. And the Mu'tazila, and Abu Abdullah al-Halimi, and al-Qadi Abu Bakr from us (Ash'aris) hold that angels are better, and upon this are the philosophers.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage states the disagreement over whether prophets or angels are superior. The author first clarifies that the debate concerns only the heavenly angels, as all agree that prophets are superior to earthly angels. He then lists the two main positions: the majority of Ash'aris, the Shia, and most religious groups hold that prophets are better; the Mu'tazila, along with two Ash'ari figures (al-Halimi and al-Qadi Abu Bakr), and the philosophers hold that angels are better. The commentary sets up the arguments that follow.

C) The Mu'tazila and some Ash'aris
D) All Muslims

Key Takeaway
The dispute on prophetic superiority is limited to heavenly angels, with the majority of Ash'aris siding with prophets and the Mu'tazila siding with angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the debate over whether prophets or angels are better, do you find yourself instinctively siding with one opinion? Could it be that your leaning is influenced more by the group you belong to than by the evidence from revelation? Are you willing to examine your own heart and ask: if the truth were opposite to what your group holds, would you have the courage to follow it?
?
The source shows that even within the Ash'ari school, there was disagreement. Have you ever assumed that your scholars or community are unanimous on matters of faith? How does this reminder that reputable scholars differed affect your humility when holding your own theological views? Will you now be more hesitant to dismiss those who disagree with you?
?
This lesson makes a careful distinction: the dispute is only about heavenly angels, not earthly ones. Do you tend to be sloppy in your understanding of religious issues, lumping things together without precision? What step can you take this week to study one theological issue with the same level of care and accuracy as the scholars who wrote these works?

The Philosophers' Theory of Reincarnation and Its Types
Al-Jurjani presents the reincarnationist theory with its four types of soul transmigration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reincarnationist theory among the philosophers, including the four types of soul transmigration and their classification as punishment or perfection.

Core Idea
Reincarnationists believe souls wander through bodies until they become perfect.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the view of a group of philosophers who advocate reincarnation (tanasukh). They distinguish between perfect souls that become free and imperfect souls that must transmigrate through various bodies until they attain perfection. The passage details the types of transmigration: naskh, maskh, raskh, and faskh, which are seen as levels of punishment for the descending souls, while ascending souls may eventually become free or attach to celestial bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall structure. Then read the translation to understand the key points.
Notice the distinction between perfect and imperfect souls.
2
Identify the four types of transmigration
Find the terms naskh, maskh, raskh, and faskh in the passage. Note what each type involves and what kind of body the soul moves to.
The passage gives examples like lion for brave, rabbit for cowardly.
3
Understand the purpose of transmigration
Consider why the reincarnationists believe souls undergo these transfers. The passage mentions that it is for perfection or as punishment.
4
Reflect on the author's implicit criticism
Although the passage mostly presents the theory, the author will later criticize it as conjecture. For now, just understand the theory.
The theory is based on the pre-eternity and immateriality of souls, which the author says have been refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا ما عليه جمهورهم وقال قوم منهم ) أي من الفلاسفة (وهم أهل التناسخ : إنما تبقى مجردة) عن الأبدان ( النفوس الكاملة التي أخرجت قوتها إلى الفعل) ولم يبق شيء من الكمالات الممكنة لها بالقوة فصارت طاهرة عن جميع العلائق الجسمانية، وتخلصت إلى عالم القدس ، ( وأما ) النفوس ( الناقصة التي بقي شيء من كمالاتها بالقوة ) فإنها تتردد في الأبدان الإنسانية وتنتقل من بدن إلى بدن آخر حتى تبلغ النهاية فيما هو كمالها من علومها وأخلاقها، فحينئذ تبقى مجردة مطهرة عن التعلق بالأبدان ( ويسمى ) هذا الانتقال ( نسخاً وقيل: ربما تنازلت إلى ( الأبدان ( الحيوانية) فتنتقل من البدن الإنساني إلى بدن حيواني يناسبه في الأوصاف كبدن الأسد للشجاع والأرنب للجبان (ويسمى مسخاً، وقيل: ) ربما تنازلت (إلى) الأجسام ( النباتية ويسمى رسخاً وقيل : إلى الجمادية) كالمعادن والبسائط أيضاً. ويسمى فسخاً قالوا وهذه التنازلات المذكورة هي مراتب العقوبات وإليها الإشارة بما ورد من الدركات الضيقة في جهنم ( هذا في المتنازلة أو أما المتصاعدة ) من مرتبة إلى ما هو أكمل منها ( فقد تتخلص من الأبدان ) كلها ) لصيرورتها كاملة ) في جميع صفاتها، كما مر ، وقد تتعلق ببعض الأجرام السماوية لبقاء حاجتها إلى الاستكمال ولا يخفى أن ذلك كله رجم بالظن بناء على قدم النفوس وتجردها وقد أبطلناهما قال الإمام الرازي: وأما القائلون بالمعاد الروحاني والجسماني معاً فقد أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين الحكمة والشريعة، فقالوا : دل العقل على أن سعادة الأرواح بمعرفة الله تعالى ،ومحبته وأن سعادة الأجسام في إدراك المحسوسات والجمع بين هاتين السعادتين في هذه الحياة غير ممكن لأن الإنسان حتى لا يلحقها تكثر وتغير بوجه من الوجوه وكيف نسبة ترتب الموجودات إليها ثم كلما ازداد النظر استبصاراً ازداد للسعادة استعداداً .
This is the position of the majority of them. And a group of them – i.e., of the philosophers – who are the reincarnationists said: Only the perfect souls that have actualized their potential and have nothing left of possible perfections in potentiality remain disembodied from bodies, having become pure from all corporeal attachments and having reached the holy world. As for the imperfect souls that have some of their perfections remaining in potentiality, they wander among human bodies and transfer from one body to another until they reach the end in what is their perfection of sciences and character. Then they remain disembodied, purified from attachment to bodies. This transfer is called 'naskh'. It is said that sometimes they descend to animal bodies, transferring from a human body to an animal body that corresponds to them in qualities, like the body of a lion for the courageous and the rabbit for the cowardly, and it is called 'maskh'. It is said that sometimes they descend to plant bodies, and it is called 'raskh', and sometimes to mineral bodies like metals and simples, and it is called 'faskh'. They said: these aforementioned descents are levels of punishments, and they refer to what is reported about the narrow depths in Hell. This is regarding the descending ones. As for the ascending ones, from one level to what is more perfect, they may completely free themselves from bodies by becoming perfect in all attributes, as mentioned, and they may attach to some celestial bodies due to their remaining need for perfection. And it is not hidden that all of this is casting conjecture based on the pre-eternity of souls and their immateriality, and we have refuted these two [premises]. Imam al-Razi said: As for those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection together, they intended to reconcile wisdom and religious law. They said: Reason indicates that the felicity of spirits lies in knowledge of God and love of Him, and the felicity of bodies lies in perceiving sensory things, and combining these two felicities in this life is impossible because man... so that multiplicity and change do not affect it in any way, and how the order of existents is related to it. Then the more one increases in insight through reflection, the more one increases in preparedness for felicity.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the views of philosophers on the afterlife after summarizing the earlier discussion on soul states. It first states the position of the majority of philosophers (جمهورهم) – likely referring to the Peripatetic view that souls remain in their state. Then it introduces a dissenting group, the reincarnationists (أهل التناسخ). They believe that only perfect souls (those that have actualized all their potential) become disembodied and reach the holy world. Imperfect souls continue to transmigrate through human bodies until they become perfect. The passage then details the types of transmigration: نسخ (naskh) – transfer to another human body; مسخ (maskh) – descent to an animal body corresponding to one's qualities; رسخ (raskh) – descent to a plant body; فسخ (faskh) – descent to a mineral body. These are considered levels of punishment (مراتب العقوبات), alluded to in religious texts about the 'narrow depths' of Hell. For ascending souls (المتصاعدة) that become perfect, they either become completely free from bodies or attach to celestial bodies if they still need perfection. The author criticizes this entire theory as pure conjecture (رجم بالظن) based on two false premises: the pre-eternity of souls (قدم النفوس) and their immateriality (تجردها), which have already been refuted. Finally, it quotes Imam al-Razi who mentions those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection, attempting to harmonize philosophy and sharia. They argue that reason shows spiritual felicity is in knowledge and love of God, while bodily felicity is in sensory perception, and the combination is impossible in this life. The quote from al-Razi is incomplete but emphasizes that increasing insight increases preparedness for felicity.

Key Terms
جمهور
تناسخ
نسخ
مسخ
رسخ
فسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reincarnationists, what happens to an imperfect soul that has some perfections remaining?
A) It remains disembodied forever.
B) It transfers to another human body until it becomes perfect.
C) It descends to an animal body immediately.
D) It is annihilated.

Key Takeaway
The reincarnationist theory posits that imperfect souls transmigrate through human, animal, plant, and mineral bodies until they attain perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The reincarnationists' theory suggests that imperfect souls are trapped in bodies until they perfect themselves. What if this were true for your soul right now? What specific imperfections would you need to shed before you could be free? Are you actively working to eliminate them, or are you comfortable in your current state?
?
This passage describes souls descending to animal bodies based on their qualities—courage or cowardice. If you examined your character today, which animal would your soul be most suited to? Does that thought disturb you enough to change?
?
The ascension of souls requires actualizing all potential perfections. What one 'scienc' (knowledge) or 'character' (moral trait) are you still leaving in potential? What concrete step will you take this week to actualize it?

The Critique of Reincarnation and the Attempt to Harmonize Wisdom and Revelation
Al-Jurjani criticizes reincarnation and cites al-Razi on the dual resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's criticism of the reincarnation theory as conjecture and Imam al-Razi's reference to those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Reincarnation is baseless conjecture; true resurrection involves both spiritual and bodily felicity.

This lesson presents the author's sharp critique of the entire reincarnationist framework, dismissing it as mere conjecture built on false premises of the pre-eternity and immateriality of souls, which he claims have already been refuted. It then introduces Imam al-Razi's mention of those who try to reconcile philosophy and sharia by affirming both spiritual and bodily resurrection, emphasizing that spiritual felicity lies in knowledge of God and bodily felicity in sensory perception, and that the combination is not possible in this life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the shift from presenting the theory to criticizing it.
The phrase 'wa la yakhfa' indicates the author's evaluation.
2
Identify the author's criticism
Find the sentence where the author says this is all 'rajm bil-zann' (casting conjecture) and on what basis (pre-eternity and immateriality of souls).
The author says these two premises have already been refuted.
3
Understand al-Razi's mention
Read the quote from Imam al-Razi. He talks about those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection and their argument.
The quote is incomplete but gives the gist of their position.
4
Consider the contrast
Think about why the author includes al-Razi's mention after his own critique. Is he endorsing it or just reporting?
Al-Razi's mention might be a positive alternative to the reincarnation theory.

This is the position of the majority of them. And a group of them – i.e., of the philosophers – who are the reincarnationists said: Only the perfect souls that have actualized their potential and have nothing left of possible perfections in potentiality remain disembodied from bodies, having become pure from all corporeal attachments and having reached the holy world. It first states the position of the majority of philosophers (جمهورهم) – likely referring to the Peripatetic view that souls remain in their state. Then it introduces a dissenting group, the reincarnationists (أهل التناسخ). They believe that only perfect souls (those that have actualized all their potential) become disembodied and reach the holy world. Imperfect souls continue to transmigrate through human bodies until they become perfect. The passage then details the types of transmigration: نسخ (naskh) – transfer to another human body; مسخ (maskh) – descent to an animal body corresponding to one's qualities; رسخ (raskh) – descent to a plant body; فسخ (faskh) – descent to a mineral body. These are considered levels of punishment (مراتب العقوبات), alluded to in religious texts about the 'narrow depths' of Hell. For ascending souls (المتصاعدة) that become perfect, they either become completely free from bodies or attach to celestial bodies if they still need perfection. The author criticizes this entire theory as pure conjecture (رجم بالظن) based on two false premises: the pre-eternity of souls (قدم النفوس) and their immateriality (تجردها), which have already been refuted. Finally, it quotes Imam al-Razi who mentions those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection, attempting to harmonize philosophy and sharia. They argue that reason shows spiritual felicity is in knowledge and love of God, while bodily felicity is in sensory perception, and the combination is impossible in this life. The quote from al-Razi is incomplete but emphasizes that increasing insight increases preparedness for felicity.

Key Terms
قدم النفوس
تجرد
معاد روحاني
معاد جسماني
رجم بالظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is supporting reincarnation.
Correction: Al-Razi is presenting a different view that affirms both spiritual and bodily resurrection, not reincarnation.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the author claim the reincarnation theory is built?
A) The pre-eternity of souls and their immateriality
B) The belief in bodily resurrection
C) The authority of religious texts
D) The majority of philosophers

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani rejects reincarnation as conjecture, while al-Razi mentions a view that combines spiritual and bodily resurrection as a synthesis of philosophy and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi says spiritual felicity is in knowing and loving God, and bodily felicity is in sensory perception. Which one do you prioritize more in your daily choices—and are you neglecting the other? How will you balance them starting now?
?
The author states that the two premises of reincarnation have been refuted. Have you ever critically examined the foundations of your own beliefs about the afterlife, or have you accepted them without proof? What one step will you take this week to move from conjecture to certainty?

The Doctrine of Metempsychosis and Its Four Types
Philosophers' view of metempsychosis and its four levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' doctrine of metempsychosis, its four subdivisions (naskh, maskh, raskh, faskh), and why Islamic theologians reject it as conjecture.

Core Idea
Metempsychosis imagines souls cycling through bodies—human, animal, plant, mineral—until purified.

This passage presents a philosophical theory of transmigration, detailing how souls allegedly move through different levels of creation based on their perfection. The author then dismisses the entire framework as baseless conjecture built on false premises about the soul's pre-eternity and incorporeality, which have already been refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the full theory of metempsychosis.
Pay attention to the distinction between perfect and imperfect souls.
2
Identify the soul types
Note how the passage divides souls into perfect and imperfect. Perfect souls are freed from bodies; imperfect souls transmigrate.
3
List the four types of transmigration
Identify the four Arabic terms: naskh (human to human), maskh (human to animal), raskh (human to plant), faskh (human to inanimate). Understand their meanings and examples.
These are considered levels of punishment for the descending souls.
4
Understand the refutation
Notice the concluding statement: the author dismisses all this as conjecture based on false premises (pre-eternity and incorporeality of souls) that were already refuted.
This shows the theological stance: the doctrine is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم منهم ) أي من الفلاسفة (وهم أهل التناسخ : إنما تبقى مجردة) عن الأبدان ( النفوس الكاملة التي أخرجت قوتها إلى الفعل) ولم يبق شيء من الكمالات الممكنة لها بالقوة فصارت طاهرة عن جميع العلائق الجسمانية، وتخلصت إلى عالم القدس ، ( وأما ) النفوس ( الناقصة التي بقي شيء من كمالاتها بالقوة ) فإنها تتردد في الأبدان الإنسانية وتنتقل من بدن إلى بدن آخر حتى تبلغ النهاية فيما هو كمالها من علومها وأخلاقها، فحينئذ تبقى مجردة مطهرة عن التعلق بالأبدان ( ويسمى ) هذا الانتقال ( نسخاً وقيل: ربما تنازلت إلى ( الأبدان ( الحيوانية) فتنتقل من البدن الإنساني إلى بدن حيواني يناسبه في الأوصاف كبدن الأسد للشجاع والأرنب للجبان (ويسمى مسخاً، وقيل: ) ربما تنازلت (إلى) الأجسام ( النباتية ويسمى رسخاً وقيل : إلى الجمادية) كالمعادن والبسائط أيضاً. ويسمى فسخاً قالوا وهذه التنازلات المذكورة هي مراتب العقوبات وإليها الإشارة بما ورد من الدركات الضيقة في جهنم ( هذا في المتنازلة أو أما المتصاعدة ) من مرتبة إلى ما هو أكمل منها ( فقد تتخلص من الأبدان ) كلها ) لصيرورتها كاملة ) في جميع صفاتها، كما مر ، وقد تتعلق ببعض الأجرام السماوية لبقاء حاجتها إلى الاستكمال ولا يخفى أن ذلك كله رجم بالظن بناء على قدم النفوس وتجردها وقد أبطلناهما
And a group among them, who are the proponents of metempsychosis, said: Only the perfect souls remain stripped of bodies, those who have actualized their potential and nothing of their possible perfections remains in potentiality, so they become pure from all physical attachments and attain to the world of sanctity. As for the imperfect souls in which something of their perfections remains in potentiality, they wander among human bodies and transfer from one body to another until they reach the end regarding what is their perfection in knowledge and morals; then they remain stripped and purified from attachment to bodies. This transfer is called naskh. It is said: Sometimes they descend to animal bodies, transferring from a human body to an animal body that matches them in attributes, like the body of a lion for the brave and the body of a rabbit for the cowardly. This is called maskh. It is said: Sometimes they descend to plant bodies, called raskh. And it is said to inanimate bodies, like minerals and simple substances, called faskh. They said: These descents mentioned are the levels of punishments, and to them refers what has been reported about the confined depths in Hell. This is regarding the descending ones. As for the ascending ones from a rank to what is more perfect, they may completely obtain release from bodies due to their becoming perfect in all their attributes, as previously mentioned, and they may attach to some celestial bodies due to the remaining need for perfection. And it is not hidden that all of this is conjecture based on the pre-eternity of souls and their incorporeality, which we have already refuted.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of metempsychosis (tanāsukh) among some philosophers. It distinguishes between perfect and imperfect souls. Perfect souls remain stripped. Imperfect souls transmigrate through human bodies until they perfect themselves. Then it enumerates four types of descent to lower bodies: naskh (human to human), maskh (human to animal), raskh (human to plant), and faskh (human to inanimate). These are considered punishments. Then it mentions ascending souls that may become free. Finally, the author refutes these ideas as baseless conjecture (rajm bi-l-ẓann) based on the false premises of pre-eternity and incorporeality of souls, which have already been refuted.

Correction: The passage shows that these are part of a philosophical system that the author rejects as conjecture, not Islamic doctrine.

The Absolute Deniers of Resurrection
Reports the view of those who deny all resurrection, claiming the soul is a mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of those who deny resurrection absolutely, including their definition of the soul and its implication for afterlife.

Core Idea
If the soul is a mixture, it dies with the body; re-creation of nothing is impossible.

Among the various views on resurrection, some philosophers denied it entirely, arguing that the soul is nothing more than a temporary blend of bodily elements. This lesson introduces their core claim and the logical consequence that makes resurrection impossible in their framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Focus on the key claim: 'al-nafs hiya al-mizāj'.
2
Identify the group and their premise
Note that the passage explicitly names 'those who deny resurrection absolutely' and states their definition of the soul as the temperament.
3
Understand the consequence
If the soul is identical with the temperament, then when the body dies, the temperament dissolves and the soul ceases to exist. Resurrection would require re-creating what no longer exists, which they deem impossible.
This shows why they deny both bodily and spiritual resurrection.
4
Connect to the broader topic
Consider how this position contrasts with other views that affirm resurrection. It represents the most extreme denial in the discussion of sam'iyyāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنكرون للمعاد مطلقاً هي فهم الذين قالوا : النفس هي المزاج فإذا مات الإنسان فقد عدمت النفس وإعادة المعدوم عندهم محال
As for those who deny resurrection absolutely, they are the ones who said: The soul is the temperament. So when a person dies, the soul becomes non-existent. And the re-creation of the non-existent is impossible according to them.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This short passage identifies a group that denies any form of resurrection. They define the soul as a mixture (mizāj) of bodily elements. Upon death, the mixture dissolves and the soul ceases to exist. Since they consider re-creation of the non-existent impossible, they deny both spiritual and bodily resurrection. This represents the most extreme denial among the philosophical positions discussed.

إعادة المعدوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This group is often confused with materialists who deny survival after death without a specific argument.
Correction: Their argument is based on defining the soul as a temperament, which dissolves at death, making re-creation logically impossible. They are not simply materialists but have a specific metaphysical claim about the nature of the soul.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The absolute deniers of resurrection define the soul as a transient mixture (mizāj) that ends at death, making resurrection impossible.

The First Proof: Threat Requires Actual Punishment
First proof of the Mu'tazila for obligatory punishment and its Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first proof for obligatory punishment and why the Ash'aris distinguish between occurrence and obligation.

Core Idea
A threat's fulfillment does not require its obligation.

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij argued that God's explicit threats of punishment for major sins must be carried out, otherwise His word would be broken. The Ash'ari response reframes the argument, showing that the threat can still be fulfilled even if punishment is not obligatory. Let's see how the first proof and its answer unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila argue that if God does not punish, it entails khulf (breach) and kadhib (lying).
Notice they equate lack of punishment with forgiveness.
2
Examine the Ash'ari answer
The answer says 'its outcome is the occurrence of punishment—so where is its obligation?' Understand why this is a grammatical shift.
The answer denies that absence of obligation implies absence of occurrence.
3
Consider the counter-objection
The text includes a potential objection: if non-obligation allows possibility of breach, that is also impossible. Read the response denying that impossibility.
The response asserts that such actions are within God's power.

Source Text

خلفاً ولا كذباً، لا يقال: إنه يستلزم جوازهما وهو أيضاً محال لأنا نقول : استحالته ممنوعة كيف وهما من الممكنات التي تشملها قدرته تعالى (الثاني: أنه إذا علم المذنب ) أي المرتكب للكبيرة ( أنه لا يعاقب على ذنبه ) بل يعفى عنه لم ينزجر عن الذنب بل ( كان ذلك تقريراً له على ذنبه ) وعدم التوبة عنه ( و ) كان ( إغراء للغير عليه وإنه قبيح مناف لمقصود الدعوة ) إلى الطاعات وترك المنهيات ( الجواب : منع تضمنه ) أي تضمن عدم وجود العقاب ( للتقرير والإغراء، إذ شمول الوعيد وتعريض الكل للعقاب وظن الوفاء بالوعيد فيه من الزجر والردع ما لا يخفى، واحتمال العفو عن البعض احتمالاً مرجواً لا ينافي ذلك)، يعني أن الوعيد عام
