Positions on the Obligation of Appointing an Imam
Enumeration of different sectarian views on whether appointing an Imam is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various sectarian positions on whether appointing an Imam is obligatory, and the basis (reason or revelation) each group cites.

Core Idea
The obligation of appointing an Imam is debated: some say by reason, others by revelation, still others upon God.

In this passage, al-Iji surveys the key Islamic sects and their stances on the necessity of the Imamate. The Ash'ari position is that it is obligatory by revelation; others differ. Understanding these views is essential for the broader discussion of Imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the different groups listed.
The passage is a straightforward enumeration.
2
Identify each group and their stance
List each sect or scholar mentioned and what they say about the obligation of appointing an Imam.
3
Note the basis each group cites
Observe whether the obligation is said to be by reason ('aql), revelation (sam'), both, or upon God.
Some groups say it is obligatory upon the community, others upon God.
4
Reflect on the Khawarij subdivision
The Khawarij have internal differences. Notice the two sub-positions and the condition of security vs. fitna.
This shows that even within a group there is variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فتقول : قد اختلفوا في أن نصب الإمام عندنا واجب علينا سمعاً وقالت المعتزلة والزيدية بل عقلاً وقال الجاحظ : والكعبي وأبو الحسين من المعتزلة بل عقلاً وسمعاً معاً وقالت الإمامية والإسماعيلية : لا يجب نصب الإمام علينا بل على الله سبحانه إلا أن الإمامية أوجبوه عليه لحفظ قوانين الشرع عن التغيير بالزيادة والنقصان والإسماعيلية أوجبوه ليكون معرفاً لله وصفاته على مامر من أنه لا بد عندهم في معرفته من معلم وقالت الخوارج : لا يجب نصب الإمام أصلا بل هو من الجائزات ومنهم من فصل، فقال بعضهم : كهشام الغوطي وأتباعه يجب عند الأمن دون الفتنة، وقال قوم : كأبي بكر الأصم وتابعيه بالعكس أي يجب عند الفتنة دون الأمن . قوله : (كهشام الغوطي) منسوب إلى غوطة بالضم وهي موضع بالشام كثير الماء والشجر .
When you have understood this, you say: They differed concerning the appointment of the Imam. In our [Ash'ari] view, it is obligatory upon us by revelation (sam'an). The Mu'tazila and Zaydiyya said: rather, by reason ('aqlan). Al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn from the Mu'tazila said: rather, by reason and revelation together. The Imamiyya and Isma'iliyya said: it is not obligatory upon us to appoint the Imam, but upon God, exalted, except that the Imamiyya made it obligatory upon Him to preserve the laws of the religion from alteration by increase or decrease, and the Isma'iliyya made it obligatory upon Him so that he would be a guide to God and His attributes, as has preceded that they require a teacher for knowledge of Him. The Khawarij said: appointing the Imam is not obligatory at all, but is among permissible things. Some of them made a distinction: some, like Hisham al-Ghuti and his followers, said it is obligatory in times of security but not in times of fitna. Others, like Abu Bakr al-Asamm and his followers, said the opposite: obligatory in times of fitna but not in times of security. His statement: (like Hisham al-Ghuti) is attributed to Ghuta, a place in Syria abundant in water and trees.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage enumerates the different positions on whether appointing an Imam is obligatory. The Ash'ari position (our school) is that it is obligatory upon the community by revelation (sam'an), meaning by textual evidence. The Mu'tazila and Zaydiyya hold it obligatory by reason ('aqlan) alone. Some Mu'tazila like al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn hold it obligatory by both reason and revelation. The Imamiyya (Twelver Shi'a) and Isma'iliyya hold that it is not obligatory upon the community but upon God; however, they differ on why: the Imamiyya say to preserve the religious laws, the Isma'iliyya say to serve as a guide for knowing God. The Khawarij hold it not obligatory at all, only permissible. Some within Khawarij distinguish: Hisham al-Ghuti says obligatory in times of security but not fitna; Abu Bakr al-Asamm says the opposite. The marginal note explains that Hisham al-Ghuti is attributed to Ghuta, a region in Syria.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'aris deny reason entirely in proving Imamate, but they accept reason in other matters; here they say the obligation is known by revelation only.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that the obligation is established by revelation (sam'an), not that reason plays no role; reason may still be used in other aspects of Imamate.

Checkpoint
Which group holds that appointing an Imam is obligatory by both reason and revelation?
A) The Mu'tazila and Zaydiyya
B) Al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn
C) The Imamiyya
D) The Khawarij

Key Takeaway
The obligation of appointing an Imam is a matter of ijtihad among sects, with each group basing its view on reason, revelation, or a combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'aris say appointing an imam is obligatory by revelation. Do you treat the choice of your community's leader as a religious duty, or do you leave it to 'politics'? What does your level of engagement with leadership issues say about your faith in practice?
?
The Isma'iliyya believe God must provide an imam as a guide. When you feel lost in your faith, do you seek a human teacher, or do you rely solely on your own reading? What would it cost you to admit you need a guide?
?
Some Khawarij say appointing an imam is not obligatory at all—just permissible. Have you ever used the excuse that something is 'optional' to avoid a difficult responsibility? What might you be sacrificing by treating collective duties as optional?

Different Opinions on the Obligation of Appointing an Imam
Classifies the views of the Ismailis, Khawarij, and others on imam obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of classical Islamic positions on the obligation of appointing an imam, including the views of the Ismailis, Khawarij, and those who made conditional distinctions.

Core Idea
The obligation of appointing an imam was debated—some saw it as necessary, others as merely permissible.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces the first objective of the fourth observatory: the obligation of appointing an imam. It surveys the positions held by various Islamic sects, from the Ismailis who considered it a religious necessity to the Khawarij who deemed it merely permissible. The passage also highlights nuanced views that condition the obligation on societal circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation to grasp the overall classification.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam manuals.
2
Identify the three broad positions
The passage presents three main positions: the Ismaili view (obligatory), the Khawarij view (not obligatory), and the views of those who distinguish between safety and tribulation.
There are sub-views within the last group: Hisham al-Ghuti and Abu Bakr al-Asamm take opposite conditions.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each
Consider why each sect holds its view. The Ismailis base their obligation on the need for a teacher (mu'allim) to know God, while the Khawarij consider imamate a permissible matter.
The reasoning is embedded in the phrase 'so that [the imam] would be a definer of God and His attributes' for the Ismailis.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about how these differing opinions reflect broader theological and political differences among Islamic sects.
This passage is a good example of how kalam addresses practical issues like leadership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : في وجوب نصب الإمام
لحفظ قوانين الشرع ) عن التغيير بالزيادة والنقصان ( والإسماعيلية) أوجبوه (ليكون معرفاً لله وصفاته على ما مر من أنه لا بد عندهم في معرفته من معلم (وقالت الخوارج : لا يجب ) نصب الإمام ( أصلا ) بل هو من الجائزات ( ومنهم من فصل، فقال بعضهم : ( كهشام الغوطي وأتباعه ) يجب عند الأمن دون الفتنة، وقال قوم : ) كأبي بكر الأصم وتابعيه ( بالعكس ) أي يجب عند الفتنة دون الأمن
قوله : (كهشام الغوطي) منسوب إلى غوطة بالضم وهي موضع بالشام كثير الماء والشجر .
The Fourth Observatory - The First Objective: On the Obligation of Appointing the Imam. For the preservation of the laws of the Sharia from alteration by addition and subtraction. And the Ismailis made it obligatory so that [the imam] would be a definer of God and His attributes, according to what has passed that they require a teacher for His knowledge. And the Khawarij said: The appointment of an imam is not obligatory at all, rather it is among the permissible things. And among them are those who made a distinction: some of them said, like Hisham al-Ghuti and his followers, that it is obligatory in times of safety but not during tribulation. And some people said, like Abu Bakr al-Asamm and his followers, the opposite: that it is obligatory during tribulation but not during safety. His statement: (like Hisham al-Ghuti) attributive to Ghuta with a damma, which is a place in Syria with much water and trees.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage defines the issue and classifies the different positions on the obligation of appointing an imam. It starts by stating the objective: the obligation of appointing an imam. It mentions the Ismailis who obligate it for a specific reason: the need for a teacher to know God. Then the Khawarij who say it is not obligatory but permissible. Then some groups make distinctions based on conditions of safety or tribulation. The gloss explains the name Hisham al-Ghuti.

Key Takeaway
There was no single consensus on the obligation of appointing an imam; opinions ranged from obligatory to permissible, with some conditioning it on social stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Khawarij considered imamate merely permissible. Does that challenge your assumptions about them? Have you ever dismissed a sect's views without actually studying their reasoning? What would it take for you to respectfully consider a position you initially reject?

The First Objection: People Are Self-Sufficient
First objection: sufficiency of people and the Bedouin example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection raised by those who deny the obligation of appointing an imam, namely that people naturally cooperate for their interests without needing an imam.

Core Idea
People's natural cooperation makes an imam seem unnecessary.

The opponents of the obligation of the imam begin by arguing that human societies are naturally ordered. People tend to cooperate for their worldly and religious needs, driven by their innate dispositions and faith. To illustrate this, they point to the Bedouins, who live without a central ruler yet manage their affairs. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection and its translation. Note the key terms.
The phrase 'توفر الناس' means sufficiency.
2
Identify the premise
The opponents claim that people suffice for their own worldly and religious interests. What evidence do they provide?
They mention nature (طباع) and religion (أديان).
3
Examine the example
The example of the Bedouins (العربان والبوادي) is used to show that societies can function without an imam. Do you think this is a strong analogy?
The commentator explains the terms.
4
Reflect on the argument
Consider why the opponents think this objection undermines the necessity of an imam. What is their underlying assumption about society?

احتج المانع من وجوب نصبه ( بوجوه : ( عارض بها دليلنا على وجوبه علينا الأول : توفر الناس على مصالحهم الدنيوية وتعاونهم على أشغالهم الدينية ( مما يحث عليه طباعهم وأديانهم فلا حاجة بهم (إلى نصب من يحكم عليهم فيما يسقلون ،به ويدل عليه أي على ما ذكرناه من عدم الحاجة إليه ) انتظام أحوال العربان والبوادي الخارجين عن حكم السلطان الثاني : الانتفاع بالإمام إنما يكون بالوصول إليه لا يخفى تعذر وصول آحاد الرعية إليه في كل ما يعن لهم من
قوله : ( توفر الناس على مصالحهم من قولهم : توفر عليه أي رعى حرماته . قوله : (العربان والبوادي العربان جمع عرب، والبوادي جمع بادية بمعنى طائفة نازلة بالبادية، وفي الحديث ( من بدا جفا أي من نزل بالبادية صار جافيا .
The denier of its obligation argued with proofs: he opposed our proof for its obligation with: First: The sufficiency of people for their worldly interests and their cooperation in their religious affairs, which their natures and religions urge them to, so they have no need to appoint someone to rule over them in what they find burdensome, and indicates that, i.e. what we mentioned of the lack of need for it, the regularity of the conditions of the Arabs and Bedouins outside the authority of the sultan. Second: The benefit from the imam is only by reaching him, and it is not hidden that it is difficult for individual subjects to reach him in everything that concerns them from...
His statement: (The sufficiency of people for their interests) from their saying: tawaffara 'alayhi, i.e. he respected his sanctities. His statement: (the Arabs and Bedouins) Arabs is plural of Arab, and Bedouins is plural of badiyah meaning a group residing in the desert, and in the hadith: 'Whoever lives in the desert becomes rough,' i.e. whoever settles in the desert becomes uncouth.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage presents the first two objections raised by those who deny the obligation of appointing an imam. The first objection is that people naturally cooperate for their interests without needing an imam; the example of Bedouins shows this. The second objection is the impracticality of accessing the imam for every need. The glosses explain the linguistic meaning of 'توفر' and 'العربان والبوادي'.

Key Terms

العربان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the opponents argue an imam is never needed, but they only deny obligation, not permissibility.
Correction: The objection is against the obligation (وجوب), not against the existence of an imam altogether. The opponents allow that an imam could be beneficial but not compulsory.

Checkpoint
What example do the opponents use to support their claim that people do not need an imam?
A) The city dwellers
B) The Bedouins
C) The scholars
D) The merchants

Key Takeaway
The first objection claims that human nature and religion suffice for social order, using Bedouins as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as though you were self-sufficient in your religious practices, ignoring the guidance of scholars or the community? What led you to that, and what was the result?
?
The objection says people's natures urge them to cooperate. Yet many of us struggle to cooperate even in daily tasks. Why do you think that is? What does that gap reveal about the need for leadership in the Muslim community?
?
Consider the Bedouin example: they lived without a sultan but still had order. Does your own life reflect that kind of natural order, or do you find you need external accountability to stay on the right path? Be honest with yourself.

The Second Objection: Inaccessibility of the Imam
Second objection: impracticality of accessing the imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that the benefit of the imam is impractical because ordinary subjects cannot easily reach him for every need.

Core Idea
An inaccessible imam is of no practical benefit.

The opponents raise a practical concern: even if an imam could theoretically guide society, accessing him for every minor issue is impossible. The passage begins this argument, though it is cut short. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Notice it is incomplete.
The text breaks off at 'من', so we only have the beginning.
2
Understand the premise
The argument is that the benefit from the imam depends on reaching him. Why is that problematic?
The imam is far from the common people.
3
Consider the implication
If the imam cannot be accessed for every need, then the very purpose of having him is defeated. How does this challenge obligation?
It suggests that the imam is not practically useful, so not obligatory.

Answering Objections to Obligation of Appointing an Imam
The author answers two objections against the obligation of nash al-imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two key objections against the obligation to appoint an imam: the objection that worldly affairs can proceed without an imam, and the objection that qualified candidates are rarely found.

Core Idea
Benefit from an imam extends beyond direct access; obligation only exists when qualified candidates are present.

The author addresses two objections that challenge the necessity of appointing an imam. The first claims that worldly affairs do not require an imam, and the second argues that the strict conditions for imamate make it nearly impossible to find a qualified candidate. The author refutes both by showing the broader benefits of an imam's authority and by clarifying when the obligation applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Identify the second objection (worldly affairs don't need an imam) and the third objection (conditions rarely met, leading to either appointing an unqualified person or abandoning duty).
The objections are presented in the beginning of the passage.
2
Study the author's answers
Note how the author answers the second by expanding benefit to include rulings and deputies, and the third by clarifying that obligation only arises when a qualified candidate exists.
The answers start after the sayings.
3
Reflect on the proverbs
Consider the two sayings: the sultan restrains more than the Quran, and the sword does what proof cannot. They reinforce the necessity of worldly authority.
These are cited to support the argument for an imam's necessity.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think about how this defense of the imamate fits into Sunni political theology and the obligation to establish leadership.
This passage is part of a larger discussion on the necessity of the imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمور الدنيوية عادة ) فلا فائدة في نصبه للعامة، فلا يكون واجباً بل جائزاً . ( الثالث : للإمامة شروط قلما توجدفي كل عصر ) وعند ذلك ( فإن أقاموا ) أي الناس (فاقدها لم يأتوا بالواجب ( عليهم بل بغيره ( وإن لم يقيموه ) أي وإن لم يقيموا الفاقد ( فقد تركوا الواجب وإن كان ممكناً عقلاً فممتنع عادة لما يرى من ثوران الفتن والاختلافات عند موت الولاة ولذلك صادفنا العربان والبوادي كالذئاب الشاردة والأسود الضارية لا يبقى بعضهم على بعض ولا يحافظ في الغالب على سنة ولا فرض ) فقد اختل أمرهم في دنياهم وليس تشوفهم أي تطلعهم ( إلى العمل بموجب غالباً ) فيهم بحيث يغنيهم عن رئاسة السلطان عليهم ( ولذلك قيل : ما بزغ السلطان ) أي يكفه ( أكثر مما بزغ القرآن . وقيل ) أيضاً : ( السيف والسنان يفعلان ما لا يفعل البرهان و الجواب ( عن الثاني : لا نسلم أن الانتفاع بالإمام إنما يكون بالوصول إليه ) فقط (بل) ويكون أيضاً ( بوصول أحكامه وسياسته ) إليهم ( ونصبه من يرجعون إليه وعن الثالث : أن تركهم لنصبه لتعذره وعدم شرط الإمامة ليس تركاً للواجب إذ لا وجوب ثمة عليهم على ذلك التقدير إنما الوجوب إذا وجد الجامع لشرائطها، فلا محذور في ذلك الترك
Usually, worldly affairs require no imam for the common people, so appointing him is not obligatory but permissible. Third: The imamate has conditions rarely found in every era, and if they (the people) appoint one lacking them, they have not performed the obligatory duty but something else; if they do not appoint him, they have abandoned the obligatory duty—even if intellectually possible, it is normally impossible due to the outbreak of strife and disagreements at the death of rulers, so we find Arabs and Bedouins like stray wolves and predatory lions, none showing mercy to another, mostly not preserving sunna or obligation. So their worldly affairs are ruined, and their aspiration to act accordingly is not sufficient to dispense with the authority of the sultan. Hence it is said: The sultan restrains more than the Quran does, and it is also said: The sword and spear do what proof cannot do. The answer to the second: We do not accept that benefit from the imam is only through direct access to him; rather, it is also through the access of his rulings and policies to them and his appointment of someone to whom they can refer. And the answer to the third: Their abandoning his appointment due to its difficulty and lack of conditions for imamate is not an abandonment of the obligatory, for there is no obligation upon them in that case; obligation only exists when the one possessing the conditions is found, so there is no harm in that abandonment.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two objections against the obligation of appointing an imam (nash al-imam). The first objection argues that worldly affairs normally proceed without an imam, so there is no benefit in appointing one for the general public; thus it is not obligatory but merely permissible. The second objection states that imamate requires conditions rarely found in every age, and if people appoint someone lacking these conditions, they fail to fulfill their duty, and if they do not appoint anyone, they abandon the duty. The author then gives a related description: if people do not have an imam, chaos ensues, as seen among Bedouins who have no mutual mercy and do not preserve religious practices, so their worldly affairs are ruined. The author cites two sayings: the sultan restrains more than the Quran, and the sword and spear achieve what proof cannot. The answer to the second objection: benefit from an imam is not limited to direct access; his rulings and policies can reach people through deputies he appoints. The answer to the third: if conditions are not met, there is no obligation to appoint a deficient imam; obligation only applies when a qualified candidate exists. Thus abandoning appointment when impossible is not a neglect of duty.

Key Terms
نصب الإمام
شرائط الإمامة
فاقد الشرط
الانتفاع بالإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Benefit from an imam requires direct personal access to him.
Correction: The author states that benefit is also achieved through the imam's rulings and policies reaching people via his appointed deputies.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, how is benefit from an imam achieved?
A) Only by direct access to the imam
B) Through the imam's rulings and policies reaching the people via deputies
C) Only through the imam's personal presence in battle
D) Through the people's complete independence from the imam

Key Takeaway
The obligation to appoint an imam holds even if not all can access him directly, and it only applies when a qualified candidate exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly benefiting from the spiritual authority in your life—through scholars and leaders—or do you dismiss their guidance because it feels indirect? What does that reveal about your humility?
?
When you face a difficult duty that seems impossible, do you use that as an excuse to abandon it entirely, or do you carefully examine whether the conditions for obligation are truly absent—and if not, what steps must you take?
?
The proverb says the sultan restrains more than the Quran. In your own heart, which has more power to check your desires: the fear of worldly consequences or the fear of Allah? Be honest—what needs to change?

The Lutf Argument and the Dilemma of the Hidden Imam
Proof from lutf and the dilemma of hidden vs manifest imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument that appointing an imam is a divine grace (lutf) and the author's refutation that a hidden imam cannot fulfill that role.

Core Idea
A hidden imam cannot be a divine grace; a manifest imam would be, but is not required.

This lesson introduces one of the key theological debates between Sunni and Shi'a Islam: whether appointing an imam is obligatory upon God as an act of grace. The Shi'a argue that an imam is necessary to guide humanity, but the author counters by distinguishing between the kind of imam that would actually function as grace—a manifest, powerful leader—and the hidden imam that the Shi'a actually believe in. This distinction exposes a contradiction in the Shi'a position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the structure: claim, then refutation.
The passage uses the term 'لطف' (lutf) which is a key concept.
2
Understand the proof from lutf
The Shi'a claim that an imam is a grace because he helps people obey and avoid sin. They then assert that grace is obligatory upon God. Ask yourself: Why would this lead to the obligation of appointing an imam?
3
Identify the author's refutation
The author first denies that grace is obligatory in that sense, then argues that even if it were, the grace only comes through a manifest imam, not a hidden one. Spot the two parts: (1) the manifest imam's functions, (2) the hidden imam's ineffectiveness.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
The author presents a dilemma: the Shi'a obligate a hidden imam (which is not grace) and do not obligate a manifest imam (which would be grace). This implies God is failing to do what is obligatory in the present time—an impossibility. See if you can restate the dilemma in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الموجب ) لنصب الإمام ) على الله بأنه لطف لكون العبد معه أقرب إلى الطاعة وأبعد عن المعصية، واللطف واجب عليه تعالى والجواب : بعد منع وجوب اللطف أن اللطف) الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يحصل بإمام ظاهر (قاهر يرجى ثوابه ويخشى عقابه، يدعو الناس إلى الطاعات ويزجرهم عن المعاصي بإقامة الحدود والقصاص وينتصف للمظلوم من الظالم ) وأنتم لا توجبونه على الله كما في هذا الزمان الذي نحن فيه فالذي توجبونه) وهو الإمام المعصوم المختفي (ليس) بلطف) إذ لا يتصور منه تقريب الناس إلى الصلاح وتبعيدهم عن الفساد ) والذي هو لطف لا توجبونه ) عليه وإلا لزم كونه تعالى في زماننا هذا تاركاً للواجب وهو محال . قوله : ( فالذي يوجبونه ليس بلطف ) أجاب الشيعة بأن وجود الإمام لطف سواء تصرف أو لم يتصرف وتصرفه الظاهر لطف آخر وإنما عدم من جهة العباد وسوء اختيارهم حيث أخافوه وتركوا نصرته ورد أولاً : بأنا لا نسلم أن وجوده بدون التصرف لطف، وثانياً : بأنه ينبغي أن يظهر لأوليائه الذين يبدلون الأرواح والأموال على محبته وليس عندهم منه إلا مجرد الاسم، فإن قيل: لعله ظهر لهم وأنتم غافلون قلنا : عدم ظهوره لهم من العاديات التي لا ارتياب فيها لعاقل كبحر[؟] من المسك.
The obliger argued for the appointment of an imam as a grace (lutf) from God, because with him the servant is closer to obedience and farther from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon Him. The answer, after denying the obligation of grace, is that the grace you mention is only attained through a manifest, dominant imam whose reward is hoped for and whose punishment is feared, who calls people to acts of obedience and restrains them from sins by establishing hadd penalties and retaliation, and who obtains justice for the oppressed from the oppressor. Yet you do not make such an imam obligatory upon God as in this time we are in. So what you make obligatory—the infallible hidden imam—is not a grace, because he cannot conceive of bringing people to righteousness or distancing them from corruption. And what is a grace you do not make obligatory upon Him; otherwise, it would entail that He in our time is abandoning the obligatory, which is impossible. His statement: (So what you obligate is not a grace.) The Shi'a answered that the existence of the imam is a grace whether he acts or not, and his manifest action is another grace; his non-manifestation is due to the people's bad choice because they frightened him and abandoned supporting him. The reply first: we do not accept that his existence without action is a grace; second: he ought to appear to his close supporters who sacrifice lives and wealth for his love, yet they have nothing from him but the name. If it is said: perhaps he has appeared to them and you are unaware, we say: his non-appearance to them is among the customary matters about which no rational person doubts, like a sea[؟] of musk.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the obligation of appointing an imam based on divine grace (lutf) and the refutation, specifically targeting the Shi'a view of a hidden imam. The proof claims that an imam is a grace from God because he helps people obey and avoid sin, and since grace is obligatory upon God, appointing an imam is obligatory. The author responds first by denying that grace is obligatory upon God (a theological premise). Then he argues that even if grace were obligatory, the grace described is only realized through a manifest, powerful imam who can enforce laws and protect rights. The Shi'a obligate a hidden, infallible imam, but such an imam cannot be a grace because he cannot influence people. Conversely, the kind of imam that would be a grace—a manifest one—the Shi'a do not obligate upon God, which would imply God is neglecting an obligation in the present age. The author then includes a more detailed refutation: the Shi'a may respond that the mere existence of the hidden imam is a grace, even without his action. The author rejects this on two grounds: first, existence without action is not a grace; second, the hidden imam should at least appear to his devoted followers, who have only his name. If one claims he appeared to them secretly, the author dismisses this as contrary to evident custom, comparing it to an absurdity.

Key Terms
اللطف
الإمام الظاهر
الإمام المعصوم المختفي
وجوب اللطف على الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of a hidden imam, even without manifest action, constitutes a divine grace.
Correction: The passage shows that grace requires actual influence, which a hidden imam cannot provide, so his existence alone is not a grace.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim is required for an imam to be considered a true lutf?
A. Infallibility
B. Hidden status
C. Manifest power and authority
D. Being chosen by the community

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Shi'a proof from lutf by showing that a hidden imam cannot fulfill the role of grace, forcing a contradiction in their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly obey God more because you believe in an unseen imam, or because of tangible consequences in this world? If your obedience does not change based on that belief, what does that reveal about your faith?
?
The passage shows that a hidden imam cannot actually restrain you from sin. What real, visible influences in your life—like teachers, parents, or laws—actually keep you from disobedience? Are you grateful for them as God’s grace, or do you neglect them?
?
If you were to insist that God must provide a certain type of guidance even when it is ineffective, would you be defending your own reasoning or submitting to God’s actual wisdom? Are you willing to admit that your assumptions about what God owes you may be wrong?

Refuting the Shi'a Response: Existence Without Action Is Not Grace
Refutation of Shi'a response that existence alone is lutf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a counter-argument that the mere existence of the hidden imam is a grace, and the author's two-fold refutation based on the ineffectiveness of unmanifest existence and the lack of appearance to devotees.

Core Idea
Merely existing without action cannot be a grace; grace requires actual benefit.

After the initial refutation, the Shi'a respond by claiming that the very existence of the hidden imam, even without his intervention, is a grace from God. They argue that his non-manifestation is due to people's failure to support him. The author now delivers a two-part rebuttal: first, existence without action is not a recognized grace; second, if the imam wants to help his followers, he should at least appear to them—but he hasn't. Even the possibility of secret appearance is dismissed as contrary to observable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shi'a response and the author's first rebuttal
The Shi'a claim that existence alone is lutf, and the author says we do not accept that without action. Ponder: Why might existence alone be insufficient?
2
Understand the second rebuttal
The author argues that the hidden imam should appear to his devoted followers, but they have only his name. Ask yourself: What does this imply about the imam's ability to function as grace?
3
Examine the objection and final dismissal
It is suggested that perhaps the imam appears secretly to his followers, but the author dismisses this as contrary to usual custom (al-‘ādiyāt). Consider the role of empirical evidence in theological argument.
4
Summarize the argument
Combine the two rebuttals into a coherent critique of the Shi'a position. The hidden imam is neither a grace in itself nor manifest to those who would benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الموجب ) لنصب الإمام ) على الله بأنه لطف لكون العبد معه أقرب إلى الطاعة وأبعد عن المعصية، واللطف واجب عليه تعالى والجواب : بعد منع وجوب اللطف أن اللطف) الذي ذكرتموه ( إنما يحصل بإمام ظاهر (قاهر يرجى ثوابه ويخشى عقابه، يدعو الناس إلى الطاعات ويزجرهم عن المعاصي بإقامة الحدود والقصاص وينتصف للمظلوم من الظالم ) وأنتم لا توجبونه على الله كما في هذا الزمان الذي نحن فيه فالذي توجبونه) وهو الإمام المعصوم المختفي (ليس) بلطف) إذ لا يتصور منه تقريب الناس إلى الصلاح وتبعيدهم عن الفساد ) والذي هو لطف لا توجبونه ) عليه وإلا لزم كونه تعالى في زماننا هذا تاركاً للواجب وهو محال . قوله : ( فالذي يوجبونه ليس بلطف ) أجاب الشيعة بأن وجود الإمام لطف سواء تصرف أو لم يتصرف وتصرفه الظاهر لطف آخر وإنما عدم من جهة العباد وسوء اختيارهم حيث أخافوه وتركوا نصرته ورد أولاً : بأنا لا نسلم أن وجوده بدون التصرف لطف، وثانياً : بأنه ينبغي أن يظهر لأوليائه الذين يبدلون الأرواح والأموال على محبته وليس عندهم منه إلا مجرد الاسم، فإن قيل: لعله ظهر لهم وأنتم غافلون قلنا : عدم ظهوره لهم من العاديات التي لا ارتياب فيها لعاقل كبحر[؟] من المسك.
The obliger argued for the appointment of an imam as a grace (lutf) from God, because with him the servant is closer to obedience and farther from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon Him. The answer, after denying the obligation of grace, is that the grace you mention is only attained through a manifest, dominant imam whose reward is hoped for and whose punishment is feared, who calls people to acts of obedience and restrains them from sins by establishing hadd penalties and retaliation, and who obtains justice for the oppressed from the oppressor. Yet you do not make such an imam obligatory upon God as in this time we are in. So what you make obligatory—the infallible hidden imam—is not a grace, because he cannot conceive of bringing people to righteousness or distancing them from corruption. And what is a grace you do not make obligatory upon Him; otherwise, it would entail that He in our time is abandoning the obligatory, which is impossible. His statement: (So what you obligate is not a grace.) The Shi'a answered that the existence of the imam is a grace whether he acts or not, and his manifest action is another grace; his non-manifestation is due to the people's bad choice because they frightened him and abandoned supporting him. The reply first: we do not accept that his existence without action is a grace; second: he ought to appear to his close supporters who sacrifice lives and wealth for his love, yet they have nothing from him but the name. If it is said: perhaps he has appeared to them and you are unaware, we say: his non-appearance to them is among the customary matters about which no rational person doubts, like a sea[؟] of musk.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the obligation of appointing an imam based on divine grace (lutf) and the refutation, specifically targeting the Shi'a view of a hidden imam. The proof claims that an imam is a grace from God because he helps people obey and avoid sin, and since grace is obligatory upon God, appointing an imam is obligatory. The author responds first by denying that grace is obligatory upon God (a theological premise). Then he argues that even if grace were obligatory, the grace described is only realized through a manifest, powerful imam who can enforce laws and protect rights. The Shi'a obligate a hidden, infallible imam, but such an imam cannot be a grace because he cannot influence people. Conversely, the kind of imam that would be a grace—a manifest one—the Shi'a do not obligate upon God, which would imply God is neglecting an obligation in the present age. The author then includes a more detailed refutation: the Shi'a may respond that the mere existence of the hidden imam is a grace, even without his action. The author rejects this on two grounds: first, existence without action is not a grace; second, the hidden imam should at least appear to his devoted followers, who have only his name. If one claims he appeared to them secretly, the author dismisses this as contrary to evident custom, comparing it to an absurdity.

Key Terms
اللطف
الإمام المعصوم المختفي
التصرف
العاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mere existence of a hidden imam, without any manifest role, can still be a divine grace.
Correction: The author clarifies that grace requires actual influence, which a hidden imam cannot provide, and that if he were a grace, he would appear to his followers.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author gives for rejecting the claim that the hidden imam's existence is a lutf?
A. Because the imam is not infallible
B. Because existence without action is not accepted as lutf
C. Because the imam should be manifest
D. Because the imam appears only to some

Key Takeaway
The hidden imam cannot be a divine grace because he has no manifest action and does not appear even to his closest followers, contradicting the purpose of grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If grace from God must produce a real effect in your obedience, where in your life are you claiming a grace that changes nothing? Will you continue to call it a blessing while your actions remain unchanged?
?
The hidden imam's followers had only his name. What do you hold onto in your religion that is merely a name or idea with no actual manifestation in your deeds or connection to God? How does the author's argument push you to seek what is real and effective?
?
The author dismisses the possibility of secret appearance as irrational. Are you relying on imagined hidden benefits from God instead of the manifest means of guidance He has given you? What would it mean for you to only trust in what is clear and customary in your faith?

Kharijite Argument on Fitna and the Response
Kharijites argue nash al-imam causes fitna; author responds with selection criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Kharijite argument against the obligation of appointing an imam is refuted by establishing clear criteria for selection that prevent fitna.

Core Idea
Fitna from imamate is prevented by prioritizing the most knowledgeable, pious, and oldest.

The Kharijites argued that appointing a leader inevitably causes conflict. Their claim has some plausibility — history shows many disputes over leadership. But does that mean we should abandon the idea entirely? In this passage, the author presents the Kharijite argument and then responds, showing that the potential for disagreement can be managed by objective criteria, thus preserving the obligation of having an imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Kharijite argument
Read the opening of the passage where the Kharijites argue that appointing an imam stirs fitna because of conflicting desires and historical experience.
Notice that they use 'absolute non-obligation' as their position.
2
Observe the author's remark
The author points out that the Kharijite argument actually seems to prove that appointing an imam is impermissible, not just non-obligatory. Why might that be?
This is a rhetorical twist.
3
Read the response
Read the answer: if no disagreement occurs, it's fine; if it does, we prioritize the most knowledgeable, then the most pious, then the oldest.
4
Identify the three criteria
List the three criteria in order: knowledge, piety, age. Reflect on why this order is logical and how it prevents fitna.
These criteria are objective and reduce conflict.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the response fully addresses the Kharijite concern: conflict is resolved by a clear hierarchy, not by abandoning the imamate.
The obligation remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجة الخوارج) على عدم وجوبه مطلقا ( أن نصبه يثير الفتنة لأن الأهواء مختلفة فيدعي كل قوم إمامة شخص وصلوحه لها دون الآخر فيقع التشاجر والتناجز والتجربة شاهدة بذلك ) نعم إن ذلك كان اختار الأمة نصب أمير أو رئيس يتقلد أمورهم ويرتب جيوشهم ويحمي حوزتهم لهم من غير أن يلحقهم بتركه حرج في الشرع ، وأنت خبير بأن هذه الحجة على عدم جواز نصب الإمام أدل منها على عدم وجوبه ) والجواب : أنه ( إن لم يقع اختلاف في نصبه فذاك وإن وقع ( يجب عندنا تقديم الأعلم فإن تساويا فالأورع وإن تساويا فالأسن وبذلك تندفع الفتنة والتخالف )
The argument of the Kharijites for its absolute non-obligation is that its appointment stirs up fitna (strife), because desires differ, so each group claims the imamate for a person and his suitability for it over another, leading to quarrel and combat, and experience testifies to that. Yes, if the community chose to appoint an amir or leader to manage their affairs, organize their armies, and protect their territory without incurring religious sin by abandoning him, (you know that this argument is more indicative of the impermissibility of appointing an imam than of its non-obligation). The answer: if no disagreement occurs in his appointment, then fine; if disagreement occurs, we must put forward the most knowledgeable, and if they are equal, the most pious, and if equal, the oldest; with that, fitna and discord are repelled.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents the Kharijite argument against the obligation of appointing an imam. The Kharijites claim that appointing an imam inevitably causes fitna because different groups have conflicting desires, each claiming their candidate is best, leading to quarrels and fighting. They cite experience as evidence. The author notes that this argument actually seems to prove that appointing an imam is not even permissible (let alone obligatory). The response: if no disagreement arises, then appointment proceeds smoothly. If disagreement does arise, it can be resolved by prioritizing the most knowledgeable candidate; if equal in knowledge, then the most pious; if equal in piety, then the oldest. This method prevents fitna and discord. Thus the potential for conflict does not negate the obligation; rather, proper selection criteria manage conflict.

Key Terms

نصب الإمام
الفتنة
تقديم الأعلم
الأورع
الأسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any disagreement over imamate makes its appointment impermissible.
Correction: Disagreement is normal, but it can be resolved by objective criteria (knowledge, piety, age), so appointment remains obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, what is the first criterion for choosing an imam when disagreement occurs?
A) The most wealthy
B) The most knowledgeable
C) The most pious
D) The oldest

Key Takeaway
The potential for conflict does not negate the obligation to appoint an imam; proper selection criteria (knowledge, piety, age) can resolve disagreements and prevent fitna.

View of the Detailers on Imamate's Obligation
Al-mufassilun's conditional ruling on imamate obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the detailers (al-mufassilun) conditionally obligate the appointment of an imam depending on social and political circumstances, contrasting with absolute views.

Core Idea
Imamate's obligation depends on the situation, not absolute.

Not all scholars agreed that appointing an imam is always obligatory. A group known as al-mufassilun (the detailers) argued that the obligation varies with circumstances. In this passage, the author presents their nuanced position, distinguishing between times of fitna, security, and fear. Understanding this view helps appreciate the diversity of Islamic political thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Note the different scenarios mentioned.
The passage uses 'تارة' (sometimes) to introduce variations.
2
Identify the two main distinctions
The detailers make a distinction between fitna and security, and also between security and fear. Find these two pairs.
They say: in fitna it's not obligatory; in justice and security it is; in peace with fairness, there is no need; in fear, it is obligatory.
3
Consider the attribution to Kharijites
The author notes that this detailed view is attributed to the Kharijites. Think about why this might be controversial.
This shows the author's awareness of sectarian positions.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This passage shows that classical scholars debated the necessity of an imam, not just its identity. Reflect on how this impacts our understanding of Islamic governance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفارقون ) أي المفصلون فقالوا تارة هو ( أي نصب الإمام ) حال الفتنة يزيدها ) إذ ربما قتلوه لاستنكافهم طاعته فلا يجب، وأما في حال العدل والأمن فيجب نصبه إذ هو أقرب إلى إظهار شعائر الإسلام (و) قالوا : (تارة) هو ( حال (الأمن والإنصاف بين الناس ( لا حاجة إليه ) وإنما يجب عند الخوف وظهور الفتن واعلم أن عبارة الكتاب هاهنا، وفي ذكر المذاهب أولاً تدل على أن القائل بالتفصيل من الخوارج وهو مخالف لظاهر عبارتي ( منهم عن الأبكار والنهاية .
As for the dividers (al-mufassilun), they said sometimes it (appointing the imam) increases fitna, for they might kill him due to their refusal to obey him, so it is not obligatory. In a state of justice and security, his appointment is obligatory because it is closer to manifesting the symbols of Islam. And they said: sometimes, in a state of security and fairness among people, there is no need for him, and it is only obligatory in times of fear and outbreak of fitna. Know that the wording of the book here, and in the earlier mention of the schools, indicates that the one who makes this detail is from the Kharijites, which contradicts the apparent meaning of my two expressions (from them about al-Abkar and al-Nihaya).
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents the opinion of those who differentiate (al-mufassilun) regarding the obligation of appointing an imam. They distinguish between times of fitna and times of security. In times of fitna, appointing an imam may increase the fitna, as people might kill him out of defiance, so it is not obligatory. In times of justice and security, appointing an imam is obligatory because it helps manifest the symbols of Islam. Alternatively, they also say that in times of security and fairness among people, there is no need for an imam, and obligation only arises during fear and fitna. The author then notes that the wording of the book here, along with earlier mentions, indicates that this detailed view comes from the Kharijites, which contradicts the author's earlier statements (possibly referring to other works 'al-Abkar' and 'al-Nihaya'). The passage thus records a nuanced position and flag a potential inconsistency in attribution.

Key Terms
الفارقون
المفصلون
حال الفتنة
حال العدل والأمن
إظهار شعائر الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation to appoint an imam is absolute and does not vary with circumstances.
Correction: The detailers argue that the obligation is conditional: in times of fitna it is not obligatory, in security it is obligatory, and sometimes in peace there is no need.

Checkpoint
According to al-mufassilun, in which situation is appointing an imam not obligatory?
A) In times of justice and security
B) In times of fitna, because it may increase the fitna
C) In times of fear and fitna
D) Only when the imam is knowledgeable

Key Takeaway
The detailers teach that the obligation to appoint an imam is not fixed but depends on the state of the community, reflecting a pragmatic approach to political authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader causing division or strife in your community, do you instinctively resist or obey? This passage suggests that in fitna, appointing an imam may not be obligatory. How does that challenge your instinct to either submit or rebel? What would you actually do if you faced such a situation today?
?
This passage teaches that in times of justice and security, having an imam is a means to manifest the symbols of Islam. Look at your own community: does the current leadership truly manifest Islam? If not, what is your personal responsibility to either support change or fulfill those symbols in your own actions? Don't avoid the discomfort.

The First Condition of Imamate: Ijtihad
The majority condition for the imam is that he be a mujtahid in fundamentals and branches of law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first condition for imamate according to the majority: that the imam must be a mujtahid in both usul and furu', and the rationale for this condition.

Core Idea
The imam must be a fully qualified religious scholar, not just a political leader.

This passage opens the section on the conditions of imamate by stating the consensus of the majority: the imam must be a mujtahid. Why is this necessary? Because he must manage religious affairs, resolve doubts, and defend the faith. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice that the text cuts off at the end of the page.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is that the imam must be a mujtahid in both usul (fundamentals) and furu' (branches of law). This means he must be able to derive rulings from the sources.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The purpose of this condition is so that the imam can manage religious affairs, establish proofs, and resolve doubts. Consider why this would be important for leadership.
This connects to the need for the imam to defend the faith and guide the community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : في شروط الإمامة في شروط الإمامة الجمهور على أن أهل (الإمامة ومستحقها من هو ( مجتهد في الأصول والفروع ليقوم بأمور (الدين متمكناً من إقامة الحجج وحل الشبه في
The second goal: On the conditions of imamate. On the conditions of imamate, the majority (jomhour) hold that the one qualified for imamate and deserving it is a mujtahid in the fundamentals and branches (of law), to manage the affairs of religion, capable of establishing proofs and solving doubts in...
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage begins the second section on the conditions of imamate. It states that the majority of scholars (al-jomhour) agree that the person who is eligible for imamate and deserves it must be a mujtahid—one qualified to exercise independent legal reasoning—in both the fundamentals of religion (usul) and its branches (furu'). The purpose is to enable him to manage religious affairs, establish proofs, and resolve doubts. The passage cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page, but the core condition is clear: the imam must possess comprehensive legal expertise.

Correction: The majority condition shows that the imam must be a mujtahid, meaning he must have deep religious knowledge to lead in both spiritual and temporal matters.

Checkpoint

C) Being the eldest in the community

Key Takeaway
The imam must be a mujtahid in both fundamental principles and derived rulings to properly lead the community in religious affairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you encounter doubts or arguments about your faith, are you able to establish proofs from the fundamentals and branches? If not, how does this expose a deficiency in your own preparation for imamate—even if only over your own household?

The Hidden Imam Is Not a Grace
Refutation of the Shi'a claim that the hidden imam is a divine grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians argue that the hidden imam cannot be considered a divine grace (luṭf) and how they refute the Shi'a counterarguments.

Core Idea
A hidden imam who cannot act is no grace at all.

This passage presents a classic Sunni–Shi'a theological debate over the nature of divine grace. The Shi'a claim that the existence of an infallible hidden imam is a grace from God, even if he is not governing. The Sunni refutation challenges this by questioning how a hidden figure can fulfill the purpose of grace—bringing people closer to righteousness. The passage then addresses the Shi'a response and offers two counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sunni claim
Read the opening statement: the hidden imam is not a grace because he cannot bring people near to righteousness or distance them from sin. Ask yourself why mere existence is insufficient for grace.
2
Grasp the Shi'a response
The Shi'a argue that the mere existence of the imam is a grace, whether he acts or not. They blame his absence on the people's bad choices. Notice how they shift the responsibility.
3
Examine the first refutation
The Sunni rejects that existence without action is a grace. This is a key point: grace must be effective in guiding people.
This is the first direct counterargument.
4
Examine the second refutation and final rebuttal
If the imam were a grace, he should appear to his sincere supporters, yet they have only his name. The final rebuttal states that his non-appearance is as obvious as a sea of musk.
The second argument uses the absence of perceived benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالذي توجبونه) وهو الإمام المعصوم المختفي (ليس) بلطف) إذ لا يتصور منه تقريب الناس إلى الصلاح وتبعيدهم عن الفساد ) والذي هو لطف لا توجبونه ) عليه وإلا لزم كونه تعالى في زماننا هذا تاركاً للواجب وهو محال . قوله : ( فالذي يوجبونه ليس بلطف ) أجاب الشيعة بأن وجود الإمام لطف سواء تصرف أو لم يتصرف وتصرفه الظاهر لطف آخر وإنما عدم من جهة العباد وسوء اختيارهم حيث أخافوه وتركوا نصرته ورد أولاً : بأنا لا نسلم أن وجوده بدون التصرف لطف، وثانياً : بأنه ينبغي أن يظهر لأوليائه الذين يبدلون الأرواح والأموال على محبته وليس عندهم منه إلا مجرد الاسم، فإن قيل: لعله ظهر لهم وأنتم غافلون قلنا : عدم ظهوره لهم من العاديات التي لا ارتياب فيها لعاقل كبحر من المسك.
What you obligate [of the imam]—which is the infallible hidden imam—is not a grace (luṭf), because it is not conceivable that he bring people near to righteousness and distance them from corruption. And what is a grace you do not obligate upon Him, otherwise it would require that God in this time of ours be abandoning what is obligatory, which is impossible. Statement: 'So what you obligate is not a grace.' The Shi'a answered that the existence of the imam is a grace whether he acts or does not act, and his apparent action is another grace; it is only absent due to the people's bad choices where they frightened him and left off supporting him. It was refuted: first, by saying we do not concede that his existence without action is a grace; second, that he should appear to his supporters who exchange souls and wealth for love of him, yet they have nothing from him but a name. If it is said: perhaps he appeared to them and you are unaware, we say: his non-appearance to them is among the customary matters about which no intelligent person doubts, like a sea of musk.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shi'a argument that the hidden imam is a divine grace (luṭf). It begins with the Sunni position: the hidden imam cannot be a grace because his existence without actual governing does not bring people closer to obedience or deter them from sin. The Sunni then notes that the Shi'a do not obligate the outward manifestation of the imam on God, yet they claim his hidden existence is a grace, which would imply God is abandoning an obligation if He does not provide a visible imam—impossible. The Shi'a respond that the mere existence of the imam is a grace regardless of his action, and his visible rule would be an additional grace; his absence is due to the people's bad choices. The Sunni refutes this in two steps: first, they reject that mere existence without action is a grace; second, they argue that if the imam is a grace, he should appear to his sincere supporters who are willing to sacrifice everything, yet they have only his name. If the Shi'a suggest he may have appeared secretly, the Sunni responds that his non-appearance is a sensible matter known by custom, like a sea of musk—an obvious fact.

Key Terms
لطف
إمام معصوم
اختفاء
تقريب
تبعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shi'a only consider the imam's existence a grace regardless of its effect.
Correction: The Sunni refutation shows that a grace must be effective in guiding people; mere existence without effect is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the Shi'a claim that the hidden imam's existence is a grace?
A) Existence without action is not a grace
B) The imam appears to his supporters
C) The people are to blame for his absence
D) The imam is not hidden

Key Takeaway
The hidden imam cannot be a divine grace because his hiddenness prevents him from fulfilling the purpose of grace—bringing people closer to righteousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that, like the hidden imam, you claim are beneficial but have no real impact on your daily life? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether those beliefs are truly guiding you closer to Allah, or whether they are just comfortable names you cling to?
?
The passage argues that a grace must actively bring people nearer to righteousness. Think of the 'grace' of having the Qur'an or the Sunnah. Are you allowing these to actively change you, or have you reduced them to a hidden treasure you rarely open? What is one step you can take today to let them actively guide you?
?
If you were to apply the same standard from this passage to your own life—demanding that every claimed blessing from Allah must produce real, observable obedience—what would you have to change or abandon? Can you name one such blessing you have treated as 'hidden in plain sight' without letting it transform you?

Refuting the Khawarij: Appointing an Imam and Preventing Fitna
Refutation of the Khawarij claim that appointing an imam causes fitna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij argument against the obligation of appointing an imam, the response that fitna is prevented through proper selection criteria, and the view of the detailers among the Khawarij.

Core Idea
Proper selection of the imam prevents fitna, refuting the Khawarij claim that appointment causes conflict.

The Khawarij argued that appointing an imam inevitably leads to fitna because people's desires differ. This passage presents their argument and the Sunni response, which explains how fitna is avoided by choosing the most learned, then the most pious, then the eldest. It also mentions a group of Khawarij who held a detailed view depending on circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij argument
Carefully read the first part of the passage where the Khawarij claim that appointing an imam stirs up fitna. Notice how they support this with experience.
The argument is against the obligation (wujūb) of appointment, not its permissibility.
2
Examine the response
The passage then gives the answer: if no disagreement occurs, it's fine; if it occurs, we must select the most learned, then the most pious, then the eldest. This is how fitna is repelled.
The response directly counters the Khawarij objection by providing a method to avoid conflict.
3
Understand the selection criteria
Focus on the hierarchy: aʿlam (most learned), then awraʿ (most pious), then asann (eldest). These criteria ensure unity.
4
Identify the detailers' position
The passage introduces the mufaṣṣilūn (detailers) who say the obligation depends on circumstances: in times of fitna it is not obligatory, but in times of justice and security it is obligatory.
The author notes that these detailers are from the Khawarij, contrary to other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجة الخوارج) على عدم وجوبه مطلقا ( أن نصبه يثير الفتنة لأن الأهواء مختلفة فيدعي كل قوم إمامة شخص وصلوحه لها دون الآخر فيقع التشاجر والتناجز والتجربة شاهدة بذلك ) نعم إن ذلك كان اختار الأمة نصب أمير أو رئيس يتقلد أمورهم ويرتب جيوشهم ويحمي حوزتهم لهم من غير أن يلحقهم بتركه حرج في الشرع ، وأنت خبير بأن هذه الحجة على عدم جواز نصب الإمام أدل منها على عدم وجوبه ) والجواب : أنه ( إن لم يقع اختلاف في نصبه فذاك وإن وقع يجب عندنا تقديم الأعلم فإن تساويا فالأورع وإن تساويا فالأسن وبذلك تندفع الفتنة والتخالف ( وأما الفارقون ) أي المفصلون فقالوا تارة هو ( أي نصب الإمام ) حال الفتنة يزيدها ) إذ ربما قتلوه لاستنكافهم طاعته فلا يجب، وأما في حال العدل والأمن فيجب نصبه إذ هو أقرب إلى إظهار شعائر الإسلام (و) قالوا : (تارة) هو ( حال (الأمن والإنصاف بين الناس ( لا حاجة إليه ) وإنما يجب عند الخوف وظهور الفتن واعلم أن عبارة الكتاب هاهنا، وفي ذكر المذاهب أولاً تدل على أن القائل بالتفصيل من الخوارج وهو مخالف لظاهر عبارتي الأبكار والنهاية .
The argument of the Khawarij) against its obligation absolutely (is that its appointment stirs up fitna because desires differ; each group claims the imamate of a person and his suitability for it over others, so wrangling and conflict occur, and experience bears witness to that. Yes, if that were so, the community would choose to appoint an amir or leader to manage their affairs, arrange their armies, protect their territory, without them incurring religious blame for leaving it. And you realize that this argument is more indicative of the impermissibility of appointing an imam than of its non-obligation. And the answer: that (if no disagreement occurs in his appointment, then that is fine; and if it occurs, we must present the most learned; if they are equal, then the most pious; if equal, then the eldest; and by that fitna and disagreement are repelled. As for the detailers (al-mufaṣṣilūn), they said sometimes it (i.e., the appointment of an imam) increases fitna in times of trouble because they might kill him due to their refusal to obey him, so it is not obligatory. But in times of justice and security, his appointment is obligatory because it is more conducive to manifesting the rites of Islam. And they said: sometimes it is that in times of security and equity among people there is no need for it; it is only obligatory at times of fear and the appearance of fitna. Know that the wording of the book here and in the mention of the schools first indicates that the one who holds the detail is from the Khawarij, contrary to the apparent wording of al-Abkar and al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij argument against the obligation of appointing an imam and then refutes it. The Khawarij claim that appointing an imam causes fitna because people's desires differ and each group claims imamate for its own candidate, leading to conflict—experience confirms this. The response first notes that this argument actually supports the non-permissibility of appointment rather than non-obligation. Then the answer: if no disagreement occurs, fine; if disagreement occurs, we should choose the most learned, then the most pious, then the eldest, and by this fitna is prevented. Then the 'detailers' (al-mufaṣṣilūn) are mentioned, who say that in times of fitna, an imam's appointment increases fitna, so it is not obligatory; but in times of justice and security, it is obligatory to manifest Islam's rites. They also say that in times of security and equity, there is no need for an imam; it is only obligatory when there is fear and fitna. The author notes that the book's wording indicates that the detailer is from the Khawarij, contrary to the apparent wording of two other works.

A) By not appointing an imam
B) By choosing the most learned, then the most pious, then the eldest
C) By letting each group choose its own imam
D) By using force

Key Takeaway
By choosing the most learned, most pious, and eldest, fitna from conflicting claims to the imamate is prevented, refuting the Khawarij argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what specific area of your life are you currently avoiding a leadership role due to potential disagreement? What step can you take today to step up despite that fear?
?
The Khawarij argued that appointment causes fitna, but the solution is proper criteria, not abandonment. Where in your own life do you tend to avoid making a decision because it might upset others? How does this lesson challenge your avoidance?
?
The detailers said in times of security there is no need for an imam. Do you sometimes act as if you don't need guidance when things are peaceful? What is one area where you have become lax in seeking counsel or following a leader because you felt secure, and how can you guard against that attitude starting today?

The Condition of Qurashiyya: Evidence and Wisdom
Presentation of the argument for and against the Qurashiyya condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and consensus basis for the condition that the imam be from Quraysh, and the rationale behind it.

Core Idea
The imam must be from Quraysh—proven by hadith, consensus, and inherent leadership qualities.

This passage explains why the Ash'aris and others require the imam to be from Quraysh. It cites a direct prophetic hadith and the consensus of the Companions at Saqifa, showing that this condition is not mere tribalism but a divinely guided principle. The source also provides a brief wisdom behind this requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the groups mentioned and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with the condition and the opposing views.
2
Identify the two positions
Find which groups stipulate the condition (Ash'aris and Jubba'is) and which deny it (Khawarij and some Mu'tazilites).
3
Understand the evidence
The main evidence is the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and the fact that the Companions acted on it at Saqifa, achieving consensus.
This makes the proof decisive.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider the explanation that Quraysh possess noble lineage and leadership qualities like generosity, bravery, and awe.
This answers why the condition exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا صفات) أخرى ( في اشتراطها خلاف الأولى : أن يكون قرشياً) اشترطه الأشاعرة والجبائيان ) ومنعه الخوارج وبعض المعتزلة لنا قوله عليه السلام « الأئمة قریش ثم إن الصحابة عملوا بمضمون هذا الحديث ) فإن أبا بكر رضي الله عنه استدل به يوم السقيفة على الأنصار حين نازعوا في الإمامة بمحضر الصحابة فقبلوه وأجمعوا عليه فصار) دليلاً قاطعاً) يفيد اليقين باشتراط القرشية (احتجوا) أي من قالوا بعدم اشتراطها... قوله : أن يكون قرشياً الحكمة أنهم أشرف الناس نسباً وحسباً، وشرائط الرياسة فيهم كالكرم والشجاعة والهيبة في نفوس العرب ولم يكن في غيرهم ما كان فيهم.
And here are other attributes in whose stipulation there is disagreement: that he be Qurayshi. The Ash'aris and the two Jubba'is stipulated it, while the Khawarij and some Mu'tazilites denied it. Our evidence is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' Then the Companions acted upon the content of this hadith, for Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, used it as evidence on the day of Saqifa against the Ansar when they disputed about the imamate in the presence of the Companions, and they accepted it and agreed upon it, so it became a decisive proof yielding certainty in stipulating Qurashiyya. They (i.e., those who denied its stipulation) argued... Statement: 'That he be Qurayshi'—the wisdom is that they are the most noble of people in lineage and honor, and the qualities of leadership (such as generosity, bravery, and awe in the eyes of the Arabs) are found in them, and what was in them was not found in others.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage discusses the additional condition that the imam be from the Quraysh tribe. The Ash'aris and the two Jubba'is (al-Jubba'i and his son) stipulate it, while the Khawarij and some Mu'tazilites reject it. The evidence for the condition is the hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and the fact that the Companions acted on it: Abu Bakr used it at Saqifa to counter the Ansar's claim, and the assembled Companions accepted it, achieving consensus (ijma'). This makes the hadith a decisive proof for the condition. The author also includes a note explaining the wisdom: Quraysh are the noblest in lineage and honor, and leadership qualities like generosity, bravery, and awe-inspiring presence are inherent in them, not found in others.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the condition of being Qurayshi is based solely on tribal prejudice rather than scriptural and consensus evidence.
Correction: The condition is supported by a prophetic hadith and the consensus of the Companions, as demonstrated at Saqifa.

Checkpoint

B) The hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and the consensus of the Companions at Saqifa
C) The opinion of the Ash'aris only
D) The practice of the Khawarij

Key Takeaway
The condition that the imam be from Quraysh is established by a prophetic hadith, the consensus of the Companions at Saqifa, and the inherent leadership qualities of the tribe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' is a divine decree. Have you ever resisted accepting a leader appointed by Allah because of personal bias or tribal loyalty? What would it take for you to submit fully to Allah's choice?
?
The wisdom mentions generosity, bravery, and awe as qualities of the Quraysh. Which of these qualities is most lacking in your own character? Will you actively cultivate it today, starting with a specific action?
?
The Companions set aside their own claims at Saqifa when they heard the hadith. Is there a matter in your life where you have been clinging to your own opinion or status despite clear evidence from the Qur'an or Sunnah? What would it cost you to accept that truth?

Why the First Three Imam Conditions Are Disputed
Lists three imam conditions and an objection arguing they are impractical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three proposed conditions for the imam and the logical objection that challenges their necessity.

Core Idea
Stipulating rare qualities for the imam leads to impossible obligations.

This passage introduces the first three conditions some scholars set for the imam: sound opinion, bravery, and a strong heart. But it immediately raises a powerful objection: these qualities are rarely found together, so requiring them would either demand the impossible or render the conditions pointless. This sets the stage for a deeper debate about which imam qualities are truly necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Notice how the author presents both the conditions and the objection in one continuous argument.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the three conditions
Find the three qualities listed: رأي (opinion/insight), شجاعة (bravery), قوة قلب (strong-heartedness). The passage explains why each is needed.
These are conditions some scholars propose, not universally accepted.
3
Understand the objection's trilemma
The objection argues that since these qualities are not found together today, three possibilities arise: either it's obligatory to appoint someone lacking them (futile), or obligatory to appoint someone possessing them (impossible), or neither. In every case, stipulating them is problematic.
This logical structure is key to the debate.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If these conditions lead to such dilemmas, should they be abandoned? How might a defender of the conditions respond? This passage does not give the answer—it sets up the controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذو رأي ) وبصارة بتدبير الحرب والسلم وترتيب الجيوش وحفظ الثغور ( ليقوم بأمور الملك (شجاع قوي القلب ليقوى على الذب عن الحوزة ) والحفظ لبيضة الإسلام بالثبات في المعارك كما روي أنه عليه السلام وقف بعد انهزام المسلمين في الصف قائلا : ( أنا النبي لا كذب أنا ابن عبد المطلب». أو لا يهوله أيضاً إقامة الحدود وضرب الرقاب ( وقيل لا اشترط ) في الإمامة ( هذه الصفات ) الثلاث ( لأنها لا توجد الآن مجتمعة، وإذا لم توجد كذلك فإما أن يجب نصب فاقدها ( فيكون اشتراطها عبثاً لتحقق الإمامة بدونها (أو) يجب نصب واجدها فيكون ( تكليفاً بما لا يطاق ( أولا يجب لا هذا ولا ذاك (و) حينئذ يكون اشتراطها مستلزماً للمفاسد التي يمكن دفعها بنصب فاقدها ( فلا تكون هذه الأوصاف معتبرة فيها
(A man) of opinion and insight into managing war and peace, arranging armies, and guarding frontiers, to carry out the affairs of kingship; brave, strong-hearted to be strong in repelling from the territory and preserving the domain of Islam by steadfastness in battles, as it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) stood after the defeat of the Muslims in the ranks saying: 'I am the Prophet, no lie, I am the son of Abd al-Muttalib.' And also, the execution of legal penalties and striking of necks does not terrify him. And it was said: These three qualities are not stipulated for the imamate, because they are not found together nowadays, and if they are not found thus, then either it is obligatory to appoint one lacking them (so stipulating them would be futile since the imamate is realized without them), or it is obligatory to appoint one who possesses them (which would be an obligation beyond capacity), or neither this nor that is obligatory, and then stipulating them would entail evils that could be repelled by appointing one lacking them, so these attributes are not to be considered in it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the first three conditions proposed for the imam (being a man of opinion, brave, strong-hearted) and the objection that these conditions are not necessary. The objector argues that since such individuals are rarely found, requiring them leads to impossible obligations or futile stipulations. The author reproduces the objection without explicitly endorsing it, setting up the later response that these conditions are not binding but the community may appoint someone lacking them to avoid greater evil.

شجاعة
قوة قلب
اشتراط
نصب الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three conditions are universally accepted by all scholars.
Correction: The passage shows a strong objection that these conditions are not binding because they are rarely found together.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection against stipulating the first three conditions for the imam?
A) They are impossible to fulfill.
B) They are not found together nowadays, leading to logical problems.
C) The Prophet did not possess them.
D) The imamate can exist without them by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The first three conditions for imam are disputed because they are rarely found together, making their stipulation either futile or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate the leaders in your community, do you impose conditions that you yourself fail to meet? What would it cost you to lower your expectations and still support them?
?
The Prophet stood alone after the Muslims fled, declaring his identity. In what area of your life are you tempted to retreat from your principles? What would it mean for you to stand firm and say 'I am a believer, no lie'?
?
The objection warns that demanding perfection leads to impossible burdens. Are there standards you place on yourself or others that God never required? How can you distinguish between a genuine duty and a self-imposed ideal that excuses inaction?
